Truth vs. Authority: Al-Juwayni's Critique of al-Baqillani
Author illustrates how jadal exposes weaknesses in famous scholars' opinions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct al-Juwayni's critique of al-Baqillani's definition of prayer and identify the logical error in relying on authority over evidence.

Core Idea
Truth is not determined by a scholar's fame, but by the strength of evidence.

This passage from a work on usul al-fiqh illustrates a fundamental principle of Islamic dialectical method: the truth of an opinion is judged by its evidence, not by the reputation of its proponent. Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni, a leading Ash'ari scholar, applies this principle to critique the earlier authority Abu Bakr al-Baqillani, showing that even great scholars can err when their definitions conflict with established consensus. By examining this example, you will learn how to use evidence-based critique to evaluate scholarly opinions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the methodological principle
Find the author's main claim about how truth is established. Look for the phrase 'الحقيقة العلمية لا تؤخذ من رأي لمجرد أن قائله إنسان مشهور' (scientific truth is not taken from an opinion merely because its speaker is a famous person).
This principle is the basis of the entire critique.
2
Extract al-Baqillani's definition of prayer
Al-Baqillani claimed that prayer (salāh) is essentially supplication (du'ā'), and that the law does not forbid calling mere supplication prayer. Identify the core of his definition.
His definition reduces prayer to a single element.
3
Analyze al-Juwayni's refutation
What evidence does al-Juwayni present to reject al-Baqillani's definition? Note the reference to the consensus (ijmā') of the bearers of the law that bowing and prostration are part of prayer.
Consensus (ijmā') is a key source of evidence in usul al-fiqh.
4
Assess the logical structure of the critique
Al-Juwayni argues that since prayer includes bowing and prostration by consensus, defining it solely as supplication is insufficient. Is this argument valid? Consider that the critique uses consensus to show incompleteness.
The critique does not reject supplication as part of prayer; it rejects the exclusion of actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لكن الحقيقة العلمية لا تؤخذ من رأي لمجرد أن قائله إنسان مشهور، بل لأن هذا الرأي يحمل قوته في ذاته ويستمد وجاهته من أدلته وبراهينه. وهذا ما وجدنا علماء أصول الفقه حريصين عليه إذ أن المنهج الجدلي الذي سلكوه في دراسة المسائل الأصولية مكنهم من اكتشاف جوانب الضعف في أقوال بعض العلماء المشهورين. من ذلك قول الجويني في حق القاضي أبي بكر الباقلاني، وهو من هو بين أئمة الأشعرية، الذين ينتمي إليهم الجويني: أما القاضي رحمة الله عليه فإنه استمر في لجاج ظاهر، فقال: الصلاة؛ الدعاء، والمسمى بها في الشرع دعاء عند وقوع أقوال وأفعال، ثم الشرع لا يزجر عن تسمية الدعاء المحض صلاة. وطَرَدَ ذلك في الألفاظ التي فيها الكلام. وهذا غير سديد، فإن حملة الشريعة مجمعون على أن الركوع والسجود من الصلاة، ومساق ما ذكره أن المسمى بالصلاة الدعاء فحسب، وليس الأمر كذلك.
But scientific truth is not taken from an opinion merely because its speaker is a famous person, but because this opinion carries its strength in itself and derives its validity from its evidences and proofs. This is what we found the scholars of usul al-fiqh keen on, for the dialectical method they followed in studying usuli issues enabled them to discover weaknesses in the statements of some famous scholars. Among that is the statement of al-Juwayni regarding the judge Abu Bakr al-Baqillani, who is among the imams of the Ash‘arites to whom al-Juwayni belongs: 'As for the judge, may God have mercy on him, he persisted in apparent obstinacy, saying: prayer is supplication, and what is called prayer in the law is supplication upon the occurrence of statements and actions, and then the law does not forbid calling mere supplication prayer. And he extended that to expressions that involve speech. This is unsound, for the bearers of the law are unanimous that bowing and prostration are part of prayer, and the thrust of what he mentioned is that what is called prayer is supplication only, and that is not the case.'
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage asserts a key methodological principle: the truth of an opinion is not guaranteed by the fame or authority of its proponent, but by the internal strength of its evidence and proofs. The author then illustrates this principle with an example from usul al-fiqh: Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni, a leading Ash‘ari scholar, criticizes a statement of the earlier Ash‘ari authority al-Baqillani. Al-Baqillani had defined 'prayer' (salāh) as essentially 'supplication' (du‘ā'), arguing that the law does not forbid calling mere supplication a prayer. Al-Juwayni refutes this by noting that the entire scholarly community (the bearers of the law) agrees that bowing (rukū‘) and prostration (sujūd) are integral parts of prayer. Therefore, al-Baqillani's definition is unsound. The example demonstrates how dialectical critique (jadal) can identify logical flaws even in the opinions of esteemed figures, simply by testing those opinions against evidence and consensus.

Key Terms
الحقيقة العلمية
المنهج الجدلي
الرأي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does al-Juwayni reject al-Baqillani's definition of prayer?
A) Because al-Baqillani was not a famous scholar.
B) Because the community of scholars unanimously includes bowing and prostration in prayer.
C) Because supplication is not part of prayer at all.
D) Because al-Juwayni disliked al-Baqillani personally.

Key Takeaway
In Islamic dialectic, the truth of an opinion is established by evidence, not by the fame of its proponent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify another instance in usul al-fiqh where a famous scholar's opinion was later rejected due to lack of evidence. (Hint: al-Shāfi‘ī's initial versus final school.) Briefly explain why it was rejected.
?
Using al-Juwayni's method, propose a critique of a hypothetical definition: 'Interest (ribā) is any excess in an exchange.' What evidence might contradict this definition?

From Dialectic to Usuli Rule: Al-Juwayni's Method
Author explains how jadal helps formulate general usuli rules, with al-Juwayni's example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how dialectical research (jadal) in usul al-fiqh leads to the formulation of general usuli rules, using al-Juwayni's rule on command as a case study.

Core Idea
Jadal transforms disputed cases into enduring legal maxims.

In this passage, the author shows that jadal in usul al-fiqh is not just about winning debates—it is a productive method for deriving general principles. By examining al-Juwayni's treatment of the command form (ṣīghat al-amr), we see how a thorough dialectical investigation crystallizes into a codified rule that later becomes a foundation for a legal school. This lesson walks through that process.

Guided Path
1
Identify the function of jadal according to the author
The author states that jadal enables scholars to scrutinize disputed issues and establish general rules. Locate this claim in the Arabic: 'ساهم البحث الجدلي في أصول الفقه... في تمكين الأصوليين من تمحيص المسائل المختلف فيها، والتوصل في شأنها إلى تقرير قواعد أصولية'.
2
Trace al-Juwayni's dialectical process
Al-Juwayni studied the 'Issue of the command form' (مسألة في صيغة الأمر), reviewed schools and evidences, then chose an opinion. The passage summarizes this process before stating the resulting rule.
3
Extract the rule al-Juwayni formulated
The rule is: 'التمحيض في الطلب متوعد على تركه وكل ما كان كذلك لا يكون إلا واجبا' – 'The specification in command is accompanied by a threat for its abandonment, and everything that is such is nothing but obligatory.' Note that this rule emerged from dialectical investigation.
4
Assess the rule's impact
The author says such rules become 'قوانين' (laws) that fiqh schools rely on. Consider how this rule would guide interpretation of commands in legal texts: commands with threats become obligatory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
4 - التوصل إلى تقرير القواعد الأصولية : لقد ساهم البحث الجدلي في أصول الفقه، أيما مساهمة، في تمكين الأصوليين من تمحيص المسائل المختلف فيها، والتوصل في شأنها إلى تقرير قواعد أصولية أصبحت بعد ذلك قوانين تستند إليها المذاهب الفقهية لأصحابها. ذلك أن الجويني بعد دراسة مستفيضة لـ: «مسألة في صيغة الأمر»، استعرض فيها مذاهب العلماء وأدلتهم، وتوصل فيها إلى رأي اختاره ورأى صوابه، ختم حديثه بصياغة رأيه في شكل قاعدة قننها واعتبرها خلاصة المسألة. من فقال : التمحيض في الطلب متوعد على تركه وكل ما كان كذلك لا يكون إلا واجبا.
4 - Arriving at the establishment of usuli rules: Dialectical research in usul al-fiqh contributed greatly to enabling the uṣūliyyūn to scrutinize disputed issues and to reach, regarding them, the establishment of usuli rules that later became laws upon which the fiqh schools of their founders rely. That is because al-Juwayni, after an extensive study of the 'Issue of the command form' (mas’alat fī ṣīghat al-amr), in which he reviewed the schools of the scholars and their evidences, and reached an opinion he chose and saw as correct, concluded his discussion by formulating his opinion in the form of a rule that he codified and considered the essence of the issue. He said: 'The specification (al-tamḥīḍ) in command is accompanied by a threat for its abandonment, and everything that is such is nothing but obligatory.'
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage highlights another function of jadal in usul al-fiqh: it helps scholars establish general rules that become foundational for legal schools. The author uses al-Juwayni as an example. After a thorough dialectical examination of a disputed issue—specifically, the form and implications of a command (ṣīghat al-amr)—al-Juwayni formulated a rule: 'The specification of a command is accompanied by a threat for leaving it, and whatever is like that is obligatory (wājib).' This rule serves as a principle to interpret commands in legal texts. The passage shows how dialectical reasoning moves from particular cases to general legal maxims (qawā‘id uṣūliyyah), which then guide the derivation of rulings in fiqh.

Key Terms
القواعد الأصولية
صيغة الأمر
التمحيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) A new fiqh ruling.
B) A general usuli rule that commands with threats are obligatory.
C) That all commands are recommendations.
D) That dialectical research is useless.

Key Takeaway
Dialectical research in usul al-fiqh yields general rules that become foundational maxims for legal schools.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify a disputed issue in usul al-fiqh (e.g., whether a command indicates obligation or recommendation) and outline how a dialectical investigation could lead to a general rule, similar to al-Juwayni's method.
?
Examine a ruling from a fiqh text and trace it back to a possible usuli rule that might have been established through jadal, as shown in this lesson.

Jadal Unveils Root Causes of Disagreement
Author shows how jadal uncovers the underlying reasons for scholarly disagreement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the root cause of a scholarly disagreement by analyzing the differing perspectives of the parties using jadal methodology.

Core Idea
Jadal clarifies disagreement by revealing each scholar's focal lens.

In this lesson, we explore how jadal not only debates positions but also uncovers the deeper reasons why scholars differ. Using the famous case of prayer in a usurped property, al-Ṭūfī demonstrates that the conflict between the negator and affirmant stems from each focusing on a different aspect of the same act. By the end, you will be able to apply this analytical lens to any disputed issue in the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two opposing positions
Locate the two parties: al-nāfī (the negator) who rules the prayer invalid, and al-muthbit (the affirmant) who rules it valid. Understand their surface-level claims.
2
Extract each party's focal lens
Notice how al-Ṭūfī describes each party's viewpoint: the negator looks at the specific essence ('ayn) of the prayer, composed of forbidden acts; the affirmant looks at the genus (jins) of prayer as a legally required act. Identify the difference in focus.
3
Identify the root cause of disagreement
The disagreement arises because the two scholars prioritize different aspects of the same action. The negator considers the concrete forbidden elements; the affirmant considers the abstract legal nature. This difference in analytical priority is the root cause.
This shows that both positions are legitimate but incomplete if considered alone.
4
Generalize the jadal insight
Apply the same method to other scholarly disagreements: look for differences in perspective, such as looking at the letter vs. the spirit of the law, or the specific vs. the general. Jadal helps reveal these underlying causes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
5 - بيان أسباب الاختلاف : إن الجدل الأصولي، بما أنه محاولة للتوصل إلى الحق في المسائل المختلف فيها، قد دعا الأصوليين الذين مارسوه إلى محاولة الكشف عن الأسباب الكامنة وراء وقوع الاختلاف في المسائل الأصولية التي دار حولها الجدل، وقد كان ذلك طريقا إلى الكشف عن أسباب الاختلاف في الكثير من المسائل الأصولية . ذلك؛ ما علق به الطوفي على الاختلاف الواقع حول مسألة الصلاة في الدار المغصوبة : وقد ظهر لك من تقرير حجة النافي ،والمثبت صحة المأخذ الذي ذكرناه أن النافي نظر إلى عين هذه الصلاة، وأنها مركبة من أفعال منهي عنها، فحكم ببطلانها، والمثبت نظر إلى جنس الصلاة وحقيقتها من حيث مطلوبة للشرع، لا جهة وقوعها في موضع مغصوب، فتحقق له الجهتان.
5 - Explanation of the causes of disagreement: The usuli dialectic, since it is an attempt to arrive at the truth in disputed issues, called the uṣūliyyūn who practiced it to attempt to uncover the underlying reasons behind the occurrence of disagreement in the usuli issues around which debate occurred, and that was a way to uncover the causes of disagreement in many usuli issues. That is what al-Ṭūfī commented on regarding the disagreement that occurred about the issue of prayer in a usurped property: 'It has become clear to you from the presentation of the argument of the negator and the affirmant of the validity of the approach we mentioned that the negator looked at the specific essence of this prayer, and that it is composed of forbidden actions, so he ruled it invalid; and the affirmant looked at the genus of prayer and its reality as something required by the law, not considering the aspect of its occurrence in a usurped place, so the two aspects were realized for him.'
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage explains how jadal helps identify why scholars disagree. The author cites an example from al-Ṭūfī concerning the validity of prayer performed in a usurped property (al-ṣalāh fī al-dār al-maghṣūbah). Some scholars (the 'negators') rule the prayer invalid, while others (the 'affirmants') consider it valid. Through dialectical analysis, al-Ṭūfī shows that the disagreement stems from a difference in perspective: the negator focuses on the specific act of prayer, which includes forbidden elements (actions in a usurped place), and thus invalidates it. The affirmant focuses on the genus of prayer, which as a legal requirement remains valid regardless of the location's legal status. Thus, both parties have valid considerations, but they prioritize different aspects. This demonstrates how jadal clarifies the root causes of disagreement.

Key Terms
أسباب الاختلاف
النافي
المثبت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because he considered the genus of prayer.
B) Because he considered the specific essence of the prayer composed of forbidden acts.
C) Because he followed al-Juwayni's rule on commands.
D) Because he rejected the use of jadal.

Key Takeaway
Jadal reveals that disagreements often arise from scholars prioritizing different aspects of a single issue, rather than from mere error or stubbornness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider another disputed fiqh issue: the validity of a conditional sale (bay' al-istisnā'). Identify two opposing positions and analyze the underlying lens each position uses (e.g., one focuses on the absence of a specific object, the other on the necessity of transactions).
?
Take the debate on whether the basmalah is part of Sūrat al-Fātiḥa. Using the same method, determine the root cause of disagreement between those who affirm and those who negate its inclusion.

How Jadal Refines Points of Contention
Author describes how jadal helps narrow disagreement and reconcile views, citing al-Ṭūfī on the intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify how tahrīr maḥall al-nizā‘ operates in an uṣūlī debate and apply it to a new issue to distinguish substantive from verbal disagreement.

Core Idea
Jadal’s power lies in precisely defining the disagreement—often revealing none exists.

In the previous lesson, we saw how jadal explains the causes of disagreement among scholars. Now we explore another vital benefit: tahrīr maḥall al-nizā‘, or refining the point of contention. By pinpointing exactly what is being argued about, jadal can shrink the scope of disagreement or even show that scholars are actually in agreement. This lesson walks through the concept and an illustrative example from al-Ṭūfī concerning the intellect.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the concept of tahrīr maḥall al-nizā‘
The passage states that jadal pushes scholars to ‘refine the point of contention’ (taḥrīr maḥall al-nizā‘). This means precisely defining the issue under debate so that the actual locus of disagreement is clear. It aims to bring viewpoints closer and narrow disagreement.
2
Analyze al-Ṭūfī’s example on the intellect
Al-Ṭūfī discusses two questions: where is the intellect located (in the head), and does it vary in quantity, quality, perfection, and deficiency? He reports two scholarly opinions: some say it varies, others say it does not. His companions (Hanbalis) argue for variation based on observable differences in intellectual effects.
Notice that the passage does not resolve the debate; it uses it to show how tahrīr clarifies what is being disputed.
3
Understand how tahrīr reduces disagreement
By defining terms like ‘intellect’ and specifying whether the disagreement is about location, variation, or something else, scholars may discover that they are arguing about different things or that their positions are compatible. The passage notes that tahrīr sometimes reveals that the circle of disagreement is narrower than thought, or even nonexistent.
This is the key outcome of tahrīr: converting apparent disputes into either genuine but limited ones, or mere verbal misunderstandings.
4
Apply the tool to another usuli issue
Take a classic usul debate: whether qiyas (analogical reasoning) is a source of law. Apply tahrīr by asking: What exactly is meant by ‘source’? Is the disagreement about its authority, its methodology, or its scope? By refining the point of contention, one might find that the opposing views are actually complementary or address different aspects.
Practicing tahrīr on familiar issues strengthens your ability to use jadal productively.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
6 - تحرير موضع النزاع والجمع بين الآراء ما أمكن : وكما ساهم الجدل الأصولي في بيان أسباب الاختلاف، فقد دفع بالأصوليين الذين سلكوا المنهج الجدلى إلى محاولة تحرير محل النزاع في المسائل الأصولية التي دار الجدل حولها، وذلك بغية تقريب وجهات النظر والتضييق من دائرة الخلاف، وذلك ما كان سببًا في الكشف عن ضيق دائرة الاختلاف في بعض الأحيان وقد كان يُعتقد أنها واسعة، وكذلك انتفاء الخلاف أصلا في بعض الأحيان وقد كان يظن أنه موجود. من ذلك ما أورده الطوفي في تعريف العقل وتحديد موضعه، واختلافه بالقلة والكثرة، والكمال والنقص ، قال رحمه الله : والمختار؛ أنه – أي العقل - في الرأس، وهل يختلف بالقلة والكثرة، والكمال والنقص؟ فيه قولان للعلماء : قال أصحابنا : نعم، لأن كمال الشيء ونقصه يعرف بكمال آثاره وأفعاله ونقصها، ونحن نشاهد قطعا تفاوت آثار العقول في الآراء والحكم والحيل
6 - Refining the point of contention and reconciling opinions as much as possible: Just as usuli dialectic contributed to explaining the causes of disagreement, it also urged the uṣūliyyūn who followed the dialectical method to attempt to refine the point of contention in the usuli issues around which debate occurred, with the aim of bringing viewpoints closer and narrowing the circle of disagreement. This caused, at times, the revelation that the circle of disagreement was narrower than it had been thought to be, and likewise, at times, the absence of disagreement altogether where it had been thought to exist. Among that is what al-Ṭūfī mentioned regarding the definition of the intellect (al-‘aql) and determining its location, and its variation in quantity and quality, and perfection and deficiency. He, may God have mercy on him, said: 'The chosen opinion is that it—i.e., the intellect—is in the head. And does it vary in quantity and quality, and perfection and deficiency? There are two opinions among the scholars: Our (Hanbali?) companions said: Yes, because the perfection and deficiency of a thing are known by the perfection and deficiency of its effects and actions, and we definitely observe the variation of the effects of intellects in opinions, judgments, and stratagems.'
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage discusses another benefit of dialectical method: tahrīr mahall al-nizā‘ (refining the point of contention). By carefully defining the locus of dispute, jadal can reduce the scope of disagreement or even show that no real disagreement exists. The author provides an example from al-Ṭūfī concerning the nature of the intellect (al-‘aql). Some scholars argued about whether the intellect varies in quantity and quality, and perfection and deficiency. Al-Ṭūfī notes that the chosen opinion is that the intellect is located in the head. Then he raises the question of variation: there are two opinions. The early companions (likely Hanbali) said yes, because the perfection of a thing is known by its effects; we see that people's intellects differ in judgments and strategies. This example illustrates how jadal forces scholars to precisely define what is being argued about, which can clarify whether the disagreement is substantive or merely verbal. The passage ends mid-quote, but the point is clear.

Key Terms
تحرير محل النزاع
العقل
الجمع بين الآراء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does tahrīr maḥall al-nizā‘ achieve according to the passage?
A) It eliminates all disagreement.
B) It may reveal that the circle of disagreement is narrower than thought or even non-existent.
C) It proves the intellect is located in the heart.
D) It shows that jadal is unnecessary.

Key Takeaway
Tahrīr maḥall al-nizā‘ is a powerful dialectical tool that prevents wasted debate by pinpointing the exact locus of disagreement, often revealing that scholars are closer than they think.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the debate on whether ijma‘ (consensus) is a definitive proof (qat‘i). Apply tahrīr maḥall al-nizā‘: what exactly is meant by ‘definitive’? Is the disagreement about the epistemological certainty of consensus, its scope, or its conditions? Write a brief analysis showing how refining the point of contention clarifies the actual area of disagreement.
?
Take another usuli issue, such as the admissibility of istihsan (juristic preference). Use tahrīr to determine if the disagreement between those who accept and reject istihsan is substantive or merely a matter of definition.

Refining the Point of Dispute: The Intellect Debate
Author shows dialectic's role in refining disputes and reconciling views, with intellect example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the dialectical method of taḥrīr maḥall al-nizāʿ to reconcile seemingly contradictory positions on a debated issue in usul al-fiqh.

Core Idea
Refining the dispute turns broad disagreements into narrow, resolvable differences.

In this passage, the author shows how usuli scholars used dialectic to pinpoint exactly what was being disagreed about. The case study is the nature of the intellect—does it vary among humans? Al-Tufi presents two camps and then reconciles them by distinguishing two types of intellect. This is a classic example of taḥrīr maḥall al-nizāʿ, and you will learn to apply this technique to other debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two opposing positions
One group (apparently Hanbalis) claims that intellect varies in quantity and quality. The other group (Mu'tazila and Ash'aris) claims it does not vary. Write down each position.
2
Examine the arguments for each side
The first group argues from observable variation in mental effects and common speech. The second group argues that intellect must be a universal proof, otherwise sophistry would result. Note the strength of both arguments.
3
Recognize that both arguments seem strong
Al-Tufi says 'the proof is strong from both sides,' implying the dispute might not be about the same thing. This is the key insight for refining the dispute.
4
See how al-Tufi reconciles by distinction
He distinguishes innate intellect (uniform) from acquired empirical intellect (variable). This shows that the two sides were not truly contradicting each other once the subject is refined.
This is the technique of taḥrīr maḥall al-nizāʿ: identifying what exactly is being contested.

6 - تحرير موضع النزاع والجمع بين الآراء ما أمكن : وكما ساهم الجدل الأصولي في بيان أسباب الاختلاف، فقد دفع بالأصوليين الذين سلكوا المنهج الجدلى إلى محاولة تحرير محل النزاع في المسائل الأصولية التي دار الجدل حولها، وذلك بغية تقريب وجهات النظر والتضييق من دائرة الخلاف، وذلك ما كان سببًا في الكشف عن ضيق دائرة الاختلاف في بعض الأحيان وقد كان يُعتقد أنها واسعة، وكذلك انتفاء الخلاف أصلا في بعض الأحيان وقد كان يظن أنه من ذلك ؛ ذلك ما أورده الطوفي في تعريف العقل وتحديد موضعه، واختلافه بالقلة والكثرة، والكمال والنقص ، قال رحمه الله : . والمختار؛ أنه - أي العقل - في الرأس، وهل يختلف بالقلة والكثرة، والكمال والنقص؟ فيه قولان للعلماء : قال أصحابنا : نعم، لأن كمال الشيء ونقصه يعرف بكمال آثاره وأفعاله ونقصها، ونحن نشاهد قطعا تفاوت آثار العقول في الآراء والحكم والحيل ونحوها، وذلك يدل على تفاوت العقول نفسها وأجمع العقلاء على صحة قول القائل : فلان أقل عقلا من فلان أو أكمل عقلا وذلك يدل على ما ذكرناه . وحكوا عن المعتزلة والأشعرية خلاف ذلك، لأن العقل حجة عامة يرجع إليها الناس عند اختلافهم، ولو تفاوتت العقول، لما كان كذلك، وكان يلزم من ذلك السفسطة، إذ كان يتسع لبعض الناس إذا ألزم أن الواحد نصف الإثنين، أو استحالة اجتماع الضدين أن يقول : أنتم تدركون ذلك وأنا لا أدركه ولما رأينا الناس عند الانتهاء إلى هذه القضايا، علمنا استواء العقول. واعلم أن الحجة قوية من الطرفين وهذا يقتضي أن النزاع ليس مورده واحدا، ووجه الجمع بين القولين ؛ أن العقل على ضربين : طبعي؛ وهو الذي لا يتفاوت في العقلاء، وكسبي تجريبي؛ وهو الذي يتفاوتون فيه
6 - Refining the point of dispute and reconciling views as much as possible: And just as usuli dialectic contributed to clarifying the causes of disagreement, it also impelled the usulis who followed the dialectical method to attempt to refine the point of dispute in the usuli issues around which dialectic revolved, in order to bring viewpoints closer and narrow the circle of disagreement. This was a reason for revealing the narrowness of the circle of disagreement sometimes when it was believed to be wide, and likewise the absence of disagreement at all sometimes when it was thought to exist. That is what al-Tufi presented in defining the intellect and determining its place, and its variation in paucity and abundance, perfection and deficiency. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: The chosen opinion is that it—i.e., the intellect—is in the head. Does it vary in paucity and abundance, perfection and deficiency? There are two opinions among scholars: Our companions said: Yes, because the perfection and deficiency of a thing are known by the perfection and deficiency of its effects and actions, and we observe definitively the variation in the effects of intellects in opinions, judgments, tricks, and the like, and that indicates the variation in the intellects themselves. And the rational people have agreed on the validity of the saying: So-and-so has less intellect than so-and-so or more complete intellect, and that indicates what we mentioned. And they narrated from the Mu'tazila and Ash'aris the opposite of that, because the intellect is a universal proof to which people refer when they differ; if intellects varied, it would not be so, and that would necessitate sophistry, since it would permit some people, when it is required that one is half of two, or the impossibility of the coexistence of opposites, to say: You perceive that and I do not perceive it. And since we see people when ending up at these propositions, we know the equality of intellects. And know that the proof is strong from both sides, and this requires that the point of contention is not one. And the way to reconcile the two opinions is that the intellect is of two types: innate, which does not vary among rational people; and acquired empirical, in which they vary.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage discusses how usuli dialectic helps refine the exact point of dispute and reconcile apparently conflicting views. The author states that just as dialectic reveals causes of disagreement, it also pushes usuli scholars to refine the locus of dispute (mahall al-niza') in debated issues, aiming to bring perspectives closer and narrow the circle of disagreement. This sometimes reveals that the circle of disagreement is narrower than assumed, or that there is no real disagreement at all. As an example, the author cites al-Tufi's discussion on the definition of the intellect (al-'aql), its location (in the head), and whether it varies in quantity and quality. Al-Tufi presents two opinions: one group (probably Hanbalis or those following his school) holds that intellect varies because its effects vary, as seen in differences in judgments and strategies; they also rely on common usage (saying someone has more or less intellect). The other group (Mu'tazila and Ash'aris) argues that intellect is a universal proof that does not vary, otherwise sophistry would follow—people could reject basic logical truths like 'one is half of two' by claiming different intellects. Al-Tufi notes that both arguments are strong, implying that the locus of disagreement is not one and the same. The reconciliation is that intellect has two types: innate (tabi'i), which is uniform across humans, and acquired empirical (kasbi tajribi), which varies. This shows how dialectic can resolve apparent contradictions by distinguishing aspects of the subject.

Key Terms
عقل طبعي
عقل كسبي تجريبي
تحرير محل النزاع

D) By asserting that intellect does not vary at all.

Key Takeaway
Dialectic refines disputes by distinguishing aspects of the subject, turning apparent contradictions into complementary truths.

How Dialectic Refines Proofs: The Case of the Undetermined Obligation
Author describes how dialectic refines arguments, with example of criticising proof for the undetermined obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify how dialectical critique exposes weaknesses in a legal proof, using the example of al-Tufi's objection to the majority's argument from legal occurrence.

Core Idea
Dialectic exposes hidden assumptions by turning the proof itself into the point of dispute.

This passage reveals a key function of usuli dialectic: it subjects proofs to scrutiny, revealing flaws that the lone researcher might miss. The example of al-Tufi's critique shows that even a widely accepted argument can be question-begging when the very occurrence of the claimed ruling is disputed. By the end of this lesson, you will understand how dialectical engagement forces proponents to refine their evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the researcher's solitary tendency
Notice that the author begins by describing how a researcher working alone may become convinced of a view and its proofs, but lacks the corrective check of dialectic.
2
Understand the value of dialectic
Dialectic brings other scholars who can spot contradictions or weaknesses, compelling the researcher to revise.
3
Examine the majority's proof
The majority (jumhur) argued for the undetermined obligation using two proofs: rational permissibility and legal occurrence. The second is under attack.
4
Locate al-Tufi's critique
Al-Tufi, though himself from the majority, points out that invoking legal occurrence is problematic because the opponent's dispute is precisely about whether such occurrence exists.
This is a form of begging the question.
5
Recognize the refinement
The critique forces the majority to either prove occurrence independently or abandon that line of argument, thus refining the evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
7 - تنقيح الأدلة المستدل بها تنقيحا علميا : إن الباحث الذي يكتفي بدراسة المسائل لوحده ربما انتصر لرأي معين وساق له أدلة يتصور أنها مقنعة لكن حين يظهر بها إلى غيره من الباحثين ربما كشفوا له عما فيها من تناقض أو تهافت فيراجع أدلته وربما راجع رأيه أصلا . وقد كان للجدل الأصولي هذه الميزة العلمية والمنهجية، إذ ظهر أن في بعض الأدلة المستخدمة في الدفاع عن آراء أصولية بعض الخلل الذي يستدعي تقويمها . من ذلك؛ أن الجمهور في قولهم في الواجب المخير أن الواجب فيه واحد لا يقبل التعيين استدلوا بالجواز العقلي والوقوع الشرعي والطوفي، رغم أنه من الجمهور، إلا أنه انتقد استدلالهم بالوقوع، قائلا : وفي الاستدلال على الجواز بالوقوع نظر، لأنه إن كان في المسألة منازع، كان دعوى الوقوع محل النزاع، بل هو يقول : الواجب في خصال الكفارة الجميع، لأنه فرد من أفراد محل النزاع
7 - Refining evidences used for inference in a scientific manner: Indeed, the researcher who is content with studying issues alone may champion a certain opinion and adduce proofs that he imagines are convincing, but when he presents them to other researchers, they may reveal to him what is in them of contradiction or weakness, so he revises his proofs and perhaps even revises his opinion altogether. And usuli dialectic had this scientific and methodological advantage, as it appeared that in some proofs used in defending usuli opinions there was some defect that necessitated their correction. Among that is that the majority (al-jumhur) in their saying regarding the undetermined obligation (al-wajib al-mukhayyar) that the obligatory act is one and does not accept specification, they argued by rational permissibility and legal occurrence (al-wuqu' al-shar'i). And al-Tufi, although he is from the majority, criticized their inference from occurrence, saying: And regarding the inference of permissibility by occurrence, there is consideration, because if there is a disputant in the issue, the claim of occurrence is precisely the point of dispute; rather, he says: The obligatory act in the expiation characteristics is all of them, because it is an individual of the individuals of the point of dispute.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage discusses how usuli dialectic refines proofs scientifically. The author explains that a researcher working alone may adopt a view and present proofs that seem convincing to him, but when he presents them to other researchers in dialectical engagement, they may reveal contradictions or weaknesses, prompting him to revise his proofs or even his opinion. This is a key scientific benefit of dialectic. He then gives an example: the majority of scholars (al-jumhur) in the issue of the 'undetermined obligation' (al-wajib al-mukhayyar—where one must choose among options, like in expiation) hold that the obligatory act is one and that it does not accept specification (takhsis). They support this by arguing from rational permissibility (al-jawaz al-'aqli) and legal occurrence (al-wuqu' al-shar'i), i.e., that such obligations have occurred in the Shari'a. However, al-Tufi, though himself from the majority, critiques their use of the argument from occurrence. He says that invoking occurrence as proof is problematic, because if there is a disputant in the issue, the claim of occurrence is precisely the point of dispute—the opponent denies that such obligations occur in the specified way. In fact, al-Tufi himself holds that in expiation characteristics, the required act is all of them (i.e., all options are individually obligatory), because it is an instance of the disputed point. This shows how dialectical critique can refine and correct proofs.

Key Terms
واجب مخير
وقوع شرعي

A) Because he denied that such obligations exist.
B) Because the claim of occurrence is precisely what is disputed.
C) Because he believed rational permissibility is stronger.
D) Because he thought the majority were wrong.

Key Takeaway
Dialectic refines proofs by exposing when an argument assumes what it needs to prove, as al-Tufi did regarding legal occurrence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the debate on whether qiyas (analogy) can be applied in hudud punishments. A Hanafi scholar argues that the Prophet prohibited analogy in hudud. A Shafi'i scholar counters that this prohibition is only for certain cases. Identify the point of dispute and explain why a simple appeal to textual occurrence (e.g., citing a hadith) would not settle the debate without first establishing that the hadith covers the contested case.

How Dialectic Refines Evidence: The Case of Wajib Mukhayyar
Author explains how jadal refines evidence by exposing flaws, with example from usul al-fiqh on wajib mukhayyar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the flaw in an argument based on 'occurrence' (wuqu') in usul al-fiqh and explain how dialectical critique exposes circular reasoning.

Core Idea
Dialectic exposes hidden assumptions—like using a disputed premise as evidence.

When you think you have the perfect proof, others may spot a flaw you missed. This is the power of jadal: it forces you to refine your evidence. In this lesson, we see how al-Tufi, from the majority himself, critiques the majority's use of 'occurrence' in the debate over the wajib mukhayyar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the majority's claim
The majority (jumhur) claim that in the indeterminate obligatory act (wajib mukhayyar), the obligation is one unspecified act that the obligee must choose.
2
Identify their evidence
They support this claim with two arguments: rational permissibility (al-jawaz al-'aqli) and religious occurrence (al-wuqu' al-shar'i). The latter means that such an obligation actually occurs in shari'a.
3
Understand al-Tufi's objection
Al-Tufi criticizes the inference from occurrence: if there is a disputant (i.e., someone who denies the majority's view), then the claim that this obligation 'occurs' is exactly what is disputed. Using it as evidence is circular—it begs the question.
The objection is methodological: in jadal, you cannot assume the very point at issue.
4
Apply the lesson to your own analysis
When evaluating any argument in usul al-fiqh, check whether any premise is itself the subject of disagreement. If so, that premise cannot serve as independent proof without further justification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
7 - تنقيح الأدلة المستدل بها تنقيحا علميا : إن الباحث الذي يكتفي بدراسة المسائل لوحده ربما انتصر لرأي معين وساق له أدلة يتصور أنها مقنعة لكن حين يظهر بها إلى غيره من الباحثين ربما كشفوا له عما فيها من تناقض أو تهافت فيراجع أدلته وربما راجع رأيه أصلا . وقد كان للجدل الأصولي هذه الميزة العلمية والمنهجية، إذ ظهر أن في بعض الأدلة المستخدمة في الدفاع عن آراء أصولية بعض الخلل الذي يستدعي تقويمها . من ذلك؛ أن الجمهور في قولهم في الواجب المخير أن الواجب فيه واحد لا يقبل التعيين استدلوا بالجواز العقلي والوقوع الشرعي والطوفي، رغم أنه من الجمهور، إلا أنه انتقد استدلالهم بالوقوع، قائلا : وفي الاستدلال على الجواز بالوقوع نظر، لأنه إن كان في المسألة منازع، كان دعوى الوقوع محل النزاع، بل هو يقول : الواجب في خصال الكفارة الجميع، لأنه فرد من أفراد محل النزاع (2).
7 - Scientific refinement of evidences used as evidence: Indeed, the researcher who suffices with studying issues alone may support a particular opinion and bring forth evidence he imagines convincing, but when he presents it to other researchers, they may reveal to him contradictions or incoherence in it, so he reviews his evidence and perhaps reviews his opinion entirely. And this was the scientific and methodological characteristic of usuli dialectic, as it appeared that in some evidences used in defending usuli opinions there is some defect that requires correction. Among that: The majority (jumhur) in their statement regarding the indeterminate obligatory act (al-wajib al-mukhayyar) – that the obligation is one unspecified – supported it by rational permissibility and religious occurrence (al-wuqu' al-shar'i). And al-Tufi, despite being from the majority, criticized their inference from occurrence, saying: 'In inferring permissibility from occurrence there is a problem, because if there is a disputant in the issue, the claim of occurrence is the point of dispute. Rather, he says: The obligation in the characteristics of expiation is all of them, because it is an instance of the subject of dispute.'
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage highlights the role of jadal in refining arguments. It begins by stating that a researcher working alone may think his arguments are sound, but when exposed to others, contradictions become apparent, leading to revision. This is a key benefit of dialectical method. The author gives an example from usul al-fiqh: the majority opinion on the 'wajib mukhayyar' (a type of obligation where one of several alternatives must be chosen, but the specific act is not predetermined) holds that the obligation is one unspecified act, and they support this with rational permissibility and 'occurrence' (wuqu') in shari'a. The scholar al-Tufi, though from the majority, criticizes their use of occurrence: if the issue is disputed, claiming that it occurred is itself the point of dispute. He argues that the obligation in expiation (kaffara) is all the characteristics (i.e., multiple acts), because it falls under the disputed subject. This shows how dialectical critique refines evidence by pointing out circularity or question-begging.

Key Terms
الواجب المخير
الوقوع

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The majority had no evidence from rational permissibility
D) The wajib mukhayyar does not exist in shari'a

Key Takeaway
In jadal, a premise that is itself the subject of dispute cannot be used as evidence without first establishing it independently.

Clarifying the Subject of Dispute (Tahrir Mahall al-Niza')
Author explains how jadal resolves verbal disputes using tahrir mahall al-niza'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between formal and substantive disagreements in usul al-fiqh by applying the method of tahrir mahall al-niza'.

Core Idea
Jadal's first task is to ensure the dispute is real, not just semantic.

In Islamic dialectic, many disputes arise not from genuine differences but from unclear formulation of the point of disagreement. The author introduces the concept of tahrir mahall al-niza' as a tool to clarify the precise issue. By analyzing the example of fard kifaya, we will see how apparent disagreements dissolve when the real point of divergence is identified.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two sides of the dispute
Look for the two groups: those who require combination of usul and ahkam knowledge, and those who allow specialization via imitation.
2
Determine what they actually agree on
Both sides accept that fard kifaya is a collective obligation; their disagreement is about the modality (how it applies to individuals).
3
Assess whether the difference is in wording or in ruling
The author notes that the first two positions on modality differ only in expression, not in practical outcome.
4
Apply the principle of formal vs substantive
If the difference has no practical effect, it is merely formal. The author declares the first disagreement 'purely formal with no apparent benefit.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
8 - حسم النزاع في المسائل التي لم يقع الاختلاف فيها حقيقة : في بعض الأحيان لا يتم تحرير محل النزاع في المسائل المختلف فيها، ويكون اختلاف التعبير عنها سببًا في دوران الجدل حولها، مع أن الاتفاق بين الفريقين المتجادلين قائم في شأنها . وهنا يأتي دور الجدل الأصولي ليكشف عن بعض هذه الاختلافات الصورية التي هي اتفاقات في الحقيقة . من ذلك ما أورده السمعاني في قوله : يجب أن يجتمع العلم بالأصول والأحكام في كل واحد من أهل الكفاية، ولا يختص بكفاية العلم بالأحكام فريق وبكفاية العلم بالأصول فريق . فإن تفرد بعلم الأحكام فريق وبعلم الأصول فريق، لم يسقط بواحد منهما فرض الكفاية في الأحكام والأصول، لأن الأحكام فروع الأصول، والأصول موضوعة للفروع، فلم يجز انفراد أحدهما عن الآخر. وذهب من قال : إن العالم يجوز له تقليد العالم، إلى أنه لا يلزم الجمع بينهما، وأنه إذا انفرد بكل واحد من الأمرين جعله كاجتماعهما في الواحد وسقط بذلك فرض الكفاية . واختلفوا بعد هذا في كيفية الوجوب في الواجب على الكفاية . فذهب طائفة من الفقهاء والأشعرية من المتكلمين إلى أنه واجب على كل واحد من أهل الفريضة بعينه، بشرط أن لم يقم به غيره. وذهبت طائفة من الفقهاء والمعتزلة من المتكلمين إلى أنه غير واجب على بعينه، إلا بشرط أن لا يقوم به غيره فيكون على الوجه الأول فرضًا إلا أن يقوم به الغير فيسقط وعلى الثاني غير فرض إلا أن لا يقوم به الغير فيجب. وذهب بعضهم إلى أنه إن غلب على ظنه أنه يقوم به غيره لا يجب عليه، وإن غلب على ظنه أنه لا يقوم به أحد وجب عليه. وهذا وجه حسن. والخلاف الأول محض صورة، لا ظهور فائدة فلا أرى له معنى (1).
8 - Resolving the dispute in issues where there is no real disagreement: Sometimes the subject of dispute is not clarified in disputed issues, and differences in expression become the reason for the dialectical exchange, while agreement between the disputing parties actually exists on it. Here comes the role of usuli dialectic to uncover some of these formal differences that are in reality agreements. Among that is what al-Sam'ani mentioned in his saying: 'Knowledge of principles (usul) and rulings (ahkam) must be combined in every one of the people of sufficiency (ahl al-kifaya), and it is not that one group specializes in knowledge of rulings and another group in knowledge of principles. For if one group specializes in knowledge of rulings and another in knowledge of principles, the collective obligation (fard kifaya) in rulings and principles is not fulfilled by either of them alone, because rulings are branches of principles, and principles are established for the branches, so it is not permissible for one to be separate from the other.' And some argued that a scholar may imitate another scholar, so combination is not necessary, and if he specializes in each of the two matters, it is considered as if they were combined in one person, and thereby the collective obligation is fulfilled. And they disagreed after this about the modality of obligation in the collective obligation. A group of jurists and Ash'ari theologians held that it is obligatory on each individual of the people of the duty, on condition that no one else fulfills it. Another group of jurists and Mu'tazili theologians held that it is not obligatory on each individual, except on condition that no one else fulfills it; so according to the first view, it is a duty unless another fulfills it, then it drops; according to the second, it is not a duty unless others do not fulfill it, then it becomes obligatory. And some held that if he strongly thinks that someone else will fulfill it, it is not obligatory on him, and if he strongly thinks that no one will fulfill it, it is obligatory. This is a good view. And the first disagreement is purely formal, with no apparent benefit, so I do not see any meaning to it.
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage addresses how jadal can resolve disputes that are merely apparent. The author states that sometimes the subject of dispute is not properly defined, leading to a debate over expressions while actual agreement exists. Usuli dialectic uncovers such formal differences. The author gives an example from the scholar al-Sam'ani regarding the collective obligation (fard kifaya) of acquiring knowledge of both usul (principles) and ahkam (rulings). Al-Sam'ani argues that both must be combined in each person capable; specialization in one only does not fulfill the obligation. Then the author presents a counter-view: that a scholar may imitate another, so specialization is acceptable, and the obligation is fulfilled as if combined. The author then describes three different positions on the modality of obligation for fard kifaya: (1) obligatory on each individual unless someone else does it, (2) not obligatory unless no one else does it, and (3) based on strong presumption (ghalabat al-zann) whether others will do it. The author remarks that the first disagreement (between the first two positions) is purely formal with no practical benefit, thus resolving the dispute as merely verbal.

Key Terms

Assessing Formal vs Substantive Disagreements: The Fard Kifaya Example
Author demonstrates how to identify formal disagreements using fard kifaya

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to assess whether a disagreement in usul al-fiqh has practical consequences by applying the criterion of formal vs substantive.

Core Idea
A disagreement that does not affect the ruling is not worth debating.

The author explicitly states that the first two positions on fard kifaya are purely formal because they lead to identical rulings. This lesson teaches you how to detect such formal disagreements in usul by focusing on practical outcome. We will analyze the three positions on fard kifaya and understand why the third based on ghalabat al-zann is considered superior.

Guided Path
1
List the three positions on the modality of fard kifaya
Position A: obligatory on each individual unless someone else fulfills it. Position B: not obligatory on each unless no one else fulfills it. Position C: based on strong presumption (ghalabat al-zann) of whether others will do it.
2
Compare the practical outcomes of positions A and B
In both, the individual's obligation is contingent on others' actions: if someone does it, the obligation drops; if not, it remains. The difference is purely linguistic.
3
See why the author calls them 'purely formal'
Since they yield identical results in all cases, the disagreement has no practical effect. The author says 'لا ظهور فائدة' (no apparent benefit).
4
Understand the advantage of the third position
The third position introduces a subjective element (ghalabat al-zann) that can lead to different judgments depending on the individual's assessment, so it is not merely formal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
8 - حسم النزاع في المسائل التي لم يقع الاختلاف فيها حقيقة : في بعض الأحيان لا يتم تحرير محل النزاع في المسائل المختلف فيها، ويكون اختلاف التعبير عنها سببًا في دوران الجدل حولها، مع أن الاتفاق بين الفريقين المتجادلين قائم في شأنها . وهنا يأتي دور الجدل الأصولي ليكشف عن بعض هذه الاختلافات الصورية التي هي اتفاقات في الحقيقة . من ذلك ما أورده السمعاني في قوله : يجب أن يجتمع العلم بالأصول والأحكام في كل واحد من أهل الكفاية، ولا يختص بكفاية العلم بالأحكام فريق وبكفاية العلم بالأصول فريق . فإن تفرد بعلم الأحكام فريق وبعلم الأصول فريق، لم يسقط بواحد منهما فرض الكفاية في الأحكام والأصول، لأن الأحكام فروع الأصول، والأصول موضوعة للفروع، فلم يجز انفراد أحدهما عن الآخر. وذهب من قال : إن العالم يجوز له تقليد العالم، إلى أنه لا يلزم الجمع بينهما، وأنه إذا انفرد بكل واحد من الأمرين جعله كاجتماعهما في الواحد وسقط بذلك فرض الكفاية . واختلفوا بعد هذا في كيفية الوجوب في الواجب على الكفاية . فذهب طائفة من الفقهاء والأشعرية من المتكلمين إلى أنه واجب على كل واحد من أهل الفريضة بعينه، بشرط أن لم يقم به غيره. وذهبت طائفة من الفقهاء والمعتزلة من المتكلمين إلى أنه غير واجب على بعينه، إلا بشرط أن لا يقوم به غيره فيكون على الوجه الأول فرضًا إلا أن يقوم به الغير فيسقط وعلى الثاني غير فرض إلا أن لا يقوم به الغير فيجب. وذهب بعضهم إلى أنه إن غلب على ظنه أنه يقوم به غيره لا يجب عليه، وإن غلب على ظنه أنه لا يقوم به أحد وجب عليه. وهذا وجه حسن. والخلاف الأول محض صورة، لا ظهور فائدة فلا أرى له معنى (1).
8 - Resolving the dispute in issues where there is no real disagreement: Sometimes the subject of dispute is not clarified in disputed issues, and differences in expression become the reason for the dialectical exchange, while agreement between the disputing parties actually exists on it. Here comes the role of usuli dialectic to uncover some of these formal differences that are in reality agreements. Among that is what al-Sam'ani mentioned in his saying: 'Knowledge of principles (usul) and rulings (ahkam) must be combined in every one of the people of sufficiency (ahl al-kifaya), and it is not that one group specializes in knowledge of rulings and another group in knowledge of principles. For if one group specializes in knowledge of rulings and another in knowledge of principles, the collective obligation (fard kifaya) in rulings and principles is not fulfilled by either of them alone, because rulings are branches of principles, and principles are established for the branches, so it is not permissible for one to be separate from the other.' And some argued that a scholar may imitate another scholar, so combination is not necessary, and if he specializes in each of the two matters, it is considered as if they were combined in one person, and thereby the collective obligation is fulfilled. And they disagreed after this about the modality of obligation in the collective obligation. A group of jurists and Ash'ari theologians held that it is obligatory on each individual of the people of the duty, on condition that no one else fulfills it. Another group of jurists and Mu'tazili theologians held that it is not obligatory on each individual, except on condition that no one else fulfills it; so according to the first view, it is a duty unless another fulfills it, then it drops; according to the second, it is not a duty unless others do not fulfill it, then it becomes obligatory. And some held that if he strongly thinks that someone else will fulfill it, it is not obligatory on him, and if he strongly thinks that no one will fulfill it, it is obligatory. This is a good view. And the first disagreement is purely formal, with no apparent benefit, so I do not see any meaning to it.
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage addresses how jadal can resolve disputes that are merely apparent. The author states that sometimes the subject of dispute is not properly defined, leading to a debate over expressions while actual agreement exists. Usuli dialectic uncovers such formal differences. The author gives an example from the scholar al-Sam'ani regarding the collective obligation (fard kifaya) of acquiring knowledge of both usul (principles) and ahkam (rulings). Al-Sam'ani argues that both must be combined in each person capable; specialization in one only does not fulfill the obligation. Then the author presents a counter-view: that a scholar may imitate another, so specialization is acceptable, and the obligation is fulfilled as if combined. The author then describes three different positions on the modality of obligation for fard kifaya: (1) obligatory on each individual unless someone else does it, (2) not obligatory unless no one else does it, and (3) based on strong presumption (ghalabat al-zann) whether others will do it. The author remarks that the first disagreement (between the first two positions) is purely formal with no practical benefit, thus resolving the dispute as merely verbal.

Key Takeaway
A disagreement that has no practical effect is merely formal and should be set aside in usul debate, as it does not advance real knowledge.

Identifying Purely Formal Disputes in Dialectic
Author uses al-Sam'ani's example to show purely formal disagreement on fard kifaya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a disagreement in Islamic legal theory is purely formal (maḥḍ ṣūra) and distinguish it from a substantive disagreement.

Core Idea
Not every difference of opinion is real; some are merely verbal and dissolve upon analysis.

In dialectical reasoning (jadal), it is crucial to distinguish between real disagreements that affect rulings and purely formal ones that are only apparent. The author presents al-Sam'ani's discussion on farḍ al-kifāya to show that two seemingly opposing views can be logically equivalent in practical outcome. Understanding this helps you avoid wasting time on sterile debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Read the passage and locate the two views about the obligation of farḍ al-kifāya in acquiring both uṣūl and furū‘. One view requires combination; the other allows separation through imitation.
2
Analyze the conditional obligation
Note the two formulations: (1) It is obligatory on each individual on condition that no one else does it; (2) It is not obligatory on any individual except on condition that no one else does it. Write them side by side.
These are the formulations the author calls maḥḍ ṣūra.
3
Compare the practical outcomes
Check whether both formulations lead to the same result in every possible scenario: if no one else acts, it becomes obligatory ; if someone else acts, it is not obligatory. They are logically equivalent.
4
Recognize the formal nature
The author dismisses this difference as purely formal (maḥḍ ṣūra) because it has no practical consequence. Understand that such disagreements arise from different phrasing but express the same rule.
This identification is a key skill in jadal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من ذلك ما أورده السمعاني في قوله : يجب أن يجتمع العلم بالأصول والأحكام في كل واحد من أهل الكفاية، ولا يختص بكفاية العلم بالأحكام فريق وبكفاية العلم بالأصول فريق . فإن تفرد بعلم الأحكام فريق وبعلم الأصول فريق، لم يسقط بواحد منهما فرض الكفاية في الأحكام والأصول، لأن الأحكام فروع الأصول، والأصول موضوعة للفروع، فلم يجز انفراد أحدهما عن الآخر. وذهب من قال : إن العالم يجوز له تقليد العالم، إلى أنه لا يلزم الجمع بينهما، وأنه إذا انفرد بكل واحد من الأمرين جعله كاجتماعهما في الواحد وسقط بذلك فرض الكفاية . واختلفوا بعد هذا في كيفية الوجوب في الواجب على الكفاية . فذهب طائفة من الفقهاء والأشعرية من المتكلمين إلى أنه واجب على كل واحد من أهل الفريضة بعينه، بشرط أن لم يقم به غيره. وذهبت طائفة من الفقهاء والمعتزلة من المتكلمين إلى أنه غير واجب على بعينه، إلا بشرط أن لا يقوم به غيره فيكون على الوجه الأول فرضًا إلا أن أحدًا يقوم به الغير فيسقط وعلى الثاني غير فرض إلا أن لا يقوم به الغير فيجب. وذهب بعضهم إلى أنه إن غلب على ظنه أنه يقوم به غيره لا يجب عليه، وإن غلب على ظنه أنه لا يقوم به أحد وجب عليه. وهذا وجه حسن. والخلاف الأول محض صورة، لا فائدة، فلا أرى له معنى (1)

(1) قواطع الأدلة في الأصول، لأبي مظفر السمعاني، ص: 43 – 44
Among that is what al-Sam'ani mentioned in his statement: It is necessary that knowledge of the principles (uṣūl) and rulings (aḥkām) be combined in every person of sufficiency (ahl al-kifāya), and that sufficiency in knowledge of rulings not be exclusive to one group and sufficiency in knowledge of principles to another. For if one group specializes in knowledge of rulings and another group in knowledge of principles, the collective duty (farḍ al-kifāya) in rulings and principles is not fulfilled by either one, because rulings are branches of principles, and principles are established for the branches, so it is not permissible for one to be isolated from the other. And those who say that a scholar may imitate another scholar held that the combination is not necessary, and that if each of the two matters is undertaken separately, it is considered as if they were combined in one person, and the collective duty is thereby fulfilled. Then they differed after this regarding the manner of obligation in what is obligatory as a collective duty. A group of jurists and Ash'ari theologians held that it is obligatory upon every individual of the obliged persons specifically, on condition that no one else fulfills it. And a group of jurists and Mu'tazili theologians held that it is not obligatory upon any individual specifically except on condition that no one else fulfills it. Thus, according to the first view, it is an obligation unless someone else fulfills it and it falls; according to the second, it is not an obligation unless no one else fulfills it and it becomes obligatory. And some held that if he is strongly likely that someone else will fulfill it, it is not obligatory upon him, and if he is strongly likely that no one will fulfill it, it becomes obligatory. And this is a good position. The first difference is purely formal, with no benefit, so I see no meaning in it.
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage illustrates how a dispute can be purely formal (maḥḍ ṣūra). The author cites al-Sam'ani's discussion on the collective obligation (farḍ al-kifāya) in acquiring knowledge of uṣūl and furū'. Al-Sam'ani describes two opinions on whether a person must combine both sciences. The first view requires combination; the second allows separation through imitation. Then he discusses the nature of the obligation: two views that are logically equivalent—one says it is wajib on each individual conditional on no one else doing it; the other says it is not wajib except conditional on no one else doing it. The author remarks that this first difference is purely formal, with no practical consequence, and dismisses it. This demonstrates the jadal principle of identifying and discarding purely verbal disagreements.

Key Terms
فرض الكفاية
محض صورة

A) Because both views lead to the same practical result.

C) Because the second view is more sound.
D) Because the views are based on different sources.

Key Takeaway
A purely formal disagreement in jadal is one where two positions are logically equivalent in practice; recognizing this saves time and focuses debate on substantive issues.

Resolving Disputes by Distinguishing Definition Types
Author quotes al-Zarkashi to show that the dispute is resolved by distinguishing label from relational definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a dispute arises from conflating label (laqabi) and relational (idafi) definitions, and resolve it by clarifying the intended definition type.

Core Idea
Label definitions name the field; relational definitions describe its content — confusing them creates false disputes.

In Islamic scholarly debates, apparent disagreements often dissolve when we realize the disputants are speaking past each other. This passage from al-Zarkashi shows how an old dispute in Usul al-Fiqh—whether the term 'usul al-fiqh' refers to the principles themselves or to the knowledge of them—is actually not a real conflict. The key is to distinguish between a label definition (ta'rif laqabi), which names the discipline as a conventional field, and a relational definition (ta'rif idafi), which describes its subject matter. Let's see how this logical move works.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two opposing positions
One group (Qadi Abu Bakr, Baydawi, Ibn al-Hajib) says usul al-fiqh is knowledge of the evidence. The other (Abu Ishaq, Juwayni, Razi, Amidi) says it is the evidence itself. Write down the two claims.
Both are quoting from the passage.
2
Recognize the common term but different definitional intents
Both sides use the same phrase 'usul al-fiqh' but they are defining it differently. Ask: is the definer trying to give a name for the science (label) or describe its content (relational)?
This is the core analytical move.
3
Distinguish laqabi from idafi definition
A label definition (laqabi) treats the term as a proper name for a discipline. A relational definition (idafi) explains what the term refers to in terms of its components. In this passage, 'knowledge' is the label, 'the evidence' is the relational content.
Ibn al-Hajib combined both definitions.
4
Apply the resolution
Al-Zarkashi concludes there is no real disagreement: each side defined according to a different purpose. The dispute dissolves when you specify which definition is intended.
This is the verification (tahqiq) in the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن ذلك ما أورده الزركشي : هل الأصول هذه الحقائق أنفسها أو العلم بها؟ طريقان: وكلام القاضي أبي بكر يقتضي أنه العلم بالأدلة، وعليه البيضاوي وابن الحاجب وغيرهما. وقطع الشيخ أبو إسحاق وإمام الحرمين في «البرهان» والرازي والآمدي بأنه نفس الأدلة ووجه الخلاف أنه كما يتوقف الفقه على هذه الحقائق، يتوقف أيضًا على العلم بها، فيجوز حينئذ إطلاق أصول الفقه على القواعد أنفسها، وعلى العلم بها . . . والتحقيق : أنه لا خلاف في ذلك، ولم يتواردوا على محل واحد، فإن من أراد اللقبي (أي التعريف اللقبي لعلم أصول الفقه) - وهو كونه علمًا على هذا الفن - حَدَّه بالعلم ومن أراد الإضافي حَدَّهه بنفس الأدلة. ولهذا جمع ابن الحاجب بينهما، عرف اللقبي بالعلم والإضافي بالأدلة»(1)

(1) البحر المحيط في أصول الفقه، للزركشي، ج: 1، ص: 25
And among that is what al-Zarkashi mentioned: Are 'the principles' (al-uṣūl) these realities themselves or the knowledge of them? There are two ways: The statement of Qāḍī Abū Bakr indicates that it is knowledge of the evidence, and al-Bayḍāwī, Ibn al-Ḥājib, and others followed him. And Shaykh Abū Isḥāq, Imām al-Ḥaramayn in 'al-Burhān', al-Rāzī, and al-Āmidī definitively stated that it is the evidence itself. The point of disagreement is that just as fiqh depends on these realities, it also depends on knowledge of them, so it is then permissible to apply the term 'uṣūl al-fiqh' to the rules themselves and to knowledge of them... The verification is that there is no disagreement in that, and they did not address a single subject. For whoever intended the label definition (al-taʿrīf al-laqabī) – which is its being a proper name for this discipline – defined it as knowledge, and whoever intended the relational definition (al-taʿrīf al-iḍāfī) defined it as the evidence themselves. For this reason, Ibn al-Ḥājib combined both: he defined the label definition as knowledge and the relational definition as the evidence.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage is an example of resolving an apparently real dispute by clarifying the different angles of definition. The dispute is about whether uṣūl al-fiqh refers to the principles/rules themselves or to the knowledge of them. Al-Zarkashi presents two views: one group (Qadi Abu Bakr et al.) defines it as knowledge of the evidence; another group (Abu Ishaq, Juwayni, Razi, Amidi) says it is the evidence itself. The key insight is that the disputants were not actually disagreeing about the same thing: those defining it as 'knowledge' are giving a label definition (tasmiya/laqabī) – naming the science as a conventional field; those defining it as 'the evidence' are giving a relational definition (iḍāfī) – describing its subject matter. Thus, Ibn al-Ḥājib combined both definitions, showing they are not contradictory. This exemplifies the dialectical principle of tafṣīl (detailed analysis) over summary judgment.

Key Terms
أصول الفقه
التعريف اللقبي
التعريف الإضافي

C) By favoring the view that it refers to knowledge.

Key Takeaway
An apparent dispute dissolves when we clarify whether the definition is a label (naming the discipline) or relational (describing its content).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Principle of Detailed Analysis (Tafṣīl) in Dialectical Disputes
Author argues for detailed treatment of disagreements and gives example about multiple subject matters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the method of tafṣīl to analyse a disputed issue in uṣūl al-fiqh by distinguishing the exact point of disagreement.

Core Idea
Summary judgments fuel disputes; detailed analysis (tafṣīl) isolates the real point of contention.

In Islamic dialectics, many disputes persist because debaters assume the disagreement covers the entire issue when it actually concerns a specific part. This lesson presents the principle of tafṣīl (detailed analysis) as a remedy. Using the example of whether a science can have multiple subject matters, you will learn how to break down a dispute into its components.

Guided Path
1
Identify the global claim being disputed
Find the overarching question that seems to be the source of disagreement. In the example, it is: 'Is it permissible for a science to have more than one subject matter?' This appears to be a yes/no question.
The global claim is often stated at the beginning of the dispute.
2
Locate the sub-issue that is the actual point of disagreement
Look for the specific condition or nuance that splits the opinions. Here, the sub-issue is whether the permissibility depends on the subject matter being relational (iḍāfī) or not. The first two positions (absolute yes, absolute no) are actually too broad; the real disagreement is about the role of relationality.
Sub-issues are often hidden in the qualifications or examples given.
3
Construct a tafṣīl: propose a condition or qualification that resolves the opposition
Formulate a detailed position that accounts for the different scenarios. The Hanafī author's tafṣīl is: 'If the subject matter is relational, multiple subjects are allowed; otherwise, not.' This breaks the seeming deadlock.
A good tafṣīl accepts both sides partially by introducing a distinguishing factor.
4
Verify that the tafṣīl accounts for the positions raised
Check that the tafṣīl explains why the earlier positions seemed plausible. For example, those who said 'yes absolutely' were thinking of relational sciences like medicine; those who said 'no absolutely' were thinking of non-relational sciences. The tafṣīl harmonizes them.
If a position is left out, the tafṣīl is incomplete.
5
Apply the same method to a new dispute in uṣūl al-fiqh
Choose a debated issue in uṣūl al-fiqh (e.g., whether qiyās is permissible in the presence of a text). Identify the global claim, find the sub-issue, and construct a tafṣīl.
Practice makes the method habitual in dialectical discussions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
9 - التفصيل في المسائل المختلف فيها وعدم الحكم عليها جملة : من دواعي استمرار النزاع في المسائل الخلافية ؛ عدم حصر دائرة الخلاف أنه فيها، فقد يكون الخلاف واقعا في جزء بسيط منها ويتصور واقع فيها كلها فيدور الجدل حول الكل مع أن الأصح دورانه حول الجزء المختلف فيه فحسب، حرص الأصوليون على التفصيل في المسائل المختلف فيها وعدم الحكم عليها جملة . مطلقا، من ذلك ما أورده الزركشي : اختلفوا : هل يجوز أن يكون للعلم أكثر من موضوع واحد أم لا؟ فقيل : يجوز مطلقا، غير أنه لابد أن يشركه في أمر ذاتي، أو عرضي، كالطب يبحث فيه عن أحوال بدن الإنسان، وعن الأدوية، ونحوها. وقيل : يمتنع ، لئلا يؤدي إلى الانتشار .. واختار صاحب «التوضيح من الحنفية تفصيلا وهو : إن كان المبحوث عنه في ذلك العلم إضافيا؛ جاز، كما أنه يبحث في الأصول عن إثبات الأدلة للحكم، والمنطق يبحث فيه عن إيصال تصور أو تصديق، إلى تصور أو تصديق، وقد يكون بعض العوارض التي لا
9 - Detailed analysis in disputed matters and not passing a summary judgment on them: Among the causes of the continuation of disputes is the failure to limit the circle of disagreement to its actual part. For the disagreement may lie in a small part of it, but one imagines it lies in the whole, so the debate revolves around the whole, whereas the correct approach is to revolve around the part that is actually disputed. For this reason, the scholars of uṣūl were careful to provide detailed analysis in disputed matters and not to pass a summary judgment on them absolutely. Among that is what al-Zarkashi mentioned: They disagreed: Is it permissible for a science to have more than one subject matter or not? It was said: It is absolutely permissible, but it must share a common essential or accidental aspect, such as medicine, which investigates the states of the human body, as well as medications and the like. And it was said: It is impossible, lest it lead to dispersion... And the author of 'al-Tawḍīḥ' among the Ḥanafīs chose a detailed view: If the object of investigation in that science is relational (iḍāfī), it is permissible, just as in uṣūl one investigates the establishment of evidence for rulings, and in logic one investigates conveying a conception or assent to a conception or assent, and there may be some accidental attributes that...
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage explains the principle of tafṣīl (detailed analysis) in dealing with disputed matters. The author warns against assuming that a disagreement covers the entire issue when it actually concerns only a part. He cites al-Zarkashi's example: the dispute over whether a science can have more than one subject matter. Some say yes absolutely, some say no, and a third position (from the Ḥanafī scholar) offers a detailed view: if the subject matter is relational (iḍāfī), multiple subjects are allowed, citing uṣūl al-fiqh and logic as examples. The text cuts off while giving further detail about accidental attributes. This illustrates how a seemingly global dispute is resolved by pinpointing the specific point of disagreement.

Key Terms
التفصيل (al-tafṣīl)
الموضوع (al-mawḍūʿ)
إضافي (iḍāfī)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is one reason for the persistence of disputes?
A) Failure to limit the circle of disagreement to the part actually in dispute.
B) The complexity of the issue.
C) Lack of qualified debaters.
D) The use of technical terms.

Key Takeaway
Detailed analysis (tafṣīl) is the essential dialectical tool for resolving disputes by isolating the precise point of disagreement and introducing a condition that accounts for opposing views.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Take the disputed issue in uṣūl al-fiqh: 'Is qiyās (analogy) permissible when a text (naṣṣ) exists?' Identify at least two opposing positions, then construct a tafṣīl that introduces a condition (e.g., based on the strength of the text) to harmonize them.
