By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the characteristics of a frivolous opponent (mujun) and explain why debating them is prohibited based on Ibn 'Aqil's advice.

Core Idea
Do not debate those who seek only vainglory, not truth.

This lesson addresses a practical scenario: when you know your opponent's goal is not truth but self-promotion. Ibn 'Aqil provides clear signs of such opponents and advises avoiding them. This ruling protects the debater's time, religion, and reputation.

Accepting Truth from an Opponent in Debate
Author states the principle of yielding to an opponent's truth and quotes al-Tufi, al-Shafi'i, and al-'Izz ibn 'Abd al-Salam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to articulate the principle of taslim in jadal and identify its justification from classical authorities.

Core Idea
In Islamic debate, yielding to truth is a religious duty, not a defeat.

This passage sets the foundational ethics of jadal: the debater's sole aim must be the manifestation of truth, not personal victory. The author supports this principle with the authority of al-Tufi, al-Shafi'i, and al-'Izz ibn 'Abd al-Salam, showing that this is a well-established norm. You will learn to recognize the imperative of conceding when the opponent's argument is strong and to distinguish truth-seeking from partisanship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core command
Read the opening sentence: 'التسليم لمخالفه إذا عرف قوة حجته وصواب رأيه'. The author commands the debater to yield when he recognizes the strength and correctness of the opponent's argument. Notice the word 'تسليم' (taslim) – it implies active concession, not mere quiet agreement.
2
Extract the purpose of jadal
From the quotations of al-Tufi and al-'Izz ibn 'Abd al-Salam, identify the stated purpose of debate. Al-Tufi says: 'يلزم كل واحد منهما قصد إظهار الحق'. Al-'Izz says: 'لا يجوز الجدل والمناظرة إلا لإظهار الحق ونصرته'. Both agree that debate is only permissible for manifesting and supporting truth.
Compare this with the opposite purpose: 'إظهار فضيلته' (showing one's own excellence).
3
Analyze the critique of partisanship
Al-'Izz condemns those who 'يتحيل لنصرة مذهبه مع أدلته من الصواب' (resort to ruses to support his school with false arguments). This includes corrupt interpretations (تأويلات فاسدة) and far-fetched answers (أجوبة نادرة). Identify the ethical violation: placing school loyalty above truth.
4
Apply the principle to a case
Imagine a student of fiqh who, in a debate, realizes that the opponent's evidence from the Quran is stronger. According to the principle of taslim, what should he do? He must announce his retraction and adopt the opponent's view, just as al-Shafi'i said: 'ما ناظرت أحدًا فباليت مع من كانت الحجة، إن كانت معه اتبعته'.
Consider the psychological difficulty; the passage warns against 'التعنت والتعصب' (obstinacy and bias).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التسليم لمخالفه إذا عرف قوة حجته وصواب رأيه، بل يسلم له قوله ويعلن عن رجوعه عن رأيه واعتناقه لرأي مخالفه ولا يحمله التعنت والتعصب على البقاء على رأيه مع علمه بخطئه. قال الطوفي: يلزم كل واحد منهما قصد إظهار الحق في مناظرته لا قصد إظهار فضيلته وأن لا يبالي قامت الحجة له أو عليه، كما نقل عن الشافعي رضي الله عنه أنه قال: ما ناظرت أحدًا فباليت مع من كانت الحجة، إن كانت معه اتبعته. وقال العز بن عبد السلام: لا يجوز الجدل والمناظرة إلا لإظهار الحق ونصرته ليُعرف ويُعمل به، فمن جادل لذلك فقد أطاع وأصاب، ومن جادل لغرض آخر فقد عصى وخاب، ولا خير فيمن يتحيل لنصرة مذهبه مع أدلته من الصواب، بأن يتأول السنة أو الإجماع أو الكتاب على غير الحق والصواب، وذلك بالتأويلات الفاسدة والأجوبة النادرة.
Yielding to one's opponent when he knows the strength of his argument and the correctness of his opinion. Rather, he concedes to his statement and announces his retraction from his own opinion and his adoption of his opponent's opinion, and obstinacy and bias do not compel him to remain upon his opinion while knowing its error. Al-Tufi said: It is necessary for each of the two [debaters] to intend the manifestation of truth in his disputation, not the manifestation of his own excellence, and not to care whether the argument stands for or against him, as was reported from al-Shafi'i, may Allah be pleased with him, that he said: I have never debated anyone and cared with whom the argument lay; if it was with him, I followed him. And al-'Izz ibn 'Abd al-Salam said: Debate and disputation are not permissible except for the manifestation and support of truth so that it may be known and acted upon. Whoever debates for that purpose has obeyed and done right, and whoever debates for another purpose has disobeyed and failed. There is no good in one who resorts to ruses to support his school [of thought] with false arguments, by interpreting the Sunnah, consensus, or the Book contrary to truth and correctness, through corrupt interpretations and far-fetched answers.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage is an ethical instruction on the conduct of jadal (debate). It opens by commanding the debater to accept truth from his opponent when he recognizes its strength, even to the point of publicly retracting his own position. The author then cites three authorities: al-Tufi, who emphasizes that the sole purpose of debate must be to manifest truth, not personal superiority, and that the debater should be indifferent to whether the evidence supports him or his opponent. He then reports al-Shafi'i's famous statement that he only cared about where the truth lay, not who presented it. Finally, al-'Izz ibn 'Abd al-Salam declares debate permissible only for the sake of truth; debating for any other purpose is disobedience. He criticizes those who twist textual evidence to support their school, using corrupt interpretations and far-fetched answers. The passage thus establishes the primary ethical norm of jadal: the sincere pursuit of truth, not victory.

Key Terms
التسليم
الجدل
إظهار الحق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) To demonstrate one's own knowledge and skill
B) To support one's school of thought
C) To manifest and support truth so that it may be known and acted upon
D) To defeat the opponent by any means

Key Takeaway
Taslim – conceding to an opponent's true argument – is a fundamental duty in Islamic debate, rooted in the pursuit of truth rather than victory.

Avoiding debate with the insincere opponent
Author prohibits debating an opponent known to seek only victory

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the characteristics of an opponent who seeks only disputation and determine when it is forbidden to engage in debate.

Core Idea
Not all debate is beneficial; debating the insincere corrupts the pursuit of truth.

In the previous lesson we established that the purpose of debate is to manifest truth, not to achieve personal victory. This passage applies that principle directly: if you know your opponent does not seek truth but only desires prominence, you must not engage in debate with them. The Hanbali jurist Ibn 'Aqil provides a vivid description of such a person and the harm that can result from debating them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the criterion for permissible debate
The passage makes clear that the permissibility of debate depends on the intention of the opponent. The key phrase is 'does not seek truth but only seeks prominence.' This is the litmus test.
The Arabic uses 'لا يبتغي الحق' — literally 'does not seek the truth'.
2
Recognize the warning signs
Ibn 'Aqil lists symptoms: frivolity (majūn), lack of concern for what is said or said to him, no aim to establish proof or support religion, but rather to dispute, boast, and be seen as victorious.
al-majūn here means recklessness in debate, not the original sense of licentiousness.
3
Evaluate the consequences
Nothing of religion or worldly good is gained. Moreover, the insincere debater may bring up embarrassing points, and if the sincere debater is silenced, it becomes a trial (fitna) for the audience.
The harm is not only personal but affects bystanders who may be misled.
4
Apply the ruling
Having identified such an opponent, one must avoid debating them and be cautious in conversing with them. The command is clear: 'يجب أن يجتنب وتحذر مكالمته' — he should be avoided and conversation with him should be feared.
This is an ethical ruling, not just advice; it carries weight in the adab al-bahth tradition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعليه لا يجوز كذلك لمن يعلم أن خصمه لا يبتغي الحق وإنما يبتغي الظهور، أن يجادله أو يدخل معه في حوار حول مسألة من المسائل الشرعية، وفي ذلك يقول ابن عقيل: «إذا كان الخصم معروفا بالمجون في الجدل وقلة الاكتراث بما يقول وما يقال له ليس غرضه إقامة حجة ولا نصرة ديانة وإنما يريد المطالبة والمباهاة وأن يقال: علا قرنه وغلب خصمه أو قطع خصمه، فينبغي أن يجتنب وتحذر مكالمته، فليس يحصل بمناظرته دين ولا دنيا، وربما أورد على خصمه ما يخجله ولا يستحسن مكافأته عليه، فينقطع في يده ويكون في انقطاعه فتنة لمن حضره».
Accordingly, it is also not permissible for one who knows that his opponent does not seek truth but only seeks prominence to debate him or enter into a dialogue with him regarding a matter of the religious sciences. In this regard, Ibn 'Aqil says: 'If the opponent is known for frivolity in debate and lack of concern for what he says and what is said to him, his aim being not to establish a proof nor to support a religion, but rather to dispute and boast, and for it to be said: He overcame his peer and defeated his opponent or silenced his opponent, then one should avoid him and be cautious in conversing with him. For nothing of religion or worldly good is gained from debating him. Perhaps he will bring against his opponent that which embarrasses him and for which it is not considered good to repay him in kind, so that the opponent is left at a loss, and his being at a loss becomes a trial for those present.'
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage is a direct application of the previous principle. Since debate is for truth, it is forbidden to engage with someone who does not seek truth but only desires fame. The author cites Ibn 'Aqil, who describes such a debater as frivolous (majnun in the sense of foolishness), unconcerned with substance, wanting only to be seen as victorious. Engaging with such a person yields no benefit—neither religious nor worldly—and may cause real harm: the honest debater may be embarrassed, and the opponent's disarray may mislead observers. Therefore, the ethical debater must discern intentions and avoid those who would corrupt the pursuit of truth.

Key Terms
المجون
المباهاة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The truth will inevitably emerge
B) The debate will produce beneficial knowledge
C) The opponent may embarrass the sincere debater and cause a trial
D) Both debaters will gain spiritual reward

Key Takeaway
Debate is only permissible when both parties seek truth; debating the insincere is forbidden as it leads to harm and no benefit.

Humility as a Foundation for Debate
Author explains that humility arises from self-knowledge and is essential for productive debate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why humility is essential for productive theological debate and identify the contrast between sincere and arrogant criticism.

Core Idea
Humility in debate springs from self-knowledge: knowing your limits makes you open to truth.

This passage teaches that genuine humility is not weakness but the fruit of honest self-assessment. Recognizing our own ignorance and limited capabilities frees us to engage with opponents without arrogance. The author argues that this attitude transforms debate into a collaborative search for truth, akin to scholarly consultation (mushāwara).

Guided Path
1
Identify the source of humility
According to the author, what knowledge produces humility? Locate the phrase 'knowledge of his own worth and awareness of his weakness, limited capabilities, and lack of knowledge.'
This is the epistemological ground of tawāḍuʿ.
2
Understand the effect on the opponent
How does the debater's humility change the opponent's behaviour? The passage says the opponent feels secure and is encouraged to enter debate without fear.
The ethical conduct creates a safe space for truth-seeking.
3
Compare debate to consultation
Why does the author liken jadal to mushāwara? Find the sentence: 'debate in its reality is more like a consultation about a scientific problem.'
Consultation implies shared purpose, not adversarial victory.
4
Distinguish sincere from insincere criticism
How does humility reveal the debater's true intention? The passage contrasts self-humility as a sign of seeking truth with arrogance that aims to appear authoritative.
The outer demeanor reflects inner motive.

2 - التواضع: أساس التواضع هو معرفة الإنسان بقدر نفسه وعلمه بضعفه ومحدودية إمكانياته وقلة علمه، هذا الشعور هو الذي يجعل الباحث عن الحق يتواضع لمخالفه في الرأي ولا يتكبر عليه ولا يحاول إشعاره بتفوقه عليه، ولذلك فهو يقيم جدله معه على هذا الأساس. وذلك ما يجعل المخالف يشعر بالأمان، فيشجعه ذلك على الدخول في الجدل والمناقشة دون خوف ولا وجل، حيث يشعر أن المناقش لا يريد أن يفرض عليه رأيه، بل يريد أن يؤيده وأن يذهب مذهبه فيكون في وحدة رأييهما قوة لرأي كل واحد منهما. فالجدل في حقيقته هو أشبه بمشاورة في مشكلة علمية وتقليب وجهات النظر للوصول إلى حل سديد في شأنها. إن تواضع الباحث المجادل هو الذي يكشف عن مقصده ويسفر عن غايته، ويؤكد إخلاصه في الوصول إلى الحق واعتناقه عند ظهوره، فالتواضع الذاتي دلالة على أن النقد أو الجدل إنما هو من أجل الحق والخير ومن أجل الإصلاح والتقويم، أما إن كان النقد إلى غير ذلك، فإن صاحبه يصطنع التكبر فيما يقدم من حجج وبينات والترفع على الناس في مخاطبتهم بما انتهى إليه من علم، ليوهم أن ما يقوله هو الحق الذي لا مراء فيه.
2 - Humility: The foundation of humility is man's knowledge of his own worth and awareness of his weakness, limited capabilities, and lack of knowledge. This feeling is what makes the seeker of truth humble toward his opponent in opinion, not arrogant toward him, and not trying to make him feel superior to him. Therefore, he establishes his debate with him on this basis. That makes the opponent feel secure, which encourages him to enter into debate and discussion without fear or dread, as he feels that the discussant does not want to impose his opinion on him, but rather wants to support him and that he should go his own way, so that in the unity of their two opinions there is strength for each opinion. Indeed, debate in its reality is more like a consultation about a scientific problem and a turning over of viewpoints to reach a sound solution regarding it. The humility of the researcher-debater is what reveals his intention and uncovers his goal, and confirms his sincerity in reaching truth and adopting it when it appears. For self-humility is an indication that criticism or debate is for the sake of truth and good, and for the sake of reform and correction. But if criticism is for other than that, then its practitioner feigns arrogance in presenting arguments and evidence, and shows superiority over people in addressing them with what he has attained of knowledge, to make believe that what he says is the truth about which there is no doubt.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage discusses the ethical virtue of humility (tawāḍuʿ) as foundational to proper debate. It begins by defining humility as rooted in self-knowledge: realizing one's own weakness and limited knowledge. This awareness naturally leads a truth-seeker to be humble toward an opponent, not arrogant or domineering. The author then explains the practical benefit: the opponent feels safe and is encouraged to participate freely, knowing that the debate is not about imposition but about collaborative truth-seeking. The passage analogizes jadal to consultation (mushāwara) among scholars. Finally, it contrasts true humility, which signals sincere pursuit of truth and reform, with false arrogance that aims to appear authoritative. The passage thus grounds ethical conduct in epistemological modesty.

Intellectual Flexibility in Jadal
Author asserts that seeking truth makes the debater intellectually flexible and open to opposing views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the intention to seek truth in debate requires intellectual flexibility and identify violations of this principle in scholarly exchanges.

Core Idea
Sincere truth-seeking makes you flexible, ready to accept evidence from any side.

This lesson examines a core ethical principle for the debater in the Islamic tradition: intellectual flexibility (al-murūna al-fikriyya). The passage ties this quality directly to the debater's primary intention—seeking the truth (al-haqq). Without that sincere intention, debate becomes a contest of egos, and intellectual flexibility is lost. Understanding this principle helps you evaluate whether a debater is genuinely pursuing truth or merely defending a position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the debater's intention
Find the phrase that states the debater's goal. The passage says 'the debater's intention of reaching truth in his debate' – this is the foundation.
Intention (qaṣd) is key in Islamic ethics; here it drives the whole process.
2
Extract the resulting traits
Identify what qualities this intention produces: intellectual flexibility, readiness to listen to the other opinion, and contemplation of its arguments and evidences.
These are not passive; they require active engagement with the opponent's case.
3
Apply to a fiqh debate scenario
Consider a debate about whether a specific contract involves ribā. If one debater refuses to consider the other's textual evidence, he violates this principle because his intention is not truth but victory.
Intellectual flexibility does not mean abandoning principles; it means following evidence.
4
Distinguish from intellectual weakness
Recognize that flexibility is not relativism. The debater accepts truth only when it becomes clear (idhā tabayyana lahu). It requires evidence, not mere openness.
The passage was cut off at 'then if it becomes clear to him' – implying acceptance upon clarity.

3 - Intellectual flexibility and readiness to accept truth when it appears: As was said before, the debater's intention of reaching truth in his debate makes him intellectually flexible and ready to listen to the other opinion and to contemplate its elements, arguments, and evidences. Commentary
This passage begins a new ethical principle: intellectual flexibility (al-murūna al-fikriyya) and readiness to accept truth. It connects this trait to the debater's primary intention: seeking truth. The author argues that this goal naturally produces flexibility and openness to opposing views, including careful examination of their evidences. The passage is interrupted at the phrase 'then if it becomes clear to him,' presumably continuing with the action of accepting that truth. Even as a fragment, it reinforces the theme that the sincere truth-seeker must be mentally agile and unbiased, ready to follow the evidence wherever it leads.

Ethical Qualities of the Debater: Humility and Flexibility
Author discusses two essential ethical qualities for the debater: humility and intellectual openness to truth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two key ethical qualities required of a debater in the Islamic tradition and explain how they serve the pursuit of truth.

Core Idea
Humility and intellectual flexibility transform debate from egoistic competition into a cooperative search for truth.

In the Islamic tradition, debate (jadal) is not about winning but about reaching truth. This lesson explores two essential ethical qualities every debater must possess: humility (tawāḍuʿ) and intellectual flexibility (murūna fikriyya). These qualities ensure that scholarly discussion remains constructive and focused on truth rather than personal pride.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the foundation of humility
The debater's awareness of his own limitations (weakness, limited knowledge) leads to genuine humility, preventing arrogance.
2
Understand the effect of humility
Humility creates a safe environment for the opponent, encouraging open discussion without fear of domination.
The passage compares debate to consultation (mushāwara).
3
Identify intellectual flexibility
Intellectual flexibility means readiness to listen to the other opinion, evaluate evidence objectively, and accept truth even from an opponent.
4
Examine scholarly support
Quotes from al-Sayrafi, al-Baji, al-Tufi, and al-Shafi'i reinforce the importance of acknowledging truth and respecting those who accept proof.
These scholars exemplify the ethical ideal in practice.
5
Contrast with sterile debate
Without humility and flexibility, debate becomes a sterile display of verbal muscle and ego, not a genuine search for truth.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
2 - التواضع : أساس التواضع: معرفة الإنسان بقدر نفسه وعلمه بضعفه ومحدودية إمكانياته وقلة علمه. هذا الشعور هو الذي يجعل الباحث عن الحق يتواضع لمخالفه في الرأي ولا يتكبر عليه ولا يحاول إشعاره بتفوقه عليه. هذا الأساس في سلوك كل إنسان عاقل، وهو إنما ينبع من معرفة الإنسان بقدر نفسه. ولذلك فهو يقيم جدله معه على هذا الأساس. وذلك ما يجعل المخالف يشعر بالأمان، فيشجعه ذلك على الدخول في الجدل والمناقشة دون خوف ولا وجل، حيث يشعر أن المناقش لا يريد أن يفرض عليه رأيه، بل يريد أن يؤيده وأن يذهب مذهبه فيكون في وحدة رأييهما قوة لرأي كل واحد منهما. فالجدل في حقيقته هو أشبه بمشاورة في مشكلة علمية وتقليب وجهات النظر للوصول إلى حل سديد في شأنها. إن تواضع الباحث المجادل هو الذي يكشف عن مقصده ويسفر عن غايته، ويؤكد إخلاصه في الوصول إلى الحق واعتناقه عند ظهوره، «فالتواضع الذاتي دلالة على أن النقد أو الجدل إنما هو من أجل الحق والخير .. ومن أجل الإصلاح والتقويم . . أما إن كان النقد إلى غير ذلك، فإن صاحبه يصطنع التكبر فيما يقدم من حجج وبينات والترفع على الناس في مخاطبتهم بما انتهى إليه من علم، ليوهم أن ما يقوله هو الحق الذي لا مراء فيه. 3 - المرونة الفكرية والاستعداد لتقبل الحق عند ظهوره : كما سبق القول فإن قصد المجادل الوصول إلى الحق في جدله يجعله مرنا فكريًا ومستعدا للاستماع للرأي الآخر وتأمل عناصره وحججه وبيناته، فإذا تبين له صوابه ورجحانه على رأيه الذاتي، لم يمنعه كون هذا الرأي صادرا عن مخالفه أن يعتنقه ويتراجع عن رأيه، لذلك على كل مجادل أن يستصحب هذه المرونة الفكرية دائمًا في كل حوار علمي يجري بينه وبين غيره ممن يخالفه الرأي، قال أبو بكر الصيرفي : واعترف بالحق إذا وضح، فإن لم يضح فالزم البرهان. وقال الباجي : إذا بان له الحق أذعن له وانقاد إليه، فإن الغرض بالنظر إصابة الحق. إن هذه المرونة الفكرية هي التي ترفع من مقام المجادل وتظهر إخلاصه للحق والعلم، وتجعل مخالفيه يحترمونه ويصغون إلى حديثه . قال الطوفي : وليقبل كل واحد منهما من صاحبه الحجة، فإنه أنبل لقدره وأَعْوَنُ على إدراك الحق وسلوك سبيل الصدق، قال الشافعي رضي الله عنه : ما ناظرت أحدا فقبل مني الحجة إلا عظم في عيني ولا ردها عليَّ إلا سقط في عيني. فالباحث المؤمن بطبيعته لين القلب، إذا ظهر له الحق يعترف به، ويترك ما كان عليه، والرجوع إلى الحق فريضة. وفقدان هذه المرونة وهذا الاستعداد لتقبل الحق عند ظهوره يجعل النقاش في موضع النزاع يتحول إلى جدل عقيم لا يراد منه إلا تسجيل المزيد من مواقف عرض العضلات الكلامية والمزايدات الجدلية التي لا تقدم أو تأخر في الموضوع، لأن الفكرة قد أعدت سلفًا بشكل لا مجال للتراجع عنه مهما كانت الأدلة المضادة، تبعًا للدوافع الذاتية والاجتماعية التي لا رابط لها بالقناعة الذاتية الفكرية المرتكزة على أساس من الحجة والبرهان.
2 - Humility: The foundation of humility is man's knowledge of his own capacity, his awareness of his weakness, limited abilities, and lack of knowledge. This feeling is what makes the seeker of truth humble towards his opponent in opinion, neither arrogant towards him nor trying to make him feel his superiority. This foundation is the basis of every rational person's conduct, and it stems from man's knowledge of his own capacity. Therefore he conducts his argument with him on this basis. This makes the opponent feel safe, encouraging him to enter into debate and discussion without fear or anxiety, where he feels that the discussant does not want to impose his opinion, but rather wants to support him and let him follow his own method, so that in the unity of their views there is strength for each opinion. For in its essence, debate is like a consultation on a scientific problem, turning over viewpoints to reach a sound solution to it. Indeed, the humility of the researcher-debater is what reveals his intention and exposes his aim, and confirms his sincerity in reaching the truth and embracing it when it appears. 'Self-humility is an indication that criticism or debate is only for the sake of truth and good ... and for the sake of reform and correction. But if criticism is otherwise, its author pretends arrogance in the arguments and proofs he presents and condescension to people in addressing them with what he has attained of knowledge, to make them believe that what he says is the truth that cannot be disputed.' 3 - Intellectual flexibility and readiness to accept truth when it appears: As previously stated, the debater's aim to reach truth in his argument makes him intellectually flexible and ready to listen to the other opinion and contemplate its elements, arguments, and proofs. When its correctness and preponderance over his own subjective opinion becomes clear to him, the fact that this opinion comes from his opponent does not prevent him from embracing it and retracting his own opinion. Therefore, every debater must constantly maintain this intellectual flexibility in every scholarly dialogue that takes place between him and others who disagree with him. Abu Bakr al-Sayrafi said: 'Acknowledge the truth when it becomes clear; if it is not clear, adhere to demonstration.' Al-Baji said: 'When the truth becomes evident to him, he submits to it and yields to it, for the purpose of investigation is to hit the truth.' Indeed, this intellectual flexibility raises the standing of the debater and shows his sincerity to truth and knowledge, and makes his opponents respect him and listen to his speech. Al-Tufi said: 'Let each of them accept the proof from his companion, for that is nobler for his status and more helpful in perceiving truth and following the path of honesty.' Imam al-Shafi'i, may Allah be pleased with him, said: 'I never debated anyone who accepted my proof except that he became great in my eyes, nor did anyone reject it except that he fell in my estimation.' The believing researcher, by his nature, is soft-hearted; when the truth appears to him, he acknowledges it and leaves what he was upon, and returning to the truth is an obligation. The loss of this flexibility and readiness to accept truth when it appears turns the discussion in a point of contention into a sterile debate, intended only to record further stances of displaying verbal muscle and dialectical bidding that neither advance nor delay the topic, because the idea has been prepared in advance in a way that leaves no room for retreat, no matter what contrary evidence appears, due to subjective and social motives that have no connection to intellectual conviction based on proof and demonstration.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage discusses two fundamental ethical qualities required for a debater who seeks truth: humility (tawāḍuʿ) and intellectual flexibility (al-murūna al-fikriyya). The author begins by defining the foundation of humility: it is the debater's awareness of his own limitations—his weakness, limited abilities, and lack of knowledge. This self-awareness naturally makes him humble toward his opponent, neither being arrogant nor trying to assert superiority. This attitude creates a safe environment where the opponent feels comfortable engaging in debate, knowing that the goal is not to impose one's view but to reach a joint stronger opinion. The author analogizes true debate to a consultation (mushāwara) on a scientific problem, where different viewpoints are examined to arrive at a sound solution. Humility reveals the debater's true intention and sincerity in seeking truth; a quote warns that self-humility shows that criticism is for the sake of truth and reform, whereas arrogance in presenting arguments indicates insincerity.

The second quality is intellectual flexibility: because the debater's goal is truth, he must be ready to listen to the other opinion, examine its evidence, and if it proves stronger than his own, accept it without being hindered by the fact that it comes from an opponent. The author quotes Abū Bakr al-Ṣayrafī urging acknowledgment of truth when clear, and al-Bājī stating that the purpose of inquiry is to hit the truth. This flexibility raises the debater's status and earns respect from opponents. Al-Ṭūfī advises mutual acceptance of proof, and al-Shāfiʿī notes that he respects those who accept his proof and loses respect for those who reject it. The author concludes that the believing researcher is naturally soft-hearted and sees returning to truth as an obligation. Lacking this flexibility turns debate into sterile argumentation driven by ego and social pressures, not genuine pursuit of truth.

Key Terms
التواضع (al-tawāḍuʿ): humility; awareness of one's limitations that prevents arrogance.
المرونة الفكرية (al-murūna al-fikriyya): intellectual flexibility; readiness to accept truth from opponents.
الجدل (al-jadal): dialectical debate aimed at truth, akin to consultation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary purpose of humility in debate?
A) To make the opponent feel inferior and thus more likely to concede.
B) To reveal the debater's sincerity and create a safe environment for the opponent.
C) To demonstrate the debater's extensive knowledge.
D) To avoid having to change one's opinion.

Key Takeaway
The ethical debater approaches argument with humility, recognizing his limitations, and with flexibility, ready to accept truth wherever it appears.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Intellectual Flexibility in Debate
Author emphasizes the necessity of intellectual flexibility and readiness to accept truth in debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the principle of intellectual flexibility in jadal and recognize its application in scholarly debate.

Core Idea
The goal of debate is truth, not victory; flexibility is essential.

In this lesson, we explore a foundational ethic of Islamic scholarly debate: intellectual flexibility (al-murūnah al-fikriyyah). Drawing on classical authorities, the author argues that a true researcher must be ready to accept the truth even from an opponent and willingly retract his own view. We will see how this principle elevates debate and what happens when it is absent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core principle
The author opens by stating that a debater should accept truth even from an opponent. Look for the statement: 'he is not prevented by the fact that this opinion comes from his opponent from adopting it.'
2
Examine the supporting authorities
Read the quotes from al-Sayrafi, al-Baji, and al-Shafi'i. Note how each emphasizes accepting truth and the argument (ḥujjah) from the opponent.
These quotes reinforce the principle.
3
Analyze the consequences of lacking flexibility
The author contrasts the flexible researcher with the obstinate one. Identify the description of the inflexible researcher and the results: sterile polemic, error, sophistry.
Pay attention to the phrase 'sterile polemic'.
4
Apply the principle to a real case
Read the example of al-Juwayni acknowledging the criticism of al-Shafi'i. How does this show flexibility? What was the criticism about?
This is a concrete demonstration from usul al-fiqh.

الخطأ
والباحث الذي يفتقد إلى هذه المرونة لا يسمى باحثا في الحقيقة وإنما هو معاند لا غير، ولذلك فهو لا يستطيع أن يبذل الجهد الكفيل بهدايته إلى الصواب وبوقايته من الخطأ، ويدل على انعدام الدواعي إلى البحث لديه، وعلى الاستعداد لتقبل كل ما يدعى إليه . فالذي لا يعترف بمجانبته للصواب عندما يتبين له ذلك، ولا يقلع عن عند معرفة أسبابه إنسان لا يمكنه أن يساهم في إحقاق الحق وفي إبطال الباطل، بل يكون إنسانًا لا يستجيب لمطالب العقل، لا مع نفسه، ولا مع غيره، وبالتالي لا يمكن أن يحصل له الاقتناع الذي يمكنه من الإقناع، بل يحصل منه الغلط والمغالطة والتمادي عليهما ) . فلا بد – إذا – من تنمية روح المرونة الفكرية والاستعداد لتغيير الرأي في التصورات أو التصديقات عندما يقوم الدليل على ذلك، لأن كل ما لا دليل عليه لا محل له في البحث .. كما أن الاقتصار على الرؤية من زاوية واحدة واتباع العادات الفكرية الراسخة من شأنهما أن يتحولا لدى الباحث غير المرن إلى مقاييس للتمييز بين الخطأ والصواب، والحق والباطل. شأن فقدان المرونة الفكرية، أن يتسبب في ضيق الأفق لدى الباحث، فلا ما يراه غيره في أفق آخر، وعندئذ يضيق لديه مجال الإبداع .. الذي يتسع باتساع الرؤية، ويتنوع بتنوع زاوية الرؤية»(2) . هذا وإن من نماذج المرونة الفكرية عند علماء أصول الفقه ؛ اعتراف الجويني بصحة النقد الذي توجه به أبو بكر بن داود الأصفهاني الظاهري إلى الإمام الشافعي، وذلك حين أغفل ذكر الإجماع ضمن مراتب الأدلة . قال الجويني : وقال أبو بكر بن داود الأصفهاني : أغفل الشافعي رحمه الله في المراتب الإجماع، وهو من أصول الشريعة فإن تكلف متكلف وزعم أن الإجماع يدل من حيث استند الخبر فاكتفى بذكر الأخبار، فهلا ذكر الإجماع أولا، واكتفى بذكره عن القياس لاستناده إليه، فالقياس مستند إلى الإجماع، وهو مستند إلى

، ن ، ص : 98 - 99 م،
Debate: Its correctness and preponderance over one's subjective opinion—he is not prevented by the fact that this opinion comes from his opponent from adopting it and retracting his own opinion. Therefore, every debater must always carry this intellectual flexibility in every scholarly dialogue that takes place between him and others who oppose him in opinion. Abu Bakr al-Sayrafi said: 'Acknowledge the truth when it becomes clear; if it does not become clear, then adhere to the proof.' Al-Baji said: 'When the truth becomes clear to him, he submits and yields to it, for the purpose of investigation is to hit the truth.' This intellectual flexibility is what elevates the status of the debater and shows his sincerity to the truth and knowledge, and makes his opponents respect him and listen to his speech. Al-Tufi said: 'Each of them should accept the argument from his companion, for it is nobler for his status and more helpful in realizing the truth and following the path of honesty.' Al-Shafi'i, may Allah be pleased with him, said: 'I have never debated anyone who accepted the argument from me except that he became great in my eyes, nor rejected it against me except that he fell in my eyes.' The issue: The researcher, who is naturally soft-hearted, when the truth appears to him, he acknowledges it and leaves what he was upon, and returning to the truth is an obligation. The loss of this flexibility and this readiness to accept the truth when it appears turns the discussion in the point of dispute into a sterile polemic intended only to record more displays of verbal muscle-flexing and dialectical one-upmanship that neither advance nor delay the subject, because the idea has been prepared in advance in a way that leaves no room for retraction regardless of contrary evidence, following subjective and social motives that have no connection to intellectual conviction based on proof and demonstration. Error: And the researcher who lacks this flexibility is not called a researcher in reality but is merely an obstinate person, and therefore he cannot exert the effort that would guide him to correctness and protect him from error. It indicates the absence of motives for research in him and the lack of readiness to accept whatever is claimed. So the one who does not acknowledge his deviation from correctness when it becomes clear to him, nor desist from error upon knowing its causes, is a person who cannot contribute to establishing truth and invalidating falsehood. Rather, he is a person who does not respond to the demands of reason, neither with himself nor with others, and consequently he cannot obtain conviction that enables him to persuade, but rather error, sophistry, and persistence in them occur from him. Therefore, it is necessary to develop the spirit of intellectual flexibility and readiness to change opinion in concepts or assents when evidence supports that, because everything without evidence has no place in research. Likewise, restricting oneself to a single angle of vision and following entrenched intellectual habits will turn the inflexible researcher's criteria for distinguishing error from correctness and truth from falsehood. The consequence of lacking intellectual flexibility is that it causes narrowness of horizon in the researcher, so he does not see what others see in a different horizon, and then his field of creativity narrows—creativity that expands with the breadth of vision and varies with the variety of angles of vision. This includes among the examples of intellectual flexibility among scholars of usul al-fiqh: al-Juwayni's acknowledgment of the validity of the criticism directed by Abu Bakr ibn Dawud al-Isfahani al-Zahiri against Imam al-Shafi'i, when he omitted mentioning consensus (ijma') among the ranks of evidence. Al-Juwayni said: 'Abu Bakr ibn Dawud al-Isfahani said: Al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, omitted consensus in the ranks, while it is one of the foundations of the Sharia. If someone were to strain and claim that consensus is indicated by virtue of being based on the report (khabar), and he sufficed with mentioning reports, then why did he not mention consensus first, and suffice with it instead of analogy (qiyas) because analogy is based on consensus, and consensus is based on...'
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage discusses the ethical and methodological principle of intellectual flexibility (al-murūnah al-fikriyyah) in debate (jadal). The author begins by stating that a debater should not be prevented from adopting a correct opinion simply because it comes from an opponent; rather, he should retract his own opinion if the truth becomes clear. This is supported by quotes from Abu Bakr al-Sayrafi, who advises to acknowledge the truth when it becomes clear and otherwise adhere to proof, and from al-Baji, who states that the purpose of investigation is to attain the truth. The author then explains that this flexibility elevates the debater's status, shows his sincerity, and earns respect from opponents. Al-Tufi is quoted urging mutual acceptance of arguments, and al-Shafi'i's famous statement is cited: that accepting an argument from him elevates the opponent in his eyes, while rejecting it lowers them. The passage then contrasts this with the loss of flexibility, which turns debate into a sterile polemic aimed at ego rather than truth. The researcher lacking flexibility is labeled obstinate (muʿānid) and incapable of contributing to truth. The author stresses the necessity of developing flexibility and readiness to change opinions in both concepts (tasawwurāt) and assents (taṣdīqāt) when evidence arises. Examples from usul al-fiqh are given: al-Juwayni's acceptance of Abu Bakr ibn Dawud's criticism of al-Shafi'i for omitting consensus in his ranking of evidence. This illustrates how even great scholars practiced intellectual flexibility by acknowledging valid criticism.

The Concept of Intellectual Flexibility
Argument for intellectual flexibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the necessity of intellectual flexibility in dialectical research and identify the signs of its absence.

Core Idea
True research demands readiness to change when evidence appears.

The author begins by asserting that a researcher who lacks intellectual flexibility is not a true researcher but an obstinate person. He then explains why this flexibility is essential for seeking truth and avoiding error. Let's analyze his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main thesis
Find the sentence where the author states that a researcher lacking flexibility is not a real researcher. This is the core claim of the paragraph.
Look for 'والباحث الذي يفتقد إلى هذه المرونة لا يسمى باحثا في الحقيقة'
2
Extract the consequences of inflexibility
List the negative outcomes the author attributes to a researcher without flexibility: inability to exert effort, absence of research motives, contribution to error, etc.
The passage lists several, so note the key phrases.
3
Analyze the analogy with perspective
The author compares limiting oneself to one perspective to following entrenched habits. How do these become false standards for distinguishing truth from falsehood?
Consider the phrase 'الاقتصار على الرؤية من زاوية واحدة'.
4
Distinguish flexibility from stubbornness
The author contrasts the flexible researcher with the obstinate one. What is the key difference in their response to evidence?
The flexible researcher changes opinion; the obstinate one does not.

فقط، وإنما تابعت الشيخ أبا محمد، وهو تابع الشيخ أبا حامد أي الغزالي)، وما ذكرته من ذلك هو معنى ما ذكراه جميعا، وإذا تأملته أنت عرفته، ولم أتبينه لهذا إلا الآن» (2) . فالأصوليون لا يترددون في الاعتراف بالحق عند ظهوره، حتى وإن كان مقتضاه مناقضًا تماما لمذهبهم الذي هم عليه في المسألة موضع الخلاف، من ذلك ما عرف به أكثر الأصوليين الحكم الشرعي بأنه: «خطاب الله . . حيث أورد عليه المعتزلة جملة من الاعتراضات وهذه الاعتراضات اعترف الطوفي بقوتها، مما جعله يعيد صياغة التعريف من جديد : قلت : فإذا قلنا : الحكم مقتضى خطاب الشارع، لم ترد علينا هذه الأسئلة، لأنا لا نقول : إن الحكم المعلل بالحوادث هو نفس كلام الله، بل هو مقتضى الله سبحانه وتعالى، وفرق بين الكلام ،ومقتضاه ، إذ الكلام إما معنى نفسي، أو قول دال، ومقتضى الكلام هو مدلول ذلك القول والمطلوب به». ثم قال معلقا على الأجوبة التي أجاب بها الأولون من أصحاب مذهبه على اعتراضات المعتزلة : وفيما ذكره الأولون من الجواب عن أسئلة المعتزلة؟ نوع تكلف، ولعلهم إذا حُوقِقُوا عليه ربما تعذر عليهم تمشيته»(3)
The researcher who lacks this flexibility is not called a researcher in truth but rather is nothing but an obstinate person. Therefore he cannot exert the effort sufficient to guide him to correctness and protect him from error, and it indicates the absence of motives for research in him, and readiness to accept everything he is called to. So he who does not acknowledge his deviation from correctness when it becomes clear to him, and does not desist upon knowing its causes, is a person who cannot contribute to establishing truth and invalidating falsehood; rather he is a person who does not respond to the demands of reason, neither with himself nor with others, and consequently he cannot attain conviction that enables him to persuade, but rather error, sophistry, and persistence in both occur from him. So it is necessary – therefore – to cultivate the spirit of intellectual flexibility and readiness to change opinion in conceptions or assents when evidence indicates that, because everything without evidence has no place in research... Just as limiting to one perspective and following entrenched intellectual habits will, for the inflexible researcher, transform into standards for distinguishing error from correctness and truth from falsehood. The state of lack of intellectual flexibility causes narrowness of horizon in the researcher, so he does not see what others see in another horizon, and then the field of creativity narrows for him... which expands with breadth of vision and diversifies with the diversity of the angle of vision. And this is among the examples of intellectual flexibility among scholars of usul al-fiqh: al-Juwayni's acknowledgment of the validity of the criticism directed by Abu Bakr ibn Dawud al-Isfahani al-Zahiri against Imam al-Shafi'i, when he omitted mentioning consensus among the ranks of evidence. Al-Juwayni said: Abu Bakr ibn Dawud al-Isfahani said: al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, omitted consensus in the ranks, and it is among the foundations of the Shari'a. If a pretender were to labor and claim that consensus indicates by virtue of being based on the report, and he sufficed with mentioning reports, then why did he not mention consensus first, and suffice with its mention over analogy because it is based on it? For analogy is based on consensus, and consensus is based on the report, and al-Shafi'i counted it. And if he had mentioned consensus, it would be more appropriate since it is higher than analogy, and analogy falls under the implications of consensus... And there is no repelling the question.' That is what al-Tufi recognized as truth in a matter among matters, and he had gone in it and followed someone else, where he returned to what became clear to him as truth. He says: My statement in al-Mukhtasar: 'or to the description of the prohibited only, with my statement after that: the prohibited is not this description but rather the described,' apparent contradiction: if the prohibited is the described, the prohibition does not refer only to its description. Rather I followed Sheikh Abu Muhammad, and he followed Sheikh Abu Hamid (i.e. al-Ghazali), and what I mentioned of that is the meaning of what they both mentioned. If you contemplate it, you will know it. I did not realize this until now.' So the usuliyyin do not hesitate to acknowledge truth when it appears, even if its requirement completely contradicts their school in the matter of disagreement. Among that is what most usuliyyin defined the legal ruling as: 'the speech of Allah...' where the Mu'tazila raised a number of objections, and al-Tufi acknowledged the strength of these objections, which made him reformulate the definition anew: I said: if we say: the ruling is the requirement of the Legislator's speech, these questions are not raised against us, because we do not say that the ruling predicated on events is itself the speech of Allah, but rather it is the requirement of Allah, exalted and majestic, and there is a difference between speech and its requirement, for speech is either an internal meaning or an indicative saying, and the requirement of speech is the indication of that saying and what is intended by it. Then he commented on the answers given by the earlier followers of his school to the Mu'tazila's objections: 'In what the earlier ones mentioned of the answer to the Mu'tazila's questions? There is some affectation, and perhaps if they were pressed on it, it might be difficult for them to make it consistent.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage is an exposition on the necessity of intellectual flexibility (المرونة الفكرية) for a researcher in dialectical debate. The author begins by stating that a researcher who lacks this flexibility is not a true researcher but an obstinate person. Such a person cannot exert the effort needed to reach correctness or avoid error, indicating a lack of sincere motivation for research and a readiness to accept any claim without scrutiny. The author then describes the consequences of inflexibility: an inability to acknowledge error, contribute to truth, or be convinced in a way that enables persuasion. Instead, such a person falls into error and sophistry. Therefore, the author asserts that one must cultivate a spirit of intellectual flexibility and readiness to change one's conceptions or assents when evidence demands it, because anything without evidence has no place in research. Moreover, limiting oneself to a single perspective and following entrenched intellectual habits can, for an inflexible researcher, turn into false standards for distinguishing truth from falsehood. The loss of flexibility narrows one's horizon, stifles creativity, and prevents seeing what others see from different vantage points. To illustrate this principle, the author provides two examples from the tradition of usul al-fiqh: First, al-Juwayni's acknowledgment of the criticism made by Abu Bakr ibn Dawud al-Isfahani against al-Shafi'i for omitting consensus (إجماع) from his ranking of evidence. Al-Juwayni reproduced the criticism, which argues that consensus is a higher source than analogy and that failing to mention it is a lapse. Second, al-Tufi's self-correction: he noticed a contradiction in his own statements in al-Mukhtar regarding the definition of the prohibited. He had claimed that the prohibition applies only to the description of the prohibited, but then said the prohibited is not the description but the described. He realizes the contradiction and admits that he merely followed earlier authorities without proper reflection. He then acknowledges the truth of the issue now. The author generalizes that the usuliyyin (scholars of usul al-fiqh) do not hesitate to acknowledge truth when it appears, even if it contradicts their own school. He cites al-Tufi's reformulation of the definition of a legal ruling (الحكم الشرعي) in response to Mu'tazili objections. Al-Tufi argued that the ruling is not Allah's speech itself but the requirement (مقتضى) of that speech, thus avoiding the objections. He criticizes the earlier answers of his school for being forced and difficult to maintain under scrutiny. Thus the passage emphasizes intellectual honesty and flexibility as essential qualities in dialectic.

Al-Juwayni's Acknowledgment of Criticism
Example of acknowledging criticism against al-Shafi'i

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the dialectical exchange between Abu Bakr ibn Dawud and al-Juwayni regarding the omission of consensus from al-Shafi'i's ranking of evidence.

Core Idea
Acknowledging valid criticism demonstrates intellectual flexibility, even against an authority.

The author provides the first example of intellectual flexibility: al-Juwayni's acknowledgment of Abu Bakr ibn Dawud's criticism that al-Shafi'i omitted consensus from his ranking of evidence. Let's break down this dialectical exchange.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector and the target
Find who is criticizing and whom they are criticizing. The objector is Abu Bakr ibn Dawud, and the target is Imam al-Shafi'i.
Look for 'أبو بكر بن داود الأصفهاني الظاهري إلى الإمام الشافعي'
2
Extract the criticism (mu'tarid)
What is the exact objection? Abu Bakr argues that al-Shafi'i omitted consensus (إجماع) from the ranks of evidence, which is problematic because consensus is a higher source than analogy (قياس).
The criticism says that al-Shafi'i should have mentioned consensus first and that analogy is based on consensus.
3
Understand al-Juwayni's acknowledgment (jawab)
Al-Juwayni does not defend al-Shafi'i but admits the criticism is valid. He even reproduces the objection. This is an act of intellectual flexibility.
The passage says 'اعتراف الجويني بصحة النقد'
4
Assess the dialectical outcome
In this exchange, the objector's point stands. The respondent (al-Juwayni) concedes, which is a winning move for the objector in terms of argument strength.
This shows that truth is prioritized over loyalty to an imam.

لديه، وعلى الاستعداد لتقبل كل ما يدعى إليه . فالذي لا يعترف بمجانبته للصواب عندما يتبين له ذلك، ولا يقلع عن عند معرفة أسبابه إنسان لا يمكنه أن يساهم في إحقاق الحق وفي إبطال الباطل، بل يكون إنسانًا لا يستجيب لمطالب العقل، لا مع نفسه، ولا مع غيره، وبالتالي لا يمكن أن يحصل له الاقتناع الذي يمكنه من الإقناع، بل يحصل منه الغلط والمغالطة والتمادي عليهما ) . فلا بد – إذا – من تنمية روح المرونة الفكرية والاستعداد لتغيير الرأي في التصورات أو التصديقات عندما يقوم الدليل على ذلك، لأن كل ما لا دليل عليه لا محل له في البحث .. كما أن الاقتصار على الرؤية من زاوية واحدة واتباع العادات الفكرية الراسخة من شأنهما أن يتحولا لدى الباحث غير المرن إلى مقاييس للتمييز بين الخطأ والصواب، والحق والباطل. شأن فقدان المرونة الفكرية، أن يتسبب في ضيق الأفق لدى الباحث، فلا ما يراه غيره في أفق آخر، وعندئذ يضيق لديه مجال الإبداع .. الذي يتسع باتساع الرؤية، ويتنوع بتنوع زاوية الرؤية»(2) . هذا وإن من نماذج المرونة الفكرية عند علماء أصول الفقه ؛ اعتراف الجويني بصحة النقد التي توجه به أبو بكر بن داود الأصفهاني الظاهري إلى الإمام الشافعي، وذلك حين أغفل ذكر الإجماع ضمن مراتب الأدلة . قال الجويني : وقال أبو بكر بن داود الأصفهاني : أغفل الشافعي رحمه الله في المراتب الإجماع، وهو من أصول الشريعة فإن تكلف متكلف وزعم أن الإجماع يدل من حيث استند الخبر فاكتفى بذكر الأخبار، فهلا ذكر الإجماع أولا، واكتفى بذكره عن القياس لاستناده إليه، فالقياس مستند إلى الإجماع، وهو مستند إلى الخبر، وقد عده الشافعي، ولو ذكر الإجماع لكان أقرب إذ هو أعلى من القياس، يندرج القياس تحت مضمنات الإجماع . . ولا دفع للسؤال» (1) . ذلك ما تبين للطوفي من حق في مسألة من المسائل، وقد كان ذهب فيها ومن إلى رأي اتبع فيه غيره، حيث رجع إلى ما تبين له أنه الحق، يقول: قولي في (المختصر: «أو إلى وصف المنهي عنه فقط ، مع قولي بعد ذلك : إن المنهي عنه ليس هذه الصفة بل الموصوف بها، ظاهر التناقض كان المنهي عنه الموصوف لم يكن النهي راجعا إلى وصفه فقط، وإنما تابعت الشيخ أبا محمد، وهو تابع الشيخ أبا حامد أي الغزالي)، وما ذكرته من ذلك هو معنى ما ذكراه جميعا، وإذا تأملته أنت عرفته، ولم أتبينه لهذا إلا الآن» (2) . فالأصوليون لا يترددون في الاعتراف بالحق عند ظهوره، حتى وإن كان مقتضاه مناقضًا تماما لمذهبهم الذي هم عليه في المسألة موضع الخلاف، من ذلك ما عرف به أكثر الأصوليين الحكم الشرعي بأنه: «خطاب الله . . حيث أورد عليه المعتزلة جملة من الاعتراضات وهذه الاعتراضات اعترف الطوفي بقوتها، مما جعله يعيد صياغة التعريف من جديد : قلت : فإذا قلنا : الحكم مقتضى خطاب الشارع، لم ترد علينا هذه الأسئلة، لأنا لا نقول : إن الحكم المعلل بالحوادث هو نفس كلام الله، بل هو مقتضى الله سبحانه وتعالى، وفرق بين الكلام ،ومقتضاه ، إذ الكلام إما معنى نفسي، أو قول دال، ومقتضى الكلام هو مدلول ذلك القول والمطلوب به». ثم قال معلقا على الأجوبة التي أجاب بها الأولون من أصحاب مذهبه على اعتراضات المعتزلة : وفيما ذكره الأولون من الجواب عن أسئلة المعتزلة؟ نوع تكلف، ولعلهم إذا حُوقِقُوا عليه ربما تعذر عليهم تمشيته»(3)
The researcher who lacks this flexibility is not called a researcher in truth but rather is nothing but an obstinate person. Therefore he cannot exert the effort sufficient to guide him to correctness and protect him from error, and it indicates the absence of motives for research in him, and readiness to accept everything he is called to. So he who does not acknowledge his deviation from correctness when it becomes clear to him, and does not desist upon knowing its causes, is a person who cannot contribute to establishing truth and invalidating falsehood; rather he is a person who does not respond to the demands of reason, neither with himself nor with others, and consequently he cannot attain conviction that enables him to persuade, but rather error, sophistry, and persistence in both occur from him. So it is necessary – therefore – to cultivate the spirit of intellectual flexibility and readiness to change opinion in conceptions or assents when evidence indicates that, because everything without evidence has no place in research... Just as limiting to one perspective and following entrenched intellectual habits will, for the inflexible researcher, transform into standards for distinguishing error from correctness and truth from falsehood. The state of lack of intellectual flexibility causes narrowness of horizon in the researcher, so he does not see what others see in another horizon, and then the field of creativity narrows for him... which expands with breadth of vision and diversifies with the diversity of the angle of vision. And this is among the examples of intellectual flexibility among scholars of usul al-fiqh: al-Juwayni's acknowledgment of the validity of the criticism directed by Abu Bakr ibn Dawud al-Isfahani al-Zahiri against Imam al-Shafi'i, when he omitted mentioning consensus among the ranks of evidence. Al-Juwayni said: Abu Bakr ibn Dawud al-Isfahani said: al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, omitted consensus in the ranks, and it is among the foundations of the Shari'a. If a pretender were to labor and claim that consensus indicates by virtue of being based on the report, and he sufficed with mentioning reports, then why did he not mention consensus first, and suffice with its mention over analogy because it is based on it? For analogy is based on consensus, and consensus is based on the report, and al-Shafi'i counted it. And if he had mentioned consensus, it would be more appropriate since it is higher than analogy, and analogy falls under the implications of consensus... And there is no repelling the question.' That is what al-Tufi recognized as truth in a matter among matters, and he had gone in it and followed someone else, where he returned to what became clear to him as truth. He says: My statement in al-Mukhtasar: 'or to the description of the prohibited only, with my statement after that: the prohibited is not this description but rather the described,' apparent contradiction: if the prohibited is the described, the prohibition does not refer only to its description. Rather I followed Sheikh Abu Muhammad, and he followed Sheikh Abu Hamid (i.e. al-Ghazali), and what I mentioned of that is the meaning of what they both mentioned. If you contemplate it, you will know it. I did not realize this until now.' So the usuliyyin do not hesitate to acknowledge truth when it appears, even if its requirement completely contradicts their school in the matter of disagreement. Among that is what most usuliyyin defined the legal ruling as: 'the speech of Allah...' where the Mu'tazila raised a number of objections, and al-Tufi acknowledged the strength of these objections, which made him reformulate the definition anew: I said: if we say: the ruling is the requirement of the Legislator's speech, these questions are not raised against us, because we do not say that the ruling predicated on events is itself the speech of Allah, but rather it is the requirement of Allah, exalted and majestic, and there is a difference between speech and its requirement, for speech is either an internal meaning or an indicative saying, and the requirement of speech is the indication of that saying and what is intended by it. Then he commented on the answers given by the earlier followers of his school to the Mu'tazila's objections: 'In what the earlier ones mentioned of the answer to the Mu'tazila's questions? There is some affectation, and perhaps if they were pressed on it, it might be difficult for them to make it consistent.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage is an exposition on the necessity of intellectual flexibility (المرونة الفكرية) for a researcher in dialectical debate. The author begins by stating that a researcher who lacks this flexibility is not a true researcher but an obstinate person. Such a person cannot exert the effort needed to reach correctness or avoid error, indicating a lack of sincere motivation for research and a readiness to accept any claim without scrutiny. The author then describes the consequences of inflexibility: an inability to acknowledge error, contribute to truth, or be convinced in a way that enables persuasion. Instead, such a person falls into error and sophistry. Therefore, the author asserts that one must cultivate a spirit of intellectual flexibility and readiness to change one's conceptions or assents when evidence demands it, because anything without evidence has no place in research. Moreover, limiting oneself to a single perspective and following entrenched intellectual habits can, for an inflexible researcher, turn into false standards for distinguishing truth from falsehood. The loss of flexibility narrows one's horizon, stifles creativity, and prevents seeing what others see from different vantage points. To illustrate this principle, the author provides two examples from the tradition of usul al-fiqh: First, al-Juwayni's acknowledgment of the criticism made by Abu Bakr ibn Dawud al-Isfahani against al-Shafi'i for omitting consensus (إجماع) from his ranking of evidence. Al-Juwayni reproduced the criticism, which argues that consensus is a higher source than analogy and that failing to mention it is a lapse. Second, al-Tufi's self-correction: he noticed a contradiction in his own statements in al-Mukhtar regarding the definition of the prohibited. He had claimed that the prohibition applies only to the description of the prohibited, but then said the prohibited is not the description but the described. He realizes the contradiction and admits that he merely followed earlier authorities without proper reflection. He then acknowledges the truth of the issue now. The author generalizes that the usuliyyin (scholars of usul al-fiqh) do not hesitate to acknowledge truth when it appears, even if it contradicts their own school. He cites al-Tufi's reformulation of the definition of a legal ruling (الحكم الشرعي) in response to Mu'tazili objections. Al-Tufi argued that the ruling is not Allah's speech itself but the requirement (مقتضى) of that speech, thus avoiding the objections. He criticizes the earlier answers of his school for being forced and difficult to maintain under scrutiny. Thus the passage emphasizes intellectual honesty and flexibility as essential qualities in dialectic.

الجدل

Checkpoint
What is the main reason Abu Bakr ibn Dawud criticizes al-Shafi'i's ranking of evidence?
A) Al-Shafi'i placed consensus above analogy.
B) Al-Shafi'i omitted consensus from the ranks.
C) Al-Shafi'i relied too much on reports.
D) Al-Shafi'i ignored analogy.

Key Takeaway
Intellectual flexibility includes acknowledging valid criticism against one's own school or authority.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In a current usul al-fiqh debate, a colleague argues that analogy is superior to consensus. Based on this lesson, what objection would you raise?
?
Practice a dialectical exchange: One person takes al-Shafi'i's position (only reports and analogy), the other objects using Abu Bakr ibn Dawud's argument. Then switch roles.

Dialectic -- Jadal
intermediate
5 min
Al-Tufi's Self-Correction on Prohibition
Example of self-correction by al-Tufi

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a logical contradiction in al-Tufi's earlier statements and explain how he corrected himself.

Core Idea
Self-correction is a hallmark of intellectual flexibility, even when it means admitting one blindly followed authority.

The second example of intellectual flexibility is al-Tufi's own self-correction regarding the definition of the prohibited. He realizes a contradiction in his previous statements and admits he merely followed earlier scholars without proper understanding. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contradiction
Al-Tufi states in al-Mukhtasar: 'or to the description of the prohibited only.' Then he says: 'the prohibited is not this description but the described.' These two statements are contradictory.
The contradiction is between limiting prohibition to the description and then saying the prohibition applies to the described object.
2
Trace the source of his earlier view
He admits he followed Sheikh Abu Muhammad, who followed al-Ghazali. He now realizes he did not fully understand the issue.
Look for 'إنما تابعت الشيخ أبا محمد، وهو تابع الشيخ أبا حامد'
3
Assess his correction
Al-Tufi does not defend his earlier statement but acknowledges the contradiction. He now sees the correct meaning.
He says: 'لم أتبينه لهذا إلا الآن' (I did not realize this until now).
4
Appreciate the flexibility
This is an example of intellectual flexibility because he openly admits error and corrects it, even though it means contradicting his own writing and the authorities he followed.
This aligns with the author's thesis about flexibility.

Commentary
This passage is an exposition on the necessity of intellectual flexibility (المرونة الفكرية) for a researcher in dialectical debate. The author begins by stating that a researcher who lacks this flexibility is not a true researcher but an obstinate person. Such a person cannot exert the effort needed to reach correctness or avoid error, indicating a lack of sincere motivation for research and a readiness to accept any claim without scrutiny. The author then describes the consequences of inflexibility: an inability to acknowledge error, contribute to truth, or be convinced in a way that enables persuasion. Instead, such a person falls into error and sophistry. Therefore, the author asserts that one must cultivate a spirit of intellectual flexibility and readiness to change one's conceptions or assents when evidence demands it, because anything without evidence has no place in research. Moreover, limiting oneself to a single perspective and following entrenched intellectual habits can, for an inflexible researcher, turn into false standards for distinguishing truth from falsehood. The loss of flexibility narrows one's horizon, stifles creativity, and prevents seeing what others see from different vantage points. To illustrate this principle, the author provides two examples from the tradition of usul al-fiqh: First, al-Juwayni's acknowledgment of the criticism made by Abu Bakr ibn Dawud al-Isfahani against al-Shafi'i for omitting consensus (إجماع) from his ranking of evidence. Al-Juwayni reproduced the criticism, which argues that consensus is a higher source than analogy and that failing to mention it is a lapse. Second, al-Tufi's self-correction: he noticed a contradiction in his own statements in al-Mukhtar regarding the definition of the prohibited. He had claimed that the prohibition applies only to the description of the prohibited, but then said the prohibited is not the description but the described. He realizes the contradiction and admits that he merely followed earlier authorities without proper reflection. He then acknowledges the truth of the issue now. The author generalizes that the usuliyyin (scholars of usul al-fiqh) do not hesitate to acknowledge truth when it appears, even if it contradicts their own school. He cites al-Tufi's reformulation of the definition of a legal ruling (الحكم الشرعي) in response to Mu'tazili objections. Al-Tufi argued that the ruling is not Allah's speech itself but the requirement (مقتضى) of that speech, thus avoiding the objections. He criticizes the earlier answers of his school for being forced and difficult to maintain under scrutiny. Thus the passage emphasizes intellectual honesty and flexibility as essential qualities in dialectic.

Checkpoint
Why did al-Tufi say his earlier statements were contradictory?
A) He claimed the prohibition applies to the description but then said it applies to the described.
B) He said the prohibition applies to the description only.
C) He followed al-Ghazali blindly.
D) He did not actually write those statements.

Key Takeaway
Intellectual flexibility includes the courage to admit one's own mistakes and correct them, even if it means contradicting prior writings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Write a short dialectical scenario where you, as a researcher, realize a contradiction in your earlier argument and correct yourself in a way that demonstrates intellectual flexibility.

Al-Tufi's Reformulation of the Legal Ruling Definition
Example of reformulating a definition to meet objections

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze al-Tufi's dialectical response to Mu'tazili objections by reformulating the definition of a legal ruling (الحكم الشرعي).

Core Idea
Flexibility can lead to redefining key concepts to avoid strong objections while preserving truth.

The final example shows al-Tufi responding to Mu'tazili objections against the common definition of a legal ruling as 'the speech of Allah'. He acknowledges the strength of the objections and reformulates the definition to maintain the essence while avoiding the critique. Let's examine his dialectical move.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original definition
Most usuliyyin defined the legal ruling as 'خطاب الله' (the speech of Allah). This was attacked by the Mu'tazila.
Look for 'عرف به أكثر الأصوليين الحكم الشرعي بأنه: خطاب الله'
2
Understand the Mu'tazili objection
The Mu'tazila raised objections. The passage does not detail them, but they likely concern the relationship between the ruling and Allah's speech.
Al-Tufi later acknowledges these objections as strong.
3
Analyze al-Tufi's reformulation
He proposes: 'الحكم مقتضى خطاب الشارع' (the ruling is the requirement of the Legislator's speech). This distinguishes between Allah's speech and its implication.
He says there is a difference between speech (الكلام) and its requirement (مقتضاه).
4
Evaluate the dialectical outcome
Al-Tufi considers this new definition immune to the objections. He also criticizes earlier responses as forced and difficult to maintain.
This demonstrates flexibility: instead of stubbornly defending the old definition, he revises it.

لديه، وعلى الاستعداد لتقبل كل ما يدعى إليه . فالذي لا يعترف بمجانبته للصواب عندما يتبين له ذلك، ولا يقلع عن عند معرفة أسبابه إنسان لا يمكنه أن يساهم في إحقاق الحق وفي إبطال الباطل، بل يكون إنسانًا لا مجال الإبداع .. الذي يتسع باتساع الرؤية، ويتنوع بتنوع زاوية الرؤية»(2) . هذا وإن من نماذج المرونة الفكرية عند علماء أصول الفقه ؛ اعتراف الجويني بصحة النقد التي توجه به أبو بكر بن داود الأصفهاني الظاهري إلى الإمام الشافعي، وذلك حين أغفل ذكر الإجماع ضمن مراتب الأدلة . قال الجويني : وقال أبو بكر بن داود الأصفهاني : أغفل الشافعي رحمه الله في المراتب الإجماع، وهو من أصول الشريعة فإن تكلف متكلف وزعم أن الإجماع يدل من حيث استند الخبر فاكتفى بذكر الأخبار، فهلا ذكر الإجماع أولا، واكتفى بذكره عن القياس لاستناده إليه، فالقياس مستند إلى الإجماع، وهو مستند إلى الخبر، وقد عده الشافعي، ولو ذكر الإجماع لكان أقرب إذ هو أعلى من القياس، يندرج القياس تحت مضمنات الإجماع . . ولا دفع للسؤال» (1) . ذلك ما تبين للطوفي من حق في مسألة من المسائل، وقد كان ذهب فيها ومن إلى رأي اتبع فيه غيره، حيث رجع إلى ما تبين له أنه الحق، يقول: قولي في (المختصر: «أو إلى وصف المنهي عنه فقط ، مع قولي بعد ذلك : إن المنهي عنه ليس هذه الصفة بل الموصوف بها، ظاهر التناقض كان المنهي عنه الموصوف لم يكن النهي راجعا إلى وصفه فقط، وإنما تابعت الشيخ أبا محمد، وهو تابع الشيخ أبا حامد أي الغزالي)، وما ذكرته من ذلك هو معنى ما ذكراه جميعا، وإذا تأملته أنت عرفته، ولم أتبينه لهذا إلا الآن» (2) . فالأصوليون لا يترددون في الاعتراف بالحق عند ظهوره، حتى وإن كان مقتضاه مناقضًا تماما لمذهبهم الذي هم عليه في المسألة موضع الخلاف، من ذلك ما عرف به أكثر الأصوليين الحكم الشرعي بأنه: «خطاب الله . . حيث أورد عليه المعتزلة جملة من الاعتراضات وهذه الاعتراضات اعترف الطوفي بقوتها، مما جعله يعيد صياغة التعريف من جديد : قلت : فإذا قلنا : الحكم مقتضى خطاب الشارع، لم ترد علينا هذه الأسئلة، لأنا لا نقول : إن الحكم المعلل بالحوادث هو نفس كلام الله، بل هو مقتضى الله سبحانه وتعالى، وفرق بين الكلام ،ومقتضاه ، إذ الكلام إما معنى نفسي، أو قول دال، ومقتضى الكلام هو مدلول ذلك القول والمطلوب به». ثم قال معلقا على الأجوبة التي أجاب بها الأولون من أصحاب مذهبه على اعتراضات المعتزلة : وفيما ذكره الأولون من الجواب عن أسئلة المعتزلة؟ نوع تكلف، ولعلهم إذا حُوقِقُوا عليه ربما تعذر عليهم تمشيته»(3)
The researcher who lacks this flexibility is not called a researcher in truth but rather is nothing but an obstinate person. Therefore he cannot exert the effort sufficient to guide him to correctness and protect him from error, and it indicates the absence of motives for research in him, and readiness to accept everything he is called to. So he who does not acknowledge his deviation from correctness when it becomes clear to him, and does not desist upon knowing its causes, is a person who cannot contribute to establishing truth and invalidating falsehood; rather he is a person who does not respond to the demands of reason, neither with himself nor with others, and consequently he cannot attain conviction that enables him to persuade, but rather error, sophistry, and persistence in both occur from him. So it is necessary – therefore – to cultivate the spirit of intellectual flexibility and readiness to change opinion in conceptions or assents when evidence indicates that, because everything without evidence has no place in research... Just as limiting to one perspective and following entrenched intellectual habits will, for the inflexible researcher, transform into standards for distinguishing error from correctness and truth from falsehood. The state of lack of intellectual flexibility causes narrowness of horizon in the researcher, so he does not see what others see in another horizon, and then the field of creativity narrows for him... which expands with breadth of vision and diversifies with the diversity of the angle of vision. And this is among the examples of intellectual flexibility among scholars of usul al-fiqh: al-Juwayni's acknowledgment of the validity of the criticism directed by Abu Bakr ibn Dawud al-Isfahani al-Zahiri against Imam al-Shafi'i, when he omitted mentioning consensus among the ranks of evidence. Al-Juwayni said: Abu Bakr ibn Dawud al-Isfahani said: al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, omitted consensus in the ranks, and it is among the foundations of the Shari'a. If a pretender were to labor and claim that consensus indicates by virtue of being based on the report, and he sufficed with mentioning reports, then why did he not mention consensus first, and suffice with its mention over analogy because it is based on it? For analogy is based on consensus, and consensus is based on the report, and al-Shafi'i counted it. And if he had mentioned consensus, it would be more appropriate since it is higher than analogy, and analogy falls under the implications of consensus... And there is no repelling the question.' That is what al-Tufi recognized as truth in a matter among matters, and he had gone in it and followed someone else, where he returned to what became clear to him as truth. He says: My statement in al-Mukhtasar: 'or to the description of the prohibited only, with my statement after that: the prohibited is not this description but rather the described,' apparent contradiction: if the prohibited is the described, the prohibition does not refer only to its description. Rather I followed Sheikh Abu Muhammad, and he followed Sheikh Abu Hamid (i.e. al-Ghazali), and what I mentioned of that is the meaning of what they both mentioned. If you contemplate it, you will know it. I did not realize this until now.' So the usuliyyin do not hesitate to acknowledge truth when it appears, even if its requirement completely contradicts their school in the matter of disagreement. Among that is what most usuliyyin defined the legal ruling as: 'the speech of Allah...' where the Mu'tazila raised a number of objections, and al-Tufi acknowledged the strength of these objections, which made him reformulate the definition anew: I said: if we say: the ruling is the requirement of the Legislator's speech, these questions are not raised against us, because we do not say that the ruling predicated on events is itself the speech of Allah, but rather it is the requirement of Allah, exalted and majestic, and there is a difference between speech and its requirement, for speech is either an internal meaning or an indicative saying, and the requirement of speech is the indication of that saying and what is intended by it. Then he commented on the answers given by the earlier followers of his school to the Mu'tazila's objections: 'In what the earlier ones mentioned of the answer to the Mu'tazila's questions? There is some affectation, and perhaps if they were pressed on it, it might be difficult for them to make it consistent.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage is an exposition on the necessity of intellectual flexibility (المرونة الفكرية) for a researcher in dialectical debate. The author begins by stating that a researcher who lacks this flexibility is not a true researcher but an obstinate person. Such a person cannot exert the effort needed to reach correctness or avoid error, indicating a lack of sincere motivation for research and a readiness to accept any claim without scrutiny. The author then describes the consequences of inflexibility: an inability to acknowledge error, contribute to truth, or be convinced in a way that enables persuasion. Instead, such a person falls into error and sophistry. Therefore, the author asserts that one must cultivate a spirit of intellectual flexibility and readiness to change one's conceptions or assents when evidence demands it, because anything without evidence has no place in research. Moreover, limiting oneself to a single perspective and following entrenched intellectual habits can, for an inflexible researcher, turn into false standards for distinguishing truth from falsehood. The loss of flexibility narrows one's horizon, stifles creativity, and prevents seeing what others see from different vantage points. To illustrate this principle, the author provides two examples from the tradition of usul al-fiqh: First, al-Juwayni's acknowledgment of the criticism made by Abu Bakr ibn Dawud al-Isfahani against al-Shafi'i for omitting consensus (إجماع) from his ranking of evidence. Al-Juwayni reproduced the criticism, which argues that consensus is a higher source than analogy and that failing to mention it is a lapse. Second, al-Tufi's self-correction: he noticed a contradiction in his own statements in al-Mukhtar regarding the definition of the prohibited. He had claimed that the prohibition applies only to the description of the prohibited, but then said the prohibited is not the description but the described. He realizes the contradiction and admits that he merely followed earlier authorities without proper reflection. He then acknowledges the truth of the issue now. The author generalizes that the usuliyyin (scholars of usul al-fiqh) do not hesitate to acknowledge truth when it appears, even if it contradicts their own school. He cites al-Tufi's reformulation of the definition of a legal ruling (الحكم الشرعي) in response to Mu'tazili objections. Al-Tufi argued that the ruling is not Allah's speech itself but the requirement (مقتضى) of that speech, thus avoiding the objections. He criticizes the earlier answers of his school for being forced and difficult to maintain under scrutiny. Thus the passage emphasizes intellectual honesty and flexibility as essential qualities in dialectic.

Key Terms
المرونة الفكرية
الجدل
الإجماع
مقتضى الكلام
