The Necessity of Rank Distinction in Jadal
Author explains the necessity of tailoring debate style to the opponent's level.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the principle of distinguishing opponent ranks in jadal and explain its benefits.

Core Idea
Debate etiquette demands tailoring your approach to your opponent's rank.

This passage from a classical jadal manual explains why knowing your opponent's rank is crucial for productive debate. Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni and Ibn Aqil lay out the foundational principle: treat each debate opponent according to their station.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
What is the author's central assertion about jadal? The passage begins with 'Therefore, it was necessary for whoever undertakes jadal to distinguish between the ranks of those he debates.'
2
Find the supporting authorities
Which scholars are quoted to back this claim? Al-Juwayni and Ibn Aqil are cited. Al-Juwayni emphasizes preserving status and addressing each opponent by his degree. Ibn Aqil categorizes opponents into three ranks.
3
Extract the three categories
According to Ibn Aqil, what are the three ranks? Equal (nazīr), higher (a'lā), and lower (adwan).
These terms are repeated in the Arabic: طبقة, أعلى, أدون.
4
Understand the consequence of ignoring ranks
What does the author warn will happen if you fail to distinguish? The author says that when distinction is lost, jadal leads to hatred, enmity, and stubbornness, destroying the benefit of debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لذلك كان لابد لمن يتصدى للجدل أن يميز بين مراتب من يجادلهم في المسائل العلمية . قال إمام الحرمين الجويني: عليك بالمحافظة على قدرك وقدر خصمك وإنزال كل واحد - في وجه كلامك معه - درجته ، ومنزلته، فتميز بين النظير وبين الأستاذ المسترشد، وبين ومن يصلح لك . ولا تناظر النظير مناظرة المبتدئ المسترشد . . ولا تناظر أستاذينك مناظرة الأكفاء والنظراء، بل تناظر كلا حقه، وتحفظ كلا على رتبته» (1) . ومراعاة مراتب من يجادلهم الباحث تجعله قادرًا على المحافظة على موقف النقاش العلمي الهادئ وعدم الخروج منه إلى المعاندة والمكابرة وما إلى ذلك من مواقف صاخبة لا تقود إلى الحق ولا تحافظ على العلاقات الطيبة بين المتجادلين أما عند فقدان هذا التمييز، فإننا نجد الجدل يقود مباشرة إلى ظهور أسباب البغضاء والشحناء وقيام مواقف العناد والتعنت فيذهب ذلك بفائدة الجدل ويقود إلى أوخم العواقب. قال ابن عقيل : اعلم أنه لا يخلو الخصم في الجدل من أن يكون في طبقة خصمه، أو أعلى أو أدون. فإن كان في طبقته كان قوله له الحق في هذا دون كذا، من قبل كيت وكيت، : ولأجل كذا، وعلى الآخر أن يتحرى له الموازنة في الخطاب، فذلك أسلم للقلوب، وأنقى لشغلها عن ترتيب النظر، فإن التطفيف في الخطاب يُعمي القلب عن فهم السؤال والجواب . وإن كان أعلى منه فليتحرَّ ويجتنب القول له : هذا خطأ، أو غلط، أو ليس كما تقول، بل يكون قوله له أرأيت إن قال قائل : يلزم على ما ذكرت كذا، وإن اعترض معترض على ما ذكرت بكذا؟ . . فإن نفوس الكرام الرؤساء المتقدمين تأبى خشونة الكلام، إذ لا عادة لهم بذلك، وإذا نفرت النفوس، عميت القلوب، وجمدت الخواطر وانسدت أبواب الفرائد، فحرم الكل الفوائد، بسفه السفيه وتقصير الجاهل في حقوق الصدور . . وأما الأدون فيكلم بكلام اللطف والتفهيم إلا أنه يجوز أن يُقال له إذا أتى بالخطأ هذا خطأ، وهذا غلط من قبل كذا ليذوق مرارة سلوك الخطأ، فيجتنبه، وحلاوة الصواب فيتبعه ورياضة هذه واجبة على العلماء وتركه سدى مضرة له، فإن عود الإكرام الذي يستحقه الأعلى ,طبقة أخلد إلى خطئه، ولم يزغه عن الغلط وازع ومقام التعليم والتأديب تارة بالعنف، وتارة باللطف، وسلوك أحدهما يفوت فائدة الآخر» (1)
Therefore, it was necessary for whoever undertakes jadal to distinguish between the ranks of those he debates in scientific matters. Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni said: You must preserve your status and your opponent's status, and place each one - in the manner of your speech with him - in his degree and station, so you distinguish between the peer and the teacher who seeks guidance, and between one who is suitable for you. Do not debate your peer as one debates a novice seeking guidance, and do not debate your two teachers as equals and peers, but debate each with his right, and preserve each in his rank. (1) And taking into account the ranks of those the researcher debates makes him able to preserve the position of calm scientific discussion and not depart from it to obstinacy and intransigence and the like of turbulent stances that do not lead to truth and do not preserve good relations between the debaters. But when this distinction is lost, we find that jadal directly leads to the appearance of causes of hatred and enmity and the establishment of positions of stubbornness and obstinacy, and that eliminates the benefit of jadal and leads to the worst consequences. Ibn Aqil said: Know that the opponent in jadal is never free from being either in the same class as his opponent, or higher, or lower. If he is in his class, he says to him 'The truth in this is such and such, because of such and such, and for this reason,' and the other must aim for balance in address, for that is safer for hearts and purer for clearing them for ordering consideration, for niggardliness in address blinds the heart from understanding question and answer. And if he is higher than him, let him be cautious and avoid saying to him 'This is an error, or a mistake, or not as you say,' but rather he should say to him: 'What do you think if someone said: Such and such follows from what you mentioned, and if someone objected to what you mentioned with such and such?' ... For the souls of noble leaders and chiefs abhor roughness of speech, since they have no custom for it, and when souls are alienated, hearts are blinded, thoughts become stagnant, and the doors of benefits are closed, so all are deprived of benefits due to the foolishness of the foolish and the neglect of the ignorant regarding the rights of the prominent. And as for the lower, he is addressed with speech of kindness and instruction, except that it is permissible to say to him when he comes with an error: 'This is an error, and this is a mistake because of such and such,' so that he tastes the bitterness of pursuing error and avoids it, and the sweetness of correctness and follows it. And training him is obligatory upon the scholars, and leaving him unchecked is harmful to him, for if the honor which the higher class deserves becomes customary, he would persist in his error, and no deterrent would turn him from falsehood. And the position of teaching and discipline is sometimes by violence, and sometimes by kindness, and adopting either one misses the benefit of the other. (1)
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical instruction on the proper conduct of debate (jadal). It begins by stating that anyone engaging in jadal must distinguish the ranks of those he debates. The author then quotes Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni, who emphasizes preserving both one's own status and the opponent's status, and addressing each according to his level — whether peer, teacher, or subordinate. The author then explains the benefit of this: it maintains a calm scholarly atmosphere and prevents animosity, whereas losing this distinction leads to hatred and loss of benefit. Then he quotes Ibn Aqil, who divides opponents into three categories: equal, higher, and lower. For an equal, balanced speech is safest. For a higher opponent, one should avoid direct criticism and instead phrase objections hypothetically, because noble souls dislike harshness. For a lower opponent, one may directly point out errors but with kindness and instruction, as training is obligatory. The passage concludes with a warning that using only force or only kindness in teaching misses the benefit.

Key Terms
نظير
أستاذ مسترشد
أدون
طبقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In jadal, the debater must classify the opponent as equal, superior, or inferior, and adjust his speech accordingly.

Addressing an Equal Opponent in Jadal
Author specifies the etiquette of debating a peer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the proper way to address an equal opponent in jadal and explain why balanced speech is important.

Core Idea
With a peer, balanced speech preserves hearts and clears the mind for reasoning.

Ibn Aqil provides specific guidance for debating an equal: use balanced, straightforward language. This avoids alienating the opponent and keeps the focus on logical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Locate the description of an equal opponent
Find the sentence starting with 'فإن كان في طبقته' (If he is in his class). This defines the equal opponent scenario.
2
Identify the recommended speech pattern
Ibn Aqil says: he says to him 'The truth in this is such and such, because of such and such, and for this reason.' The key is direct, balanced explanation.
Note the phrase 'الموازنة في الخطاب' (balance in address).
3
Understand why balanced speech is important
Why does Ibn Aqil say this approach is safest? 'For that is safer for hearts and purer for clearing them for ordering consideration.' Imbalance blinds the heart.
Niggardliness in address (التطفيف في الخطاب) is warned against.

لذلك كان لابد لمن يتصدى للجدل أن يميز بين مراتب من يجادلهم في المسائل العلمية . قال إمام الحرمين الجويني: عليك بالمحافظة على قدرك وقدر خصمك وإنزال كل واحد - في وجه كلامك معه - درجته ، ومنزلته، فتميز بين النظير وبين الأستاذ المسترشد، وبين ومن يصلح لك . ولا تناظر النظير مناظرة المبتدئ المسترشد . . ولا تناظر أستاذينك مناظرة الأكفاء والنظراء، بل تناظر كلا حقه، وتحفظ كلا على رتبته» (1) . ومراعاة مراتب من يجادلهم الباحث تجعله قادرًا على المحافظة على موقف النقاش العلمي الهادئ وعدم الخروج منه إلى المعاندة والمكابرة وما إلى ذلك من مواقف صاخبة لا تقود إلى الحق ولا تحافظ على العلاقات الطيبة بين المتجادلين أما عند فقدان هذا التمييز، فإننا نجد الجدل يقود مباشرة إلى ظهور أسباب البغضاء والشحناء وقيام مواقف العناد والتعنت فيذهب ذلك بفائدة الجدل ويقود إلى أوخم العواقب. قال ابن عقيل : اعلم أنه لا يخلو الخصم في الجدل من أن يكون في طبقة خصمه، أو أعلى أو أدون. فإن كان في طبقته كان قوله له الحق في هذا دون كذا، من قبل كيت وكيت، : ولأجل كذا، وعلى الآخر أن يتحرى له الموازنة في الخطاب، فذلك أسلم للقلوب، وأنقى لشغلها عن ترتيب النظر، فإن التطفيف في الخطاب يُعمي القلب عن فهم السؤال والجواب . وإن كان أعلى منه فليتحرَّ ويجتنب القول له : هذا خطأ، أو غلط، أو ليس كما تقول، بل يكون قوله له أرأيت إن قال قائل : يلزم على ما ذكرت كذا، وإن اعترض معترض على ما ذكرت بكذا؟ . . فإن نفوس الكرام الرؤساء المتقدمين تأبى خشونة الكلام، إذ لا عادة لهم بذلك، وإذا نفرت النفوس، عميت القلوب، وجمدت الخواطر وانسدت أبواب الفرائد، فحرم الكل الفوائد، بسفه السفيه وتقصير الجاهل في حقوق الصدور . . وأما الأدون فيكلم بكلام اللطف والتفهيم إلا أنه يجوز أن يُقال له إذا أتى بالخطأ هذا خطأ، وهذا غلط من قبل كذا ليذوق مرارة سلوك الخطأ، فيجتنبه، وحلاوة الصواب فيتبعه ورياضة هذه واجبة على العلماء وتركه سدى مضرة له، فإن عود الإكرام الذي يستحقه الأعلى ,طبقة أخلد إلى خطئه، ولم يزغه عن الغلط وازع ومقام التعليم والتأديب تارة بالعنف، وتارة باللطف، وسلوك أحدهما يفوت فائدة الآخر» (1)
Therefore, it was necessary for whoever undertakes jadal to distinguish between the ranks of those he debates in scientific matters. Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni said: You must preserve your status and your opponent's status, and place each one - in the manner of your speech with him - in his degree and station, so you distinguish between the peer and the teacher who seeks guidance, and between one who is suitable for you. Do not debate your peer as one debates a novice seeking guidance, and do not debate your two teachers as equals and peers, but debate each with his right, and preserve each in his rank. (1) And taking into account the ranks of those the researcher debates makes him able to preserve the position of calm scientific discussion and not depart from it to obstinacy and intransigence and the like of turbulent stances that do not lead to truth and do not preserve good relations between the debaters. But when this distinction is lost, we find that jadal directly leads to the appearance of causes of hatred and enmity and the establishment of positions of stubbornness and obstinacy, and that eliminates the benefit of jadal and leads to the worst consequences. Ibn Aqil said: Know that the opponent in jadal is never free from being either in the same class as his opponent, or higher, or lower. If he is in his class, he says to him 'The truth in this is such and such, because of such and such, and for this reason,' and the other must aim for balance in address, for that is safer for hearts and purer for clearing them for ordering consideration, for niggardliness in address blinds the heart from understanding question and answer. And if he is higher than him, let him be cautious and avoid saying to him 'This is an error, or a mistake, or not as you say,' but rather he should say to him: 'What do you think if someone said: Such and such follows from what you mentioned, and if someone objected to what you mentioned with such and such?' ... For the souls of noble leaders and chiefs abhor roughness of speech, since they have no custom for it, and when souls are alienated, hearts are blinded, thoughts become stagnant, and the doors of benefits are closed, so all are deprived of benefits due to the foolishness of the foolish and the neglect of the ignorant regarding the rights of the prominent. And as for the lower, he is addressed with speech of kindness and instruction, except that it is permissible to say to him when he comes with an error: 'This is an error, and this is a mistake because of such and such,' so that he tastes the bitterness of pursuing error and avoids it, and the sweetness of correctness and follows it. And training him is obligatory upon the scholars, and leaving him unchecked is harmful to him, for if the honor which the higher class deserves becomes customary, he would persist in his error, and no deterrent would turn him from falsehood. And the position of teaching and discipline is sometimes by violence, and sometimes by kindness, and adopting either one misses the benefit of the other. (1)
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical instruction on the proper conduct of debate (jadal). It begins by stating that anyone engaging in jadal must distinguish the ranks of those he debates. The author then quotes Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni, who emphasizes preserving both one's own status and the opponent's status, and addressing each according to his level — whether peer, teacher, or subordinate. The author then explains the benefit of this: it maintains a calm scholarly atmosphere and prevents animosity, whereas losing this distinction leads to hatred and loss of benefit. Then he quotes Ibn Aqil, who divides opponents into three categories: equal, higher, and lower. For an equal, balanced speech is safest. For a higher opponent, one should avoid direct criticism and instead phrase objections hypothetically, because noble souls dislike harshness. For a lower opponent, one may directly point out errors but with kindness and instruction, as training is obligatory. The passage concludes with a warning that using only force or only kindness in teaching misses the benefit.

نظير
طبقة
تطفيف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does Ibn Aqil say about 'niggardliness in address' (التطفيف في الخطاب)?
A) It is recommended to speed up the debate
B) It blinds the heart from understanding question and answer
C) It is only forbidden with higher opponents
D) It shows strength in argument

Key Takeaway
When debating a peer, use balanced, respectful speech to facilitate clear thinking.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a sentence that would be appropriate for addressing an equal opponent in a debate about the ruling of a new financial transaction, following Ibn Aqil's advice. (Example: 'The evidence indicates that this transaction is permissible because it is analogous to sale, and for this reason...')
?
Why does Ibn Aqil say balance in address is 'safer for hearts'? It preserves mutual respect and prevents emotional reactions that block reason.

Addressing a Superior or Inferior Opponent in Jadal
Author specifies the etiquette of debating a superior or inferior.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate the etiquette of addressing a superior opponent from addressing an inferior opponent in jadal.

Core Idea
Speak to superiors humbly and indirectly; correct inferiors gently but firmly.

Ibn Aqil distinguishes between higher and lower opponents. For a superior, avoid direct criticism; for an inferior, you may correct directly but with kindness. Each serves a different pedagogical purpose.

Guided Path
1
Find the guidance for a higher opponent
Locate the sentence starting with 'وإن كان أعلى منه' (And if he is higher than him). Read what Ibn Aqil says about avoiding direct criticism.
2
Identify the recommended phrasing for a superior
Instead of saying 'This is an error,' use hypothetical questions: 'What do you think if someone said...?' This preserves the superior's dignity.
The reason given: 'souls of noble leaders abhor roughness of speech.'
3
Find the guidance for a lower opponent
Now find 'وأما الأدون' (And as for the lower). Note the recommended approach: speech of kindness and instruction, but direct correction is permissible.
4
Understand the purpose of direct correction for the lower
Ibn Aqil says the lower opponent should taste the bitterness of error to avoid it and the sweetness of correctness to follow it. Training is obligatory.
If left unchecked, he will persist in error.

Balancing Truth and Courtesy in Debate
Author emphasizes that humility does not mean tolerating falsehood; one must correct error gently.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principles of balancing truth and courtesy in a jadal exchange, identifying when to correct an opponent's error and when to desist.

Core Idea
Defending truth does not require harshness; gentleness wins hearts.

This passage from a jadal manual addresses a common misconception: that being polite means accepting falsehood. Instead, it teaches that the debater must be a firm defender of truth, but also gentle in correction. We will explore the specific guidelines from al-Juwayni and al-Jawzi on when and how to correct an opponent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core principle
The author states that humility does not mean tolerating falsehood. Find the Arabic sentence that expresses this: 'لا يعني تواضع الباحث المجادل أن يتهاون في شأن الحق فيتسامح في تجاوزه وتجاهله'.
2
Analyze al-Juwayni's warning
Extract the condition for tolerating the opponent: only when it does not harm the truth. Al-Juwayni says: 'Do not tolerate the opponent except in a place where you know with certainty that toleration does not harm you.'
3
Examine al-Jawzi's method
Al-Jawzi gives two scenarios: if the opponent slips, stop him without shaming; if he persists, desist unless the error might become settled among listeners, in which case correct it gently.
4
Apply to a real debate scenario
Imagine a debate over the ruling of a new financial transaction. The opponent claims it is permissible based on a weak analogy. Determine which guideline applies and how to respond firmly yet gently.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
5 - عدم التسامح مع الخصم فيما يتعلق بالحق : لا يعني تواضع الباحث المجادل أن يتهاون في شأن الحق فيتسامح في تجاوزه وتجاهله، بل ينبغي عليه أن يقف في وجه كل محاولة لتجاوز الحق أو الطمس عليه دون أن يحجزه هذا عن التسامح مع الخصم في أي موقف يبدر منه ولا يكون فيه تجاوز للحق أو تطاول عليه . وفي هذا قال الجويني: لا تسامح الخصم إلا في موضع يعلم يقينا أن المسامحة فيه لا تضرك ، لأنه طالما قيل : المسامحة في المناظرة شؤم»(2) فموقف المجادل هو موقف المدافع عن الحق المنافح عن حماه بكل قوة وجهد، لكن هذا لا أن يعني يهين خصمه أو يخجله، بل يتحرى الوسيلة الهادئة المتوددة في التنبيه إلى الخطأ والكشف عن وجه الصواب. قال أبو محمد الجوزي: إن زل خصمه فليوقفه على زلله، غير مخجل له بالتشنيع عليه، فإن أصر أمسك ، إلا أن يكون ذلك الزلل مما يحاذر استقراره عند السامعين، فينبههم على الصواب فيه بألطف الوجوه، جمعا بين المصلحتين (3) ثم إن الإنصاف يقتضي من الباحث إذا ما زل خصمه في مسألة أن لا يجعل من
5 - Not tolerating the opponent regarding the truth: The humility of the researcher-debater does not mean that he is lenient in the matter of truth by tolerating its transgression and ignorance. Rather, he must stand against every attempt to transgress the truth or obscure it, without this preventing him from tolerating the opponent in any attitude that emanates from him that does not involve transgression against the truth or arrogance towards it. And in this regard, al-Juwayni said: Do not tolerate the opponent except in a place where you know with certainty that toleration does not harm you, because it has long been said: 'toleration in debate is ill-omened.' (2) So the position of the debater is the position of the defender of truth, the one who protects its preserve with all strength and effort. But this does not mean that he insults his opponent or embarrasses him. Rather, he seeks the gentle and affectionate means in alerting to error and revealing the face of correctness. Abu Muhammad al-Jawzi said: If his opponent slips, let him stop him from his slip without shaming him by rebuking. If he persists, he should desist, unless that slip is such that its settlement among the listeners is feared, in which case he alerts them to the correctness of it in the gentlest ways, combining the two interests. (3) Then fairness requires of the researcher that when his opponent slips in a matter, that he does not make from...
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical instruction on balancing firmness in truth with courtesy towards the opponent. It begins by stating that humility does not mean tolerating falsehood; one must oppose any transgression of truth, but without letting that prevent tolerance for the opponent's non-transgressive attitudes. The author then quotes al-Juwayni who warns against tolerating the opponent where it harms the truth, citing the maxim that tolerance in debate is ill-omened. The author explains that the debater is a defender of truth, yet this does not allow insult or embarrassment. Then al-Jawzi is quoted on how to correct an opponent's error: stop him without shaming, but if he persists, desist unless the error might become settled among listeners, in which case correct it gently. The passage ends with an incomplete sentence about fairness requiring that the researcher not make something (likely not make the slip a habit).

Key Terms

المنافح عن الحق

According to al-Juwayni, when should one tolerate the opponent in debate?
A) When the opponent is of higher rank

C) Always, out of politeness
D) Never, under any circumstances

Key Takeaway
In jadal, one must neither sacrifice truth for politeness nor sacrifice politeness for truth; the balanced approach is to correct error gently and only tolerate when truth is unharmed.

Balancing Truth and Fairness in Debate
Author discusses not tolerating falsehood in debate while maintaining humility, citing Juwayni, Ibn al-Jawzi, Ibn Aqil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between permissible tolerance and harmful leniency in debate, and apply Ibn 'Aqil's principle of evaluating each argument independently.

Core Idea
Defend truth firmly but fairly; one error does not invalidate all views.

Islamic debate ethics require both courage in defending truth and justice in evaluating opponents. This lesson examines how classical scholars like al-Juwayni, Ibn al-Jawzi, and Ibn 'Aqil balanced these demands, providing a framework for principled dialectical engagement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core tension
The author contrasts humility with tolerance of falsehood. What is the key distinction? Humility does not mean compromising on truth; it means treating the opponent with respect while firmly opposing falsehood.
This sets the stage for the entire passage.
2
Extract al-Juwayni's rule
Al-Juwayni states: 'Do not tolerate the opponent except in a place where you know for certain that tolerance will not harm you.' Tolerance is only permissible when it does not damage the truth.
This is a conditional permission, not a blanket rule.
3
Apply Ibn al-Jawzi's graded response
Ibn al-Jawzi outlines a graded approach: point out the error without shaming; if the opponent persists, stop unless the error might mislead listeners, in which case gently clarify the truth.
The response depends on the risk of harm to the audience.
4
Analyze Ibn 'Aqil's fairness principle
Ibn 'Aqil argues that an error in one school does not prove error in another, just as lying in one report does not prove lying in all reports. Each argument must be evaluated independently.
This prevents hasty generalization and unjust condemnation.
5
Differentiate frequency from certainty
Frequent errors create suspicion and reduce confidence, but they do not constitute definitive proof of corruption. The opponent's overall reliability is assessed, but each claim is judged on its own.
This nuance avoids both naivety and unfairness.

5 - عدم التسامح مع الخصم فيما يتعلق بالحق : لا يعني تواضع الباحث المجادل أن يتهاون في شأن الحق فيتسامح في تجاوزه وتجاهله، بل ينبغي عليه أن يقف في وجه كل محاولة لتجاوز الحق أو الطمس عليه دون أن يحجزه هذا عن التسامح مع الخصم في أي موقف يبدر منه ولا يكون فيه تجاوز للحق أو تطاول عليه . وفي هذا قال الجويني: لا تسامح الخصم إلا في موضع يعلم يقينا أن المسامحة فيه لا تضرك ، لأنه طالما قيل : المسامحة في المناظرة شؤم»(2) فموقف المجادل هو موقف المدافع عن الحق المنافح عن حماه بكل قوة وجهد، لكن هذا لا أن يعني یهین خصمه أو يخجله، بل يتحرى الوسيلة الهادئة المتوددة في التنبيه إلى الخطأ والكشف عن وجه الصواب. قال أبو محمد الجوزي: إن زل خصمه فليوقفه على زلله، غير مخجل له بالتشنيع عليه، فإن أصر أمسك ، إلا أن يكون ذلك الزلل مما يحاذر استقراره عند السامعين، فينبههم على الصواب فيه بألطف الوجوه، جمعا بين المصلحتين (3) ثم إن الإنصاف يقتضي من الباحث إذا ما زل خصمه في مسألة أن لا يجعل من ذلك سببًا إلى رد كل ما يصدر عنه من رأي، فالزلل في رأي لا يعني الزلل في كل الآراء، والعدل يقتضي أن ينظر إلى كل رأي على حدة ويعالج على أساس ما ينطوي عليه من خطأ أو صواب. وفي هذا يقول ابن عقيل : إذا كان أحد الخصمين في الجدل قد أخطأ في بعض المذاهب، فاحذر الاغترار بذلك، فإنه ليس في خطئه في مذهب دليل على أنه قد أخطأ في مذهب آخر، كما ليس في كذبه في خبر دليل على أنه قد كذب في خبر آخر أخبر به فلا تلتفت إلى التمويه بأن بعض مذاهب فلان يتعلق ببعض، فإن فسد واحد منها فسد جميعها، فإن ذلك يحملك على التخطئة بغير بصيرة لمن لعل أن يكون مصيبا فيما أتى به فاعتبر ذلك، ولا تتكل على مثل هذا المعنى، ولكن إذا كثر خطؤه، أوجب ذلك تهما لمذهبه وقلة سكون إلى اختياره، من غير أن يحصل ذلك دليلا على فساده لا محالة»(1)
5 - Not tolerating the opponent concerning the truth: The humility of the researching debater does not mean that he is lenient in the matter of truth and thus tolerates its transgression and disregard. Rather, he must stand against every attempt to transgress the truth or obliterate it, without this preventing him from tolerating the opponent in any situation that arises from him that does not involve transgression against the truth or aggression. In this regard, al-Juwayni said: 'Do not tolerate the opponent except in a place where you know for certain that tolerance will not harm you, for it has long been said: Tolerance in debate is a bad omen.' So the position of the debater is the position of a defender of truth, vigorously protecting its sanctuary with all strength and effort, but this does not mean that he insults his opponent or shames him. Rather, he seeks a calm and affectionate means of pointing out the error and revealing the correct position. Abu Muhammad al-Jawzi said: 'If his opponent slips, let him point out his slip without shaming him by harsh criticism. If he persists, then desist, unless that slip is such that its stability among the listeners is feared, in which case he should alert them to the correct view in the gentlest manner, combining both benefits.' Then fairness requires that if the researcher's opponent errs in an issue, he should not make that a reason to reject every opinion issued by him. For a slip in one opinion does not mean a slip in all opinions. Justice requires that each opinion be considered separately and treated based on what it contains of error or correctness. In this regard, Ibn 'Aqil said: 'If one of the two opponents in debate errs in some school, beware of being deceived by that. For his error in one school is not evidence that he erred in another school, just as his lying in one report is not evidence that he lied in another report he reported. So do not pay attention to the sophistry that some schools of so-and-so are connected to others, and if one fails then all fail. Indeed, that will lead you to declare someone wrong without insight, when perhaps he is correct in what he presented. So consider that, and do not rely on such a notion. But if his errors are frequent, that creates suspicion of his school and lack of confidence in his choice, without that becoming a definitive proof of its corruption.'
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage discusses the principle of not tolerating falsehood in debate while maintaining proper conduct. The author begins by clarifying that a researcher's humility does not mean compromising on truth; rather, he must firmly oppose any attempt to transgress it. However, this firmness does not entail harshness or humiliation of the opponent. The author supports this with a quote from al-Juwayni: tolerance is only permissible where it does not harm the truth, as tolerance in debate is ominous. Then, citing Ibn al-Jawzi, the author outlines a graded response: if the opponent errs, point out the error without shaming; if he persists, stop unless the error might mislead listeners, in which case gently clarify the truth. The passage then addresses a related issue: fairness requires that an opponent's error in one matter does not lead to rejecting all his views. Ibn 'Aqil warns against assuming that a mistake in one school implies mistakes in others, similar to not assuming a liar always lies. However, frequent errors do create suspicion but not definitive proof of corruption. The lesson emphasizes balanced conduct: defending truth without injustice.

Key Terms
مسامحة
تخطئة
زلل

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) That all his views are wrong.
B) That he is likely wrong in other schools as well.
C) That this error alone does not prove error in other views.
D) That he must be lying about everything.

Key Takeaway
Defending truth requires both courage and fairness: stand firm against falsehood but judge each argument on its own merits.

Attentiveness to the Opponent's Speech in Debate
Advice on attentiveness to avoid deception in debate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key practices for maintaining attentiveness in debate to avoid being misled by the opponent's deceptions.

Core Idea
Attentiveness is the shield that prevents the debater from being deceived by the opponent's fallacies.

This passage from a usul al-fiqh debate manual emphasizes that the successful debater is not the one who speaks most, but the one who listens most carefully. Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni instructs that thorough understanding of the opponent's speech is the only safeguard against confusion and deception. Without this skill, the debater may inadvertently help the opponent elevate falsehood over truth.

Guided Path
1
Suspend judgment
Before responding, fully absorb the opponent's words without interpreting them through your own biases. This prevents premature reactions.
Al-Juwayni says to understand 'to the utmost degree and thoroughness.'
2
Parse the structure
Break the opponent's speech into claims, evidence, and conclusions. Identify the logical flow.
This helps you place everything in its proper place.
3
Detect potential fallacies
Listen for ambiguities, overgeneralizations, or deceptive comparisons (talbis, tazwir). Be alert to every doubt.
The author warns that fallacies may be hidden in the opponent's speech.
4
Place your response correctly
Once the speech is fully understood, place your counter-arguments precisely, addressing each point in order.
This prevents confusion in the order of speech and avoids mixing truth with falsehood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
6 - التيقظ لكلام المخالف وتفهمه جيدا: إن المجادل الناجح هو من يملك القدرة على الاستمرار في متابعة المخالف فيما يصدر عنه من كلام أو خطاب، وكذا التنبه لكل شبهة أو مغالطة يمكن أن يتضمنها هذا الكلام أو الخطاب، وهذه القدرة هي التي تمكنه من مواصلة الجدل المسألة موضع النزاع على مستوى واحد من التمكن والاقتدار العلمي فلا يقع في موقع الضعف الذي يمكن مخالفه من تقرير ما يريد تقريره عليه من أفكار قد تحمل في طياتها مغالطات ترفع من مقام الباطل وتنزل بمقام الحق، لهذا نبه إمام الحرمين الجويني كل مجادل إلى ضرورة التنبه لهذا فقال : عليك بمراعاة كلام الخصم وتفهم معانيه على غاية الحد والاستقصاء، فإن فيه أمانًا من اضطراب ترتيب فصول الكلام عليك فيسهل عليك عند ذلك وضع كل شيء موضعه وفيه أيضًا أمان من تلبيس الخصم والذهاب عن تزويره. ولا تمكنه من جمع القصور عليك في الأسئلة والأجوبة، فإنه يؤدي إلى انتشار الكلام واختلاط مواضع النكتة والتباس موضع الحق بغيره، وإن طول عليك
6 - Alertness to the opponent's speech and understanding it well: The successful debater is one who possesses the ability to continuously follow the opponent in what he utters of speech or discourse, and also to be alert to every doubt or fallacy that may be contained in this speech or discourse. This ability enables him to continue the debate on the issue under dispute at a consistent level of scholarly competence and capability, so that he does not fall into a position of weakness that allows his opponent to establish upon him ideas that may carry fallacies that elevate falsehood and lower truth. For this reason, Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni alerted every debater to the necessity of being attentive to this, saying: 'You must observe the opponent's speech and understand its meanings to the utmost degree and thoroughness. For in that is safety from confusion in the order of the sections of speech upon you, and it will become easy for you then to place everything in its proper place. It also contains safety from the opponent's deception and being led away by his misrepresentation. Do not enable him to accumulate shortcomings against you in questions and answers, for that leads to dispersion of speech, mixing of subtle points, and confusion of the truth with other things, even if it becomes lengthy for you.'
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage discusses the critical skill of attentiveness in debate. The author begins by defining the successful debater as one who can continuously follow the opponent's speech and be alert to any doubts or fallacies. This ability maintains the debate at a high scholarly level, preventing the debater from becoming weak and allowing the opponent to impose fallacious ideas. The author then quotes al-Juwayni, who instructs to observe the opponent's speech thoroughly, understand it completely, and thereby avoid confusion and be able to respond correctly. Al-Juwayni warns against allowing the opponent to accumulate weaknesses in one's questions and answers, as this leads to disorder and obscures the truth. The lesson emphasizes that careful listening and analysis are essential to successful debate.

Key Takeaway

Ibn 'Aqil's Rule: Error in One Opinion Does Not Imply Error in All
Ibn 'Aqil warns against the fallacy of extrapolating error from one opinion to another.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fallacy of extrapolating error from one opinion to another in a jadal debate and apply Ibn 'Aqil's rule to evaluate each opinion independently.

Core Idea
Error in one opinion is not evidence of error in another; judge each argument on its own.

In Islamic dialectic (jadal), fairness requires that each opinion be evaluated on its own merits. Ibn 'Aqil warns against a common fallacy: using a single mistake to dismiss all of a scholar's positions. This lesson unpacks that principle and its application in debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Fallacy
Read the passage and locate where Ibn 'Aqil describes the erroneous inference: error in one opinion does not imply error in all opinions. This is the fallacy of hasty generalization.
2
Understand the Analogy
Ibn 'Aqil compares error in a school of thought to a lie in a report. Just as one lie does not prove all reports are lies, one error does not prove all opinions are erroneous.
3
Recognize the Qualification
Ibn 'Aqil adds that if errors become numerous, it may create suspicion but does not constitute decisive proof. Note the nuance: frequency matters but does not automatically invalidate.
4
Apply the Rule
In a debate, when an opponent tries to dismiss all of a scholar's views because of one mistake, use Ibn 'Aqil's rule to demand separate consideration of each opinion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذلك سببًا إلى رد كل ما يصدر عنه من رأي، فالزلل في رأي لا يعني الزلل في كل الآراء، والعدل يقتضي أن ينظر إلى كل رأي على حدة ويعالج على أساس ما ينطوي عليه من خطأ أو صواب. وفي هذا يقول ابن عقيل : إذا كان أحد الخصمين في الجدل قد أخطأ في بعض المذاهب، فاحذر الاغترار بذلك، فإنه ليس في خطئه في مذهب دليل على أنه قد أخطأ في مذهب آخر، كما ليس في كذبه في خبر دليل على أنه قد كذب في خبر آخر أخبر به فلا تلتفت إلى التمويه بأن بعض مذاهب فلان يتعلق ببعض، فإن فسد واحد منها فسد جميعها، فإن ذلك يحملك على التخطئة بغير بصيرة لمن لعل أن يكون مصيبا فيما أتى به فاعتبر ذلك، ولا تتكل على مثل هذا المعنى، ولكن إذا كثر خطؤه، أوجب ذلك تهما لمذهبه وقلة سكون إلى اختياره، من غير أن يحصل ذلك دليلا على فساده لا محالة
That is a cause to reject every opinion that issued from him, for a slip in one opinion does not mean a slip in all opinions, and justice requires looking at each opinion separately and treating it based on what it contains of error or correctness. In this regard, Ibn 'Aqil said: If one of the two disputants in dialectic has erred in some schools of thought, beware of being deceived by that, for his error in one school is not evidence that he erred in another school, just as his lie in one report is not evidence that he lied in another report he reported. So do not pay attention to the obfuscation that some of so-and-so's schools of thought are connected to others, such that if one is corrupt, all of them are corrupt, for that will lead you to falsely accuse without insight someone who may be correct in what he presented. So consider that, and do not rely on such a notion. But if his errors become numerous, that may cause suspicion regarding his school and lack of confidence in his choice, without that being a decisive proof of its corruption.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage, written in the context of Islamic legal dialectic (jadal), presents a crucial principle of fairness in debate. The author begins by stating that a mistake in one opinion does not imply a mistake in all opinions—each opinion must be judged on its own merits. He then quotes Ibn 'Aqil, a Hanbali jurist, who warns against the fallacy of extrapolating error. Ibn 'Aqil uses a powerful analogy: just as a lie in one report does not prove that the person lied in another report, so too an error in one doctrine does not prove error in another. He cautions against the deceptive argument that because some doctrines of a scholar are interconnected, the corruption of one necessitates the corruption of all. This would lead to unjust condemnation without insight. However, Ibn 'Aqil adds a qualification: if errors become frequent, that may create a presumption of weakness, but it does not constitute conclusive proof of corruption. The lesson is a key principle in the ethics of dialectic: avoid hasty generalizations and judge each argument individually. This is directly applicable to fiqh debates where scholars may have multiple positions.

Key Terms
الجدل
التمويه

D) He should be rejected as a source of knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Judge each opinion on its own merits; do not let one error taint all others.

The Necessity of Attentiveness in Dialectic: Practical and Cognitive Benefits
Al-Juwayni and Ibn 'Aqil stress careful attention to the opponent's speech and explain the cognitive benefits of jadal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply al-Juwayni's strategy of summarizing and confining the opponent's speech, and explain how jadal sharpens the mind according to Ibn 'Aqil.

Core Idea
Attentiveness in jadal is a shared duty that sharpens the mind and safeguards truth.

This passage from a classical jadal manual emphasises that successful disputation requires constant attentiveness to the opponent's speech. Imam al-Juwayni provides practical steps to avoid confusion and counter deceit, while Ibn 'Aqil highlights the cognitive benefits of dialectic as a tool for intellectual refinement. Together, they show that jadal is not merely competitive but a collaborative pursuit of truth requiring mutual intellectual effort.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core requirement
The passage begins by stating that the successful disputant must continuously follow the opponent's speech and be alert to every fallacy or ambiguity. This ability keeps the debate on a level playing field.
This is the baseline skill for any jadal participant.
2
Extract al-Juwayni's practical advice
Al-Juwayni advises: observe the opponent's speech and understand its meanings thoroughly. Summarize long arguments to pinpoint the core issue, then confine the opponent to that point. Get his admission on precise wording and meaning to prevent escape from logical consequences.
This strategy prevents the opponent from shifting ground or denying claims.
3
Recognise the mutual obligation
Attentiveness is required from both sides, not just one. It cultivates intellectual reflection, mental comprehension, and quick-wittedness for both parties.
Jadal is a cooperative exercise in truth-seeking.
4
Understand Ibn 'Aqil's analogy and its implication
Ibn 'Aqil compares the mind to the eye: the mind sleeps longer and needs more sharpening than a sword. Dialectic sharpens, hones, stirs thoughts, and extracts subtle points. It is an instrument for the mind's apprehension of truth.
The analogy underscores that jadal is not just about winning but about intellectual growth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التيقظ لكلام المخالف وتفهمه جيدا: إن المجادل الناجح هو من يملك القدرة على الاستمرار في متابعة المخالف فيما يصدر عنه من كلام أو خطاب، وكذا التنبه لكل شبهة أو مغالطة يمكن أن يتضمنها هذا الكلام أو الخطاب، وهذه القدرة هي التي تمكنه من مواصلة الجدل المسألة موضع النزاع على مستوى واحد من التمكن والاقتدار العلمي فلا يقع في موقع الضعف الذي يمكن مخالفه من تقرير ما يريد تقريره عليه من أفكار قد تحمل في طياتها مغالطات ترفع من مقام الباطل وتنزل بمقام الحق، لهذا نبه إمام الحرمين الجويني كل مجادل إلى ضرورة التنبه لهذا فقال : عليك بمراعاة كلام الخصم وتفهم معانيه على غاية الحد والاستقصاء، فإن فيه أمانًا من اضطراب ترتيب فصول الكلام عليك فيسهل عليك عند ذلك وضع كل شيء موضعه وفيه أيضًا أمان من تلبيس الخصم والذهاب عن تزويره. ولا تمكنه من جمع القصور عليك في الأسئلة والأجوبة، فإنه يؤدي إلى انتشار الكلام واختلاط مواضع النكتة والتباس موضع الحق بغيره، وإن طول عليك كلامه بعباراته الطويلة، فلخص من جميعها موضع الحاجة إليه فتحصره عليه، ثم تكلم فيه بما يليق به؛ لأنك إذا أحصرت عليه في كلامه ألفاظه ومعانيه وأخذت إقراره في كل ذلك فقلت : ألست قلت كذا ومعناه كذا؟ لم يمكنه الهرب مما يلزمه عليه من كلامك ولا الرجوع، وإذا لم تفعل ذلك، ربما ناكرك عند الإلزام، فتسد مواضع الخلل حين تنبه له عند الالتزام. وهذا التنبه والتيقظ الدائم المستمر لا يلزم أحد المتجادلين دون الآخر، بل يلزمهما معا في آن واحد وهو الكفيل بتنمية روح التأمل الفكري والإدراك العقلي وسرعة البديهة عند كل من الطرفين، وقد تنبه الإمام ابن عقيل الحنبلي إلى هذه الفائدة في الجدل، فقال: «إذا كان الغرض بالجدل إدراك الحق، وكان السبيل إلى ذلك التثبت والتأمل ، وجب على كل واحد من الخصمين استعمالهما، وإلا حصلا على مجرد الطلب مع حرمان الظفر، وحاجة كل واحد من الخصمين إلى التنبه على ما يأتي به صاحبه كحاجة الآخر إلى ذلك . قال بعض العلماء في هذا الشأن : العقل أطول رقدة من العين، وأحوج إلى الشَّحْدِ من السيف، وقد أحسن التشبيه، لأن العقل يحصل به درك الحق كما يدرك بالعين الشخص ، إلا أن حاجة العقل إلى التنبيه على الحق أشد من حاجة العين إلى التنبيه على الشخص، ولربما أيقظ العين من رقدتها لكزة أو كلمة توجب اليقظة بسرعة، والعقل يحتاج في تنبيهه إلى عمل، وهو تخليص نظره من آفات النظر المعترضة . فالجدل يشحذ ويرهف ويثير الخواطر، ويخرج الدقائق، وكل ذلك آلة لإدراك العقل للحق ، فإذا كان لا سبيل إلى حل شبهة الخصم في الجدل إلا بعد إدراكها، فلابد لخصمه من التأمل لما يأتي به فإن وقع له معنى الشبهة تمكن من كسرها بما يدخل عليها من الفساد الظاهر والبيان القاهر، وإن لم يقع له، راجعه في ذلك إلى أن يستقر الأمر على إظهار أنه قد أورد ما يحتاج إلى حل، أو لم يورد ذلك، فيكون كلامه أو إمساكه بحسب ما يظهر من الحال، فهذه طريقة الإنصاف، والتي
Attentiveness to the opponent's speech and understanding it well: The successful disputant is one who possesses the ability to continuously follow the opponent in what he utters in speech or discourse, and also to be alert to every ambiguity or fallacy that this speech or discourse may contain. This ability enables him to continue the debate on the issue under dispute at a single level of mastery and scholarly competence, so he does not fall into a position of weakness that allows his opponent to establish what he wants to establish upon him of ideas that may contain fallacies that elevate falsehood and degrade truth. For this reason, Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni alerted every disputant to the necessity of being heedful of this, saying: You must observe the opponent's speech and understand its meanings to the utmost extent and thoroughness, for in that is safety from disorder in the arrangement of the chapters of speech upon you, and it will become easy for you then to place everything in its proper place. In it also is safety from the opponent's deceit and avoidance of his fabrication. Do not allow him to gather shortcomings against you in questions and answers, for that leads to dispersion of speech and mixing up of subtle points and confusion of the truth with other things. Even if his speech becomes long with lengthy expressions, summarize from all of it the point needed, thus confining him to it, then speak about it appropriately. Because if you confine him in his speech to his words and meanings and take his admission on all of that, saying: 'Did you not say such-and-such, and its meaning is such-and-such?' he will not be able to escape what follows from your speech nor to retract. And if you do not do that, perhaps he will deny [your claim] when you impose [the conclusion], so you block the points of weakness when you are alerted to them at the moment of commitment. And this constant and continuous attentiveness is not required of one disputant without the other; rather it is required of both at the same time, and it guarantees the development of the spirit of intellectual reflection, mental comprehension, and quick-wittedness for both parties. Imam Ibn 'Aqil al-Hanbali drew attention to this benefit in dialectic, saying: 'If the purpose of dialectic is to apprehend truth, and the way to that is verification and reflection, then each of the two disputants must employ them; otherwise they will obtain mere pursuit with deprivation of success. The need of each disputant for attentiveness to what his companion brings is like the need of the other for that. Some scholars said in this regard: The mind sleeps longer than the eye, and is more in need of sharpening than the sword. And he made a good analogy, for truth is apprehended by the mind just as the person is apprehended by the eye, except that the mind's need for alerting to truth is greater than the eye's need for alerting to the person. Perhaps a poke or a word that quickly brings wakefulness may awaken the eye from its sleep, but the mind, in being alerted, needs effort: to purify its reflection from the defects of reflection that obstruct it. So dialectic sharpens, hones, stirs thoughts, and extracts subtle points, and all that is an instrument for the mind's apprehension of truth. So if there is no way to solve the opponent's ambiguity in dialectic except after apprehending it, then his opponent must reflect on what he brings. If he grasps the meaning of the ambiguity, he can break it by bringing against it apparent corruption and overwhelming demonstration. And if he does not grasp it, he should refer back to him until the matter settles on showing whether he has raised something that needs a solution or not, so his speech or silence is according to what appears from the situation. This is the way of fairness, and which...'
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive treatment of the practical and intellectual requirements of successful dialectic (jadal) in the Islamic tradition. It opens with a heading emphasizing the need for attentiveness to the opponent's speech and understanding it well. The author then describes the ideal disputant as someone who can follow the opponent's every word and be alert to any fallacy or ambiguity. This ability maintains the debate on a level playing field. The text then quotes Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni, who advises thorough observation of the opponent's words to avoid confusion and to be able to counter deceit. Juwayni recommends summarizing long speeches to pinpoint the core issue and then getting the opponent's admission on the precise wording and meaning, preventing escape from logical consequences. This practical strategy is part of the adab al-jadal (ethics of debate). Next, the author explains that this mutual attentiveness is required from both sides, as it cultivates mental reflection and quickness. Ibn 'Aqil is then quoted to reinforce this: the very purpose of dialectic—truth—requires verification and reflection. He uses a striking analogy: the mind sleeps longer than the eye, so dialectic sharpens it like a sword. The passage concludes by stating that resolving an opponent's ambiguity requires first apprehending it, then either breaking it with clear arguments or asking for clarification. This approach ensures fairness (insaf). The passage thus serves as both a practical manual for disputants and a philosophical justification for the discipline of jadal as a tool for intellectual refinement.

Key Terms

التلبيس
النكتة

?

Why Both Disputants Must Keep Constant Vigilance
Author asserts mutual vigilance essential in jadal, quoting Ibn 'Aqil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why constant vigilance is required from both disputants in jadal and cite Ibn 'Aqil's statement to support the claim.

Core Idea
Vigilance in jadal is a mutual duty; without it, truth-seeking fails.

In the heat of debate, it is tempting to think only the respondent needs to be alert. But the author insists that awareness must be constant for both parties. Let's examine why this is so and how Ibn 'Aqil grounds this in the very purpose of jadal: the pursuit of truth.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's core claim
Read the first sentence and the later statement 'وهذا التنبه والتيقظ الدائم المستمر لا يلزم أحد المتجادلين دون الآخر، بل يلزمهما معاً'. The author explicitly says vigilance binds both disputants.
2
Understand the practical consequence
The author gives an example: if you summarize your opponent's words and secure his affirmation, he cannot escape. This shows that vigilance prevents evasion.
This is the technique of 'taḥṣīr' (confining the opponent).
3
Analyze Ibn 'Aqil's argument
Ibn 'Aqil says: the purpose of jadal is truth; the means are verification and reflection; so both must employ them. Trace his logical chain.
4
Connect to the broader lesson
Why does the author stress this? Without mutual vigilance, jadal becomes a mere contest, not a truth-seeking tool. This is foundational for fair disputation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كلامه بعباراته الطويلة، فلخص من جميعها موضع الحاجة إليه فتحصره عليه، ثم تكلم فيه بما يليق به؛ لأنك إذا أحصرت عليه في كلامه ألفاظه ومعانيه وأخذت إقراره في كل ذلك فقلت: ألست قلت كذا ومعناه كذا؟ لم يمكنه الهرب مما يلزمه عليه من كلامك ولا الرجوع، وإذا لم تفعل ذلك، ربما ناكرك عند الإلزام، فتسد مواضع الخلل حين تنبه له عند الالتزام. وهذا التنبه والتيقظ الدائم المستمر لا يلزم أحد المتجادلين دون الآخر، بل يلزمهما معاً في آن واحد وهو الكفيل بتنمية روح التأمل الفكري والإدراك العقلي وسرعة البديهة عند كل من الطرفين، وقد تنبه الإمام ابن عقيل الحنبلي إلى هذه الفائدة في الجدل، فقال: إذا كان الغرض بالجدل إدراك الحق، وكان السبيل إلى ذلك التثبت والتأمل، وجب على كل واحد من الخصمين استعمالهما، وإلا حصلا على مجرد الطلب مع حرمان الظفر، وحاجة كل واحد من الخصمين إلى التنبه على ما يأتي به صاحبه كحاجة الآخر إلى ذلك. قال بعض العلماء في هذا الشأن: العقل أطول رقدة من العين، وأحوج إلى الشَّحْدِ من السيف، وقد أحسن التشبيه، لأن العقل يحصل به درك الحق كما يدرك بالعين الشخص، إلا أن حاجة العقل إلى التنبيه على الحق أشد من حاجة العين إلى التنبيه على الشخص، ولربما أيقظ العين من رقدتها لكزة أو كلمة توجب اليقظة بسرعة، والعقل يحتاج في تنبيهه إلى عمل، وهو تخليص نظره من آفات النظر المعترضة. فالجدل يشحذ ويرهف ويثير الخواطر، ويخرج الدقائق، وكل ذلك آلة لإدراك العقل للحق، فإذا كان لا سبيل إلى حل شبهة الخصم في الجدل إلا بعد إدراكها، فلابد لخصمه من التأمل لما يأتي به فإن وقع له معنى الشبهة تمكن من كسرها بما يدخل عليها من الفساد الظاهر والبيان القاهر، وإن لم يقع له، راجعه في ذلك إلى أن يستقر الأمر على إظهار أنه قد أورد ما يحتاج إلى حل، أو لم يورد ذلك، فيكون كلامه أو إمساكه بحسب ما يظهر من الحال، فهذه طريقة الإنصاف.
His speech with his long phrases, so summarize from all of it the place of need to it and confine it to him, then speak about it what is appropriate; because when you confine him in his speech to his words and meanings and take his affirmation in all of that and say: 'Did you not say such and such and its meaning is such and such?' he cannot escape from what is incumbent upon him from your words nor retract. And if you do not do that, perhaps he will dispute with you when you impose, so you block the points of weakness when you become aware of them at the moment of commitment. This constant and continuous awareness and vigilance is not incumbent upon one of the two disputants to the exclusion of the other, but rather it is incumbent upon both at the same moment, and it is the guarantor of developing the spirit of intellectual reflection, rational perception, and quick-wittedness for both parties. Imam Ibn 'Aqil al-Hanbali pointed out this benefit in jadal, saying: 'If the purpose of jadal is to perceive the truth, and the means to that is verification and reflection, then each of the two opponents must employ them, otherwise they attain mere seeking without success, and the need of each opponent for awareness of what his companion brings is like the need of the other for that.' Some scholars said in this regard: 'The intellect sleeps longer than the eye, and is more in need of sharpening than the sword.' And he likened it well, because the intellect perceives truth as the eye perceives a person, except that the intellect's need for alerting to the truth is more intense than the eye's need for alerting to a person. Perhaps a poke or a word that brings about wakefulness quickly awakens the eye from its sleep, but the intellect needs effort in its awakening, which is purifying its sight from the afflictions of objectionable reasoning. Thus jadal sharpens, whets, stirs thoughts, and brings out subtleties, and all of that is an instrument for the intellect's perception of truth. Since there is no way to resolve the opponent's doubt in jadal except after perceiving it, then his opponent must reflect upon what he brings. If the meaning of the doubt occurs to him, he can break it by what enters it of manifest corruption and overwhelming proof. And if it does not occur to him, he returns to him on that until the matter settles on showing that he has brought what needs a solution, or he did not bring that, so his speech or silence is according to what appears of the situation. This is the method of fairness.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage discusses the necessity of constant vigilance and mental alertness for both disputants in jadal. It begins with a practical example: when you summarize your opponent's lengthy speech, extracting the exact words and meanings, and then ask for confirmation, he cannot escape the implications. If you do not do this, he may deny when you impose the consequence. This shows the need for continuous awareness. The author then states that this awareness is required from both parties simultaneously, as it develops intellectual reflection, rational perception, and quick-wittedness. He quotes Imam Ibn 'Aqil (d. 513 AH) who emphasizes that if the goal of jadal is truth, then both must employ verification and reflection, otherwise they fail. A scholar likens the intellect to the eye: the intellect sleeps longer and needs more sharpening. While the eye can be awakened quickly, the intellect requires effort to purify itself from faulty reasoning. Jadal sharpens the intellect, stirs thoughts, and brings out subtle points, all of which help perceive truth. Finally, the passage explains that to refute an opponent's doubt, one must first understand it fully; then either break it with clear corruption and proof, or if not understood, continue questioning until the issue is clarified. This is the fair method.

Key Takeaway
Constant, mutual vigilance is not optional in jadal; it is the necessary condition for truth-seeking.

The Intellect's Need for Sharpening and the Method of Fairness
Author uses simile and explains fair method for handling doubts in jadal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the simile of the intellect and eye, describe how jadal sharpens the intellect, and outline the fair method for refuting doubts.

Core Idea
Jadal sharpens the intellect like a whetstone; truth emerges through patient reflection.

The author now uses a striking simile: the intellect sleeps longer than the eye and needs more sharpening than a sword. Jadal is the tool that whets the mind. He then lays out the fair procedure for handling an opponent's doubt: first understand it, then either refute or seek clarification.

Guided Path
1
Examine the simile
The scholar says: 'The intellect sleeps longer than the eye, and is more in need of sharpening than a sword.' Why is this comparison powerful?
The eye can be awakened with a poke, but intellect requires sustained effort to purify.
2
Identify how jadal helps
The author lists: jadal sharpens, whets, stirs thoughts, and brings out subtleties. All these aid the intellect in perceiving truth.
3
Understand the method for refutation
To resolve an opponent's doubt (shubha), you must first fully perceive it. Then either break it with manifest corruption and clear proof, or if you don't understand, continue questioning.
This is the 'method of fairness' (ṭarīqat al-inṣāf).
4
Apply to a real scenario
Imagine an opponent argues that qiyas is invalid because it is based on analogy, which is weak. How would you apply this method?
First, understand his objection fully. Then you might show that valid qiyas uses a clear effective cause ('illa), so it's not weak analogy.

Abandoning Partisanship to Seek Truth in Jadal
Author emphasizes the importance of abandoning prior bias and being ready to accept truth regardless of which party presents it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between truth-seeking jadal and intellectual sparring based on the attitude towards prior bias.

Core Idea
Abandoning partisanship is essential for jadal to achieve its purpose: discovering truth.

The passage presents the seventh etiquette of jadal: both parties must set aside their prior viewpoints and commit to accepting truth from any source. This is the foundation for productive dialectic. The author warns against turning jadal into mere intellectual sparring, which becomes an illegitimate use of the method. Understanding this distinction is crucial for engaging in proper munazara.

Guided Path
1
Extract the core ethical requirement
Find the sentence where the author states that both parties must abandon partisanship and declare readiness to accept the truth, regardless of source. This is the central claim of the passage.
The phrase 'تخلي كل من الفريقين عن التعصب' marks the requirement.
2
Contrast the two modes of jadal
Identify the description of proper jadal (seeking truth) versus improper jadal (intellectual sparring). Note the different goals: reaching truth vs. sharpening the mind or outlasting opponents.
The author contrasts 'الوصول إلى الحق' with 'السجال الفكري'.
3
Interpret Ibn Hubayra's authority
Analyze the quote from Ibn Hubayra. What conditions make jadal an innovation? Look for the elements: unwillingness to retract, lack of companionship, and absence of hearts prepared for truth.
Ibn Hubayra says scholars spoke of such jadal as 'محدث' (innovation).
4
Synthesize the condition for valid jadal
Conclude by stating the required mindset: the researcher must enter jadal with a mind and soul free from any prior attachment to a particular viewpoint, avoiding selfishness and bias.
The passage ends with 'خالي الذهن والنفس من أي استمساك سابق'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
7 - التخلي عن التعصب لوجهة النظر السابقة في الموضوع: إن من أهم الآداب التي ينبغي أن تتوفر في كل من يتصدى للجدل: تخلي كل من الفريقين المتصديين للمحاورة الجدلية حول موضوع معين، عن التعصب لوجهة نظره السابقة، وإعلانهما الاستعداد التام للبحث عن الحقيقة والأخذ بها عند ظهورها سواء أكانت هي وجهة نظره السابقة، أو وجهة نظر من يحاوره في المناظرة، أو وجهة نظر أخرى. وهذا ما يتفق مع القصد الأساس من الجدل والذي هو الوصول إلى الحق والصواب في المسألة المتنازع فيها، أما إن تحول الجدل في المسألة إلى مجرد سجال فكري لا يبتغي من ورائه كل من الطرفين إلا شحذ الذهن ومطاولة الخصوم والمخالفين فإنه يكون استعمالا للوسيلة المشروعة في الغرض غير المشروع، لأن الهدف ليس هو الوصول إلى الحق وإنما مجرد الجدال، إذ يبقى كل واحد من الطرفين متمسكا برأيه حتى وإن ظهر الحق على لسان مخالفه، وهذا النوع من الجدل ينبغي اجتنابه وعدم الخوض فيه، وهذا ما أكده الوزير العالم ابن هبيرة حين قال: الجدل الذي يقع بين أرباب المذاهب، أوفق ما يحمل الأمر فيه: بأن يخرج مخرج الإعادة والدرس، فأما اجتماع متجاذبين في مسألة، مع أن كلا لا يطمع أن يرجع إن ظهرت حجة ولا فيه مؤانسة ومودة وتوطئة القلوب لوعي حق، بل هو على الضد، فتكلم فيه العلماء محدث. وهو فلابد إذا أن يدخل الباحث إلى ميدان الجدل وهو خالي الذهن والنفس من أي استمساك سابق بوجهة نظر معينة من وجهات النظر المختلفة، وذلك للابتعاد عن كل أجواء التعصب والأنانية التي تصرف النفوس والأفكار عن تفهم الحق أو التسليم به ولو انكشف لها واضحًا جليًا.
7 - Abandoning partisanship to the prior viewpoint on the subject: Among the most important etiquettes that should be present in everyone who undertakes jadal is: the abandonment by each of the two parties undertaking the dialectical conversation about a specific subject of partisanship to their prior viewpoint, and their declaration of complete readiness to search for the truth and adopt it when it appears, whether it is their prior viewpoint, or the viewpoint of the one they are dialoguing with in the debate, or another viewpoint. This is consistent with the fundamental aim of jadal, which is reaching the truth and correctness in the disputed matter. However, if the jadal on the matter turns into mere intellectual sparring in which each of the two parties seeks only to sharpen the mind and outlast opponents and dissenters, then it becomes using a legitimate means for an illegitimate purpose, because the goal is not reaching the truth but merely disputation. Indeed, each of the two parties remains clinging to his opinion even if the truth appears on the tongue of his opponent. This type of jadal should be avoided and not engaged in. This is what the scholar vizier Ibn Hubayra affirmed when he said: 'The jadal that occurs among the leaders of the schools of thought - the most appropriate way to treat the matter is that it be brought out on the lines of review and study. As for two people contending on a matter, with that each does not hope to retract if evidence appears, nor is there in it companionship, affection, and preparation of hearts for perceiving truth; on the contrary, it is the opposite. Thus the scholars spoke about it as an innovation.' Therefore, necessarily, the researcher must enter the arena of jadal while his mind and soul are free from any prior clinging to any particular viewpoint among the different viewpoints, in order to stay away from all atmospheres of partisanship and selfishness that turn souls and thoughts away from understanding the truth or submitting to it even if it is clearly and evidently revealed to them.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh etiquette of jadal: abandoning partisanship to prior views. It begins by stating that both parties must set aside their previous biases and declare readiness to accept truth from any source. This aligns with the fundamental goal of jadal: reaching truth and correctness in the disputed matter. The author then warns against degenerating into mere intellectual sparring, where the goal becomes sharpening the mind or outlasting opponents rather than seeking truth. In such case, each clings to his opinion even when truth appears from the other, making the exercise illegitimate. The author quotes Ibn Hubayra (d. 560 AH), who says that proper jadal among school leaders should be like review and study. When two people contend without willingness to retract when evidence appears, and without companionship or affection to prepare hearts for truth, it becomes an innovation (muhdath) that scholars have criticized. The passage concludes that the researcher must enter jadal with a mind and soul free from any prior attachment to a particular viewpoint, avoiding partisanship and selfishness that hinder understanding or acceptance of truth, even when it is clear.

Key Terms
التعصب (al-ta'aṣṣub)
السجال الفكري (al-sijāl al-fikrī)
محدث (muḥdath)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is the best form of debate.
B) It should be treated as review and study.
C) Scholars spoke of it as an innovation (muhdath).
D) It is permissible if both parties are sincere.

Key Takeaway
Jadal is only legitimate when pursued with an open mind, ready to accept truth from any source; otherwise it becomes a blameworthy innovation.

Identify whether this qualifies as truth-seeking jadal or intellectual sparring according to the passage. Justify your answer with specific phrases from the text.

Mutual Respect Between Debaters: The First Pillar of Jadal Etiquette
Author discusses mutual respect as the first etiquette governing the relationship between debaters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list at least five specific obligations that debaters owe each other according to Imam Ibn 'Aqil and explain how each obligation facilitates the pursuit of truth.

Core Idea
Mutual respect is the backbone of scholarly jadal; without it, debate becomes a battlefield instead of a collaborative inquiry.

In the previous section, we learned about the personal qualities a debater must cultivate. Now, the author turns to the relationship between debaters. Since jadal is a joint search for truth, the interaction must be governed by mutual ethics. The first and most fundamental of these is mutual respect, which Imam Ibn 'Aqil elaborates in a powerful list of dos and don'ts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Relationship Type
Read the author's statement: the debaters are partners in research (al-bahth al-mushtarik). This is not an adversarial relationship; they share the goal of reaching truth. Contrast this with competitive or hostile relationships.
This sets the tone for all subsequent etiquettes.
2
Extract Ibn 'Aqil's Obligations
Carefully read the Arabic quotation from Ibn 'Aqil. List each duty mentioned, such as conciseness in address, not interrupting, paying attention, no shouting, reflecting on what the other says, avoiding harshness, not forcing denial of necessary truths unless required by one's madhhab, keeping within bounds in questioning/answering, not belittling, correcting only when the argument compels, focusing on meaning over wording, and staying within customary expression.
The text cuts off mid-sentence, but these are the key obligations given.
3
Categorize the Obligations
Group the obligations into categories: communication behaviors (listening, speaking politely), attitude (respect, humility), epistemic boundaries (when to correct), and focus (meaning vs. wording). This helps understand the breadth of mutual respect.
Each category serves a different purpose in preserving the integrity of the debate.
4
Evaluate the Rationale
For each obligation, think about how it protects the process of reaching truth. For example, not interrupting ensures the full argument is heard; avoiding belittlement prevents defensiveness; focusing on meaning avoids quibbling over wording. Connect each rule to the goal of truth-seeking.
This step builds appreciation for why these rules are not mere politeness but epistemic necessities.

المبحث الثاني
الآداب الخاصة بالعلاقة بين المتجادلين
إن الجدل العلمي كما يفترض توفر جملة من المواصفات الأدبية يتحلى بها الباحث المجادل في شخصه ويلزم بها نفسه، يتطلب كذلك توفر جملة من المواصفات الأدبية الأخرى التي تضبط علاقته بغيره ممن يمكن أن يدير معه عملية الجدل، ذلك أن هذه العلاقة هي علاقة التعاون العلمي والبحث العلمي المشترك المبتغى من ورائه الوصول إلى الحقيقة في المسألة موضع الجدل، ولذلك فإن العملية الجدلية لا يمكنها أن تفضي إلى تحقيق أهدافها وتسير سيرًا سليما في أطوارها إلا إذا توفرت فيها جملة من الأخلاقيات المتبادلة بين طرفي الجدل، وهي :
1 - احترام بعضهما البعض وعدم استصغار أحدهما للآخر :
الجدل العلمي – كما سبق أن أكدنا -

اعلم أنه يجب لكل واحد منهما على صاحبه مثل الذي يجب للآخر عليه ؛ من الإجمال في خطابه، وترك التقطيع لكلامه والإقبال عليه، وترك الصياح في وجهه والتأمل لما يأتي به، والتجنب للحدة والضجر عليه، وترك الحمل له على جحد الضرورة إلا من حيث يلزمه ذلك بمذهبه، وترك الإخراج له عن الحد الذي ينبغي أن يكون عليه في السؤال أو الجواب، وترك الاستصغار له والاحتقار لما يأتي به إلا من حيث تلزمه الحجة إياه والتنبيه له على ذلك إن بدر منه، أو مناقضته إن ظهرت في كلامه، وأن لا يمانعه العبارة إذا أدت المعنى وكان الغرض إنما هو في المعنى دون العبارة، وأن لا يخرج في عبارته عن العادة، وأن لا
The Second Section: Etiquettes Specifically Related to the Relationship Between the Two Debaters
Indeed, scholarly jadal, just as it presupposes the presence of a set of literary qualities that the debating researcher adorns himself with and binds himself to, likewise requires the presence of a set of other literary qualities that govern his relationship with others with whom he may conduct the process of jadal, because this relationship is one of scholarly cooperation and joint academic research intended to reach the truth in the matter that is the subject of jadal. Therefore, the dialectical process cannot lead to achieving its goals and proceed smoothly in its stages unless a set of mutual ethics between the two sides of jadal is present. They are:
1 – Respecting one another and not belittling one another:
Scholarly jadal – as we have already affirmed – is jadal that aims to reach the truth in the matter that is the subject of jadal. Therefore, each of the two debaters has no connection with the other except a relationship of joint research in the matter. There is not between them, in truth, any hatred or animosity that might drive one to transgress against the sanctity or rank of the other, which requires that jadal be conducted between them on a solid foundation of mutual respect. This is what Imam Ibn 'Aqil affirmed when he said:
Know that each one of them owes to his companion the same as the other owes to him: conciseness in addressing him, leaving off interrupting his speech and paying attention to it, leaving off shouting in his face, reflecting on what he brings, avoiding harshness and annoyance towards him, leaving off forcing him to deny a necessary truth unless his madhhab requires it, leaving off driving him beyond the proper limit in questioning or answering, leaving off belittling him and despising what he brings unless the argument compels him to do so and alerting him to it if it occurs from him, or his contradiction if it appears in his speech, and that he should not argue with him about wording if it conveys the meaning and the purpose is only in the meaning not the wording, and that he should not depart in his wording from custom, and that he should not...
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage introduces the second section of etiquettes, which govern the relationship between debaters. The author begins by noting that just as the debater must have personal qualities, he must also adhere to interpersonal ethics. The debaters are partners in a scholarly collaboration aimed at finding truth; thus, their relationship must be based on mutual ethics. The first of these is mutual respect and avoiding belittlement. The author explains that scholarly jadal is a joint search for truth, so there should be no hatred or animosity that might lead to disrespect. This requires a solid foundation of mutual respect. He then supports this with a quotation from Imam Ibn 'Aqil, who outlines detailed obligations: each debater owes the other courtesy, attentive listening, avoiding outbursts, not forcing the opponent to deny self-evident truths unless his own school obligates it, not exceeding bounds in questioning/answering, not belittling the opponent's arguments unless the evidence compels correction, and focusing on meaning rather than wording. The passage ends abruptly, but it clearly establishes that the debaters must treat each other with dignity and respect throughout the process.

استصغار
جدل علمي

Key Takeaway
Mutual respect in jadal is not mere courtesy; it is an epistemic necessity for collaborative truth-seeking, operationalized through specific duties like attentive listening and avoiding belittlement.

Etiquettes of Mutual Respect in Jadal
Author enumerates etiquettes of mutual respect between disputants

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the etiquettes of mutual respect between disputants in a jadal exchange, especially the prohibition of belittling the opponent.

Core Idea
In jadal, the opponent is a partner, not an enemy.

This passage from a work on jadal (dialectic) lays out the foundational etiquette for the relationship between disputants. It emphasizes that jadal is a cooperative search for truth, not a contest of wits. The most critical rule is mutual respect: you must not belittle your opponent. This lesson will walk you through the key principles and their rationale.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the cooperative nature of jadal
Note the author's insistence that jadal is 'scientific cooperation and joint scientific research' aimed at reaching truth. This frames the relationship as collaborative, not adversarial.
The term 'jadal' here is explicitly truth-seeking, not victory-oriented.
2
Identify the prohibition of belittling
Find where the author and the quoted scholars forbid 'istihqar' (belittling) and 'istikhfaf' (treating lightly). Al-Juwayni says 'Beware of belittling the one you debate.'
Belittling includes mocking, interrupting, shouting, or any action that demeans the opponent.
3
Analyze the consequences of belittling
According to al-Juwayni, belittling disrupts the disputant's own mental clarity: 'your mind will not be collected, your intellect will not be clear, and your heart will not be strong.' The author adds that it weakens one's critical stance.
Belittling harms the belittler more than the opponent.
4
Apply the etiquettes to a hypothetical debate
Consider a debate between a Hanafi and a Shafi'i on wiping over socks. How would these etiquettes guide the exchange? For instance, they must not interrupt, must listen attentively, and must not belittle the other's evidence.
Practise identifying violations in sample scenarios.
