المبحث الثاني
الآداب الخاصة بالعلاقة بين المتجادلين
إن الجدل العلمي كما يفترض توفر جملة من المواصفات الأدبية يتحلى بها الباحث المجادل في شخصه ويلزم بها نفسه، يتطلب كذلك توفر جملة من المواصفات الأدبية الأخرى التي تضبط علاقته بغيره ممن يمكن أن يدير معه عملية الجدل، ذلك أن هذه العلاقة هي علاقة التعاون العلمي والبحث العلمي المشترك المبتغى من ورائه الوصول إلى الحقيقة في المسألة موضع الجدل، ولذلك فإن العملية الجدلية لا يمكنها أن تفضي إلى تحقيق أهدافها وتسير سيرًا سليما في أطوارها إلا إذا توفرت فيها جملة من الأخلاقيات المتبادلة بين طرفي الجدل،
وهي :
1 - احترام بعضهما البعض وعدم استصغار أحدهما للآخر :
الجدل العلمي – كما سبق أن أكدنا - هو الجدل الذي يتوخى منه الوصول إلى الحق في المسألة موضع الجدل، ولذلك فإن كلا المتجادلين لا تربطه بالآخر سوى علاقة البحث المشترك في المسألة، وليس بينهما في حقيقة الأمر بغضاء أو شحناء قد تدفع بأحدهما إلى التعدي على حرمة الآخر أو مقامه، مما يتطلب أن يجري الجدل بينهما على أساس متين من الاحترام المتبادل، وهذا ما أكده الإمام ابن عقيل حين قال :
اعلم أنه يجب لكل واحد منهما على صاحبه مثل الذي يجب للآخر عليه؛ من الإجمال في خطابه، وترك التقطيع لكلامه والإقبال عليه، وترك الصياح في وجهه والتأمل لما يأتي به، والتجنب للحدة والضجر عليه، وترك الحمل له على جحد الضرورة إلا من حيث يلزمه ذلك بمذهبه، وترك الإخراج له عن الحد الذي ينبغي أن يكون عليه في السؤال أو الجواب، وترك الاستصغار له والاحتقار لما يأتي به إلا من حيث تلزمه الحجة إياه والتنبيه له على ذلك إن بدر منه، أو مناقضته إن ظهرت في كلامه، وأن لا يمانعه العبارة إذا أدت المعنى وكان الغرض إنما هو في المعنى دون العبارة، وأن لا يخرج في عبارته عن العادة، وأن لا يدخل في كلامه ما ليس منه ولا يستعمل ما يقتضي التعدي على خصمه والتعدي : خروجه عما يقتضيه السؤال والجواب ولا يمنعه البناء على أمثلة، ولا يشنع ما ليس بشنيع في مذهبه أو يعود عليه من الشناعة مثله، ولا يأخذ عليه شرف المجلس للاستظهار عليه ولا يستعمل الإيهام بما يخرج عن حد الكلام(1) وكل هذا يتطلب من المجادل أن لا يستحقر خصمه ولا يستهين بجانبه، بل يعامله معاملة الند للند، دون أدنى شعور بهوانه أو ضعفه .
قال الجويني : إياك واستصغار من تناظره والاستهزاء به - كائنا من كان - لأن خصمك ، إن كان من المفترض عليك في الدين، مناظرته فهو نظيرك، ولا يجمل بك إلا مناظرة النظير للنظير، وإن يَكُ من تكلمه غير أهل لأن تناظره، كان الواجب ألا تفاتحه بالكلام ، فإذا فاتحته ثم استصغرته واستخففت به لم يجتمع ذهنك ولا صفاء قريحتك، ولا اشتد خاطرك (2) .
وقال الخطيب البغدادي : «لا يستحقر خصمه لصغره فيسامحه في نظره، بل يكون على نهج واحد في الاستيفاء والاستقصاء) (3) .
إن استحقار الخصم المخالف والاستهانة به من شأنها أن تضعف الموقف النقدي للمجادل وتجعله لا يتهيأ فكريًا لمناقشة مخالفه وهذا ما قد يُمَكِّن مخالفه من التقوي عليه وإظهار ضعفه وعدم تمكنه .
ولذلك حذر إمام الحرمين الجويني كل مجادل من الاستهانة بمخالفه، فقال: لا يستحقر أحدهما صاحبه بما يقع له من الخطأ في مذهب أو دلالة أو غير ذلك، فإنه إذا اغتر بخطئه ربما أصاب فيما لا خروج له عنه واستحقار الخصم كاستحقار يسير من النار، فإنه ينتشر من يسيرها ما يحترق به كثير من الدنيا»(4)
The Second Topic: The Specific Etiquettes of the Relationship Between the Two Disputants. Scientific jadal, just as it presupposes a set of literary qualities that the disputant researcher adorns in his person and obliges himself to, also requires a set of other literary qualities that govern his relationship with others with whom he may conduct the process of jadal. This is because this relationship is a relationship of scientific cooperation and joint scientific research, whose aim is to reach the truth in the matter under dispute. Therefore, the jadal process cannot lead to achieving its goals and proceed soundly in its stages unless a set of mutual ethics between the two disputants is present. They are: 1. Mutual respect and not belittling one another: Scientific jadal – as we have emphasized – is the jadal that seeks to reach the truth in the matter under dispute. Therefore, each of the two disputants has no connection with the other other than the relationship of joint research on the matter. In reality, there is no hatred or enmity between them that might drive one to transgress against the sanctity or status of the other. This requires that the jadal proceed between them on a solid foundation of mutual respect. This is what Imam Ibn 'Aqil affirmed when he said: 'Know that each one of them owes to his companion the same as the other owes him: conciseness in speech, not interrupting his speech and paying attention to him, refraining from shouting in his face, contemplating what he brings, avoiding harshness and annoyance towards him, not forcing him to deny a necessity except where his school of thought requires it, not taking him out of the proper limit in questioning or answering, not belittling him or despising what he brings except where the proof obliges him to do so and drawing his attention to that if it occurs from him, or pointing out a contradiction if it appears in his speech, and that he should not obstruct his expression if it conveys the meaning since the purpose is only the meaning and not the expression, and that he should not depart in his expression from custom, and that he should not insert into his speech what is not part of it nor use what requires transgression against his opponent, and transgression means going beyond what is required in questioning and answering, and he should not prevent building on examples, nor exaggerate what is not exaggerated in his school of thought or return upon him with similar exaggeration, nor take advantage of the honor of the session to gain an upper hand, nor use obscurity that goes beyond the bounds of speech.' (1) All of this requires the disputant to not belittle his opponent nor underestimate his status, but rather treat him as an equal, without any feeling of his insignificance or weakness. Al-Juwayni said: 'Beware of belittling the one you debate and mocking him – whoever he may be – because your opponent, if he is one whom you are obliged to debate in religion, is your peer, and it is not fitting for you except to debate peer to peer. If the one you speak to is not worthy of debating you, then you should not engage him in speech. If you do engage him and then belittle him and treat him lightly, your mind will not be collected, your intellect will not be clear, and your heart will not be strong.' (2) Al-Khatib al-Baghdadi said: 'He should not belittle his opponent due to his youth so as to be lenient in his consideration, but rather be consistent in thoroughness and comprehensiveness.' (3) Indeed, belittling and underestimating the opposing opponent weakens the critical stance of the disputant and makes him not intellectually prepared to discuss his opponent. This may enable his opponent to gain power over him and show his weakness and lack of competence. Therefore, Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni warned every disputant against underestimating his opponent, saying: 'One should not belittle the other due to an error he makes in a school of thought or evidence or otherwise. For if he is deluded by his error, he may hit upon something from which there is no escape. And belittling the opponent is like belittling a small ember of fire, for from its smallness spreads what burns a great portion of the world.' (4)
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the etiquette (adab) required in the relationship between two disputants in scholarly jadal (dialectic). It is not a definition of jadal itself, but a prescription for proper conduct to ensure the process remains cooperative and truth-seeking. The author begins by emphasizing that scientific jadal is a collaborative endeavor aimed at reaching the truth, and therefore requires a set of mutual ethical standards. The first and most important is mutual respect and the prohibition of belittling one's opponent. The author then quotes several classical scholars to reinforce this. Ibn 'Aqil provides a comprehensive list of obligations each disputant owes the other: concise speech, not interrupting, refraining from shouting, attentively listening, avoiding harshness, not forcing the opponent to deny a necessity unless his own school requires it, keeping within proper bounds in questions and answers, not belittling or despising the opponent's arguments unless the proof demands correction, pointing out contradictions politely, not obstructing expression if the meaning is clear, avoiding unusual expressions, not inserting irrelevant speech, not transgressing by going beyond the proper scope of question and answer, not preventing building on examples, not exaggerating faults, not using the honor of the session for advantage, and avoiding obscurity. The common thread is treating the opponent as an equal. Al-Juwayni warns that belittling the opponent disrupts one's own mental clarity and strength. Al-Khatib al-Baghdadi stresses consistency in thoroughness regardless of the opponent's age or status. The author then explains that belittling weakens one's critical position and may allow the opponent to gain the upper hand. Al-Juwayni further warns that belittling is dangerous because even a small fire can spread and cause great destruction. The passage thus functions as a guide to maintaining a disciplined, respectful, and productive dialectical exchange.

استحقار
خصم

The Etiquette of Debate: Respecting the Opponent
Author compiles admonitions against belittling opponents in jadal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key admonitions against belittling an opponent in jadal and apply the principle of respectful dialogue.

Core Idea
Respect your opponent in debate; belittling destroys your own argument.

Islamic dialectical tradition (jadal) is not only about logic but also about adab. This lesson compiles warnings from leading scholars against belittling the opponent, showing that respect is integral to effective argumentation. Passages from al-Juwayni, al-Khatib al-Baghdadi, al-Zarkashi, al-Tufi, and Ibn Aqil illustrate the ethical framework.

Guided Path
1
Identify prohibited behaviors
List the opening prohibitions: inserting extraneous speech, transgression, denouncing the innocent, using obfuscation, and taking advantage of status.
2
Analyze al-Juwayni's warning
Extract his statement that belittling destroys mental focus, natural disposition, and resolve.
3
Interpret the fire metaphor
Al-Juwayni compares belittling to a small fire that spreads and burns much of the world, showing its potential harm.
4
Apply al-Tufi's positive prescription
Note his advice to soften speech, accept the opponent's points, and offer counterarguments gently.
5
Understand Ibn Aqil's virtue of patience
See how forbearance and patience with the opponent's irritations elevate the disputant among scholars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يدخل في كلامه ما ليس منه ولا يستعمل ما يقتضي التعدي على خصمه والتعدي : خروجه عما يقتضيه السؤال والجواب ولا يمنعه البناء على أمثلة، ولا يشنع ما ليس بشنيع في مذهبه أو يعود عليه من الشناعة مثله، ولا يأخذ عليه شرف المجلس للاستظهار عليه ولا يستعمل الإيهام بما يخرج عن حد الكلام»(1) وكل هذا يتطلب من المجادل أن لا يستحقر خصمه ولا يستهين بجانبه، بل يعامله معاملة الند للند، دون أدنى شعور بهوانه أو ضعفه .
.
قال الجويني : إياك واستصغار من تناظره والاستهزاء به - کائنا من كان - لأن خصمك ، إن كان من المفترض عليك في الدين ،مناظرته فهو نظيرك، ولا يجمل بك إلا مناظرة النظير للنظير، وإن يَكُ من تكلمه غير أهل لأن تناظره، كان الواجب ألا تفتتحه بالكلام ، فإذا فاتحته ثم استصغرته واستخففت به لم يجتمع ذهنك ولا صفاء قريحتك، ولا اشتد خاطرك (2) .
وقال الخطيب البغدادي : «لا يستحقر خصمه لصغره فيسامحه في نظره، بل يكون على نهج واحد في الاستيفاء والاستقصاء) (3) .
إن استحقار الخصم المخالف والاستهانة به من شأنها أن تضعف الموقف النقدي للمجادل وتجعله لا يتهيأ فكريًا لمناقشة مخالفه وهذا ما قد يُمَكِّن مخالفه من التقوي عليه وإظهار ضعفه وعدم تمكنه .
ولذلك حذر إمام الحرمين الجويني كل مجادل من الاستهانة بمخالفه، فقال: لا يستحقر أحدهما صاحبه بما يقع له من الخطأ في مذهب أو دلالة أو غير ذلك، فإنه إذا اغتر بخطئه ربما أصاب فيما لا خروج له عنه واستحقار الخصم كاستحقار يسير من النار، فإنه ينتشر من يسيرها ما يحرق به كثير من الدنيا»(4).
وهو ذاته التحذير الذي صدر عن الإمام الزركشي حين قال : إياك أن تستصغر خصمًا، فإن استصغرته فالوجه ألا تكلمه، فلربما هجم من استصغاره الانقطاع لقلة التحفظ منه والاهتمام به» (1) . فالعلاقة التي ينبغي أن تحكم الحوار بين المتجادلين هي علاقة الأدب الإسلامي المتكامل، الذي يقتضي أن يسود الاحترام والتسامح والتجاوز عن الهفوات غير المضرة بالحق .
قال الطوفي: «وليلن كل منهما لخصمه الكلام ولا يغلظ عليه، وليتلق ما يصدر عنه بقبول و لطف ،وتحسين مثل أن يقول : ما ذكرته حسن متجه، لكن يرد عليه كذا أو يعارضه كذا (2) .
ويحلم ويتطلب هذا؛ أنه إذا أخطأ أحدهما في حق الآخر، وجب عليه أن يصبر عليه معه ولا يعامله بالمثل، ويتحرى البعد عن المشاغبات والمماحكات التي لا تنفع سير عملية الجدل ولا تضيف إلى البحث العلمي أي نتيجة علمية جديدة، قال ابن عقيل: من كان الصبر على شعب السائل في الجدل فضيلة، والحلم عن بادرة إن كانت منه ،رفعة فينبغي لمن أحب اكتساب الفضائل أن يستعمل ذلك بحسب علمه بما له فيه من الحفظ الجزيل والمحل الجليل وليس ينقصه الحلم إلا عند جاهل ولا يضع منه الصبر على شنب السائل إلا عند غبي يعتقد أن ذلك الذل والركاكة وانخساس النفس وقصور اللسان في الشغب هو الفضل، فإن من خاض فيه تعوده، ومن تعوده حرم الإصابة واستروح إليه ومن عرف بذلك سقط سقوط الذرة، ومن صبر على ذلك وحلم عنه ارتفع في نفوس العلماء، ونبل عند أهل الجدل، وبانت منه القوة على نفسه حيث منعها المقابلة على الجفاء بمثله، والقوة على خصمه حيث أحوجه إلى الشغب، لا سيما إذا ظهر منه أنه فعل ذلك حرصا على الإرشاد إلى الحق ومحبة الاستنقاذ من الباطل الذي أثارته الشبهة من
He inserts into his speech what is not from it, and does not use what necessitates transgression against his opponent. Transgression: his departure from what the question and answer require, and he does not prevent the building upon examples, nor does he denounce what is not denounceable in his school of thought, or something similar to it returns upon him from denouncement. He does not hold against him the honor of the assembly to gain advantage over him, nor does he use obfuscation that goes beyond the bounds of speech. All of this requires the disputant not to belittle his opponent nor to underestimate his standing, but rather to treat him as an equal, without any feeling of his insignificance or weakness.

Al-Juwayni said: Beware of belittling the one you debate and mocking him – whoever he may be – because your opponent, if he is one whom it is obligatory upon you in religion to debate, then he is your peer, and it is not becoming for you except to debate peer with peer. If the one you speak with is not worthy of your debating him, then the obligation is that you not initiate speech with him. If you do initiate and then belittle him and treat him lightly, your mind will not gather, nor your natural disposition become clear, nor your resolve strengthen.

Al-Khatib al-Baghdadi said: He should not belittle his opponent due to his smallness, thereby being lenient in his consideration, but rather he should be on a single path in thoroughness and exhaustiveness.

Indeed, belittling the opposing opponent and underestimating him tends to weaken the critical stance of the disputant and make him not intellectually prepared to discuss his opponent. This may enable his opponent to overpower him and show his weakness and lack of ability.

Therefore, Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni warned every disputant against underestimating his opponent, saying: Let neither of them belittle his companion due to the error that occurs from him in doctrine or evidence or otherwise, for if he is deceived by his error, perhaps he will hit upon something from which he cannot escape. Belittling the opponent is like belittling a small amount of fire, for from its small amount spreads that which burns much of the world.

This is the same warning issued by Imam al-Zarkashi when he said: Beware of belittling an opponent, for if you belittle him, the proper course is not to speak to him; perhaps from his belittlement comes defeat due to lack of caution and concern for him.

So the relationship that should govern dialogue between the two disputants is a relationship of complete Islamic etiquette, which demands that respect, tolerance, and overlooking slips that do not harm the truth prevail.

Al-Tufi said: Let each of them soften speech to his opponent and not be harsh to him, and let him receive what comes from him with acceptance and gentleness, and improvement by saying: What you mentioned is good and has a direction, but there is such-and-such objection or such-and-such counterargument.

And he should forbear. This requires that if one of them errs against the other, it is obligatory upon him to be patient with him and not to treat him in kind, and to strive to stay away from squabbles and bickering that do not benefit the process of debate nor add any new scientific result to the research. Ibn Aqil said: Whoever – patience with the comb of the questioner in debate is a virtue, and forbearance over an emotional outburst if it comes from him is an elevation; then it is befitting for one who loves to acquire virtues to employ that according to his knowledge of what he has of abundant preservation and lofty rank. Forbearance diminishes him only before an ignorant person, and patience with the comb of the questioner reduces him only before a dullard who believes that abasement, weakness, lowliness of soul, and shortness of tongue in wrangling are virtue. For whoever plunges into it becomes accustomed to it; whoever becomes accustomed to it is deprived of correctness and becomes at ease with it; whoever is known for that falls like a particle; whoever is patient with it and forbears rises in the eyes of the scholars and becomes noble among the people of debate, and his strength over himself is apparent, as he prevents himself from reciprocating harshness with its like, and his strength over his opponent is apparent, as he forces him to wrangling, especially if it appears that he did that out of eagerness to guide to the truth and love of rescuing from falsehood that doubt has stirred.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage is a compilation of admonitions on the etiquette of dialectical debate (jadal). It opens by listing prohibited behaviours: introducing extraneous speech, transgressing the bounds of question and answer, denouncing what is not denounceable, taking advantage of status, and using obfuscation. The core requirement is that the disputant must not belittle or underestimate his opponent, but treat him as an equal. Al-Juwayni warns that belittling the opponent destroys one's mental focus and rhetorical skill. Al-Khatib al-Baghdadi stresses consistency in thoroughness regardless of the opponent's stature. The text then explains that belittling weakens the disputant's critical stance and may allow the opponent to gain an upper hand. Al-Juwayni adds a powerful metaphor: belittling is like a small fire that can spread and burn much of the world. Al-Zarkashi echoes this, noting that belittling leads to carelessness and defeat. The passage then prescribes a relationship of complete Islamic etiquette: respect, tolerance, and overlooking harmless slips. Al-Tufi advises softening speech, gentleness, and acknowledging the opponent's points before offering counterarguments. Finally, Ibn Aqil distinguishes patience and forbearance from the ignorance of those who consider them weakness. He explains that true virtue lies in controlling oneself and thereby elevating one's status among scholars, while wrangling and bickering lead to loss. The emphasis throughout is that proper adab is essential for productive dialectic aimed at seeking truth.

الشنب

Etiquette of Jadal: The Conduct of a Truthful Debater
This passage lays out the ethical framework for jadal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principles of Islamic etiquette in a jadal exchange, including avoiding belittling the opponent, speaking gently, and responding with patience and forbearance.

Core Idea
Jadal is not combat; it is cooperation clothed in courtesy.

The passage opens with a stern warning from Imam al-Zarkashi: never belittle your opponent, for that leads to defeat. The relationship must be governed by complete Islamic etiquette—respect, tolerance, and overlooking minor slips. This lesson unpacks the specific etiquettes prescribed by al-Tufi and Ibn 'Aqil for conducting debate as a virtuous, truth-seeking encounter.

Guided Path
1
Identify the warning
Notice al-Zarkashi's prohibition: 'Beware of belittling an opponent.' Why does belittling lead to defeat? Because it results in lack of caution and concern, potentially causing a sudden loss.
The warning frames the entire ethos of jadal: the opponent is not an enemy to be crushed but a partner in search of truth.
2
Extract the positive etiquette
From al-Tufi, extract the prescribed behaviors: soften speech, accept with gentleness, respond by acknowledging the valid aspect of the opponent's point before stating a refutation.
This mirrors the adab of al-Imam al-Shafi'i who would say, 'You are correct in this aspect, but...'
3
Reflect on the virtue of patience
Ibn 'Aqil contrasts patience and forbearance with acrimony and wrangling. Understand why forbearance is a sign of strength over oneself and the opponent, and why it raises one's station among scholars.
Patience here is not weakness; it is a strategic virtue that compels the opponent to resort to wrangling, thus exposing their lack of adab.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو ذاته التحذير الذي صدر عن الإمام الزركشي حين قال : إياك أن تستصغر خصمًا، فإن استصغرته فالوجه ألا تكلمه، فلربما هجم من استصغاره الانقطاع لقلة التحفظ منه والاهتمام به. فالعلاقة التي ينبغي أن تحكم الحوار بين المتجادلين هي علاقة الأدب الإسلامي المتكامل، الذي يقتضي أن يسود الاحترام والتسامح والتجاوز عن الهفوات غير المضرة بالحق. قال الطوفي: «وليلن كل منهما لخصمه الكلام ولا يغلظ عليه، وليتلق ما يصدر عنه بقبول و لطف ،وتحسين مثل أن يقول : ما ذكرته حسن متجه، لكن يرد عليه كذا أو يعارضه كذا. ويحلم ويتطلب هذا ؛ أنه إذا أخطأ أحدهما في حق الأخر، وجب عليه أن يصبر عليه معه ولا يعامله بالمثل، ويتحرى البعد عن المشاغبات والمماحكات التي لا تنفع سير عملية الجدل ولا تضيف إلى البحث العلمي أي نتيجة علمية جديدة، قال ابن عقيل: من إذا كان الصبر على شعب السائل في الجدل فضيلة، والحلم عن بادرة إن كانت منه ،رفعة فينبغي لمن أحب اكتساب الفضائل أن يستعمل ذلك بحسب علمه بما له فيه من الحفظ الجزيل والمحل الجليل وليس ينقصه الحلم إلا عند جاهل ولا يضع منه الصبر على شنب السائل إلا عند غبي يعتقد أن ذلك الذل والركاكة وانخساس النفس وقصور اللسان في الشغب هو الفضل، فإن من خاض فيه تعوده، ومن تعوده حرم الإصابة واستروح إليه ومن عرف بذلك سقط سقوط الذرة، ومن صبر على ذلك وحلم عنه ارتفع في نفوس العلماء، ونبل عند أهل الجدل، وبانت منه القوة على نفسه حيث منعها المقابلة على الجفاء بمثله، والقوة على خصمه حيث أحوجه إلى الشغب، لا سيما إذا ظهر منه أنه فعل ذلك حرصا على الإرشاد إلى الحق ومحبة الاستنقاذ من الباطل الذي أثارته الشبهة من الضلال المؤدي بصاحبه إلى العطب والهلاك فله بهذه النية الجميلة الثواب من ربه والمدحة من كل منصف حضره أو سمع به 2 - الرغبة المشتركة في تبادل الرأي والتعاون لإظهار الحق: لا يمكن أن تؤدي العملية الجدلية مهمتها أو تحقق هدفها إلا إذا كان الدافع إليها هو طلب الحق والرغبة في الوصول إليه، وهذا الدافع ينبغي أن يتوفر لدى طرفي الجدل في آن واحد بحيث ينطلق كل منهما من شعور بالتعاون مع الآخر لأجل الوصول إلى الحق في المسألة محل النزاع والجدل فلا بد من وجود رغبة لدى المتحاورين في تبادل الآراء من أجل الاقتناع والإقناع ولا توجد هذه الرغبة إلا عند وجود الحافز إليها، الذي لا بد أن يكون حافزا مطلقا يدفع إلى طلب المعرفة من أجل المعرفة. فإن «هدف المناظرة هو في الأصل تعاون الفريقين المتناظرين على معرفة الحقيقة والتوصل إليها بتبصير كل منهما صاحبه بالأماكن المظلمة عليه، والتي خفيت عنه حينما أخذ ينظر باحثًا عن الحقيقة، وذلك حينما لا يكون أحدهما واقفًا على الحقيقة المبينة وقوفًا قطعيًا غير قابل للنقض، أما في هذه الحالة فإن هدف المناظرة إنما هو تبصير الواقف على الحقيقة أخاه المناظر له بها، والأخذ بيده في طرق الاستدلال الصحيح لإبلاغه وجه الحق المشرق، وذلك باستخدام الحوار البريء من التعصب الخالي من العنف والانفعال المتمشي وفق الأصول العامة للحوار الذي يهدف فيه كل من الفريقين المتحاورين أن يصل إلى الحقيقة، كأنه جاهل بها» قال الغزالي رحمه الله : الجدال معاونة على النظر، ومصاولة بأسلحة الخواطر والفكر
It is the same warning issued by Imam al-Zarkashi when he said: Beware of belittling an opponent, for if you belittle him, the proper course is not to speak to him, because perhaps from belittling him comes defeat due to lack of caution and concern for him. The relationship that should govern the dialogue between the disputants is that of complete Islamic etiquette, which requires that respect, tolerance, and overlooking of slips that do not harm the truth prevail. Al-Tufi said: 'Let each of them soften his speech to his opponent and not be harsh with him, and let him receive what comes from him with acceptance and gentleness, and improvement, such as saying: What you mentioned is good and valid, but it is refuted by such-and-such or opposed by such-and-such.' And let him forbear. And this requires that if one of them errs against the other's right, he must be patient with him and not retaliate in kind, and avoid quarrels and squabbles that do not benefit the conduct of the debate and do not add any new scientific result to academic research. Ibn 'Aqil said: 'If patience with the opponent's acrimony in debate is a virtue, and forbearance if an outburst comes from him is an elevation, then whoever loves to acquire virtues should practice that according to his knowledge of the great reward and sublime station he has in it. Forbearance only diminishes him in the eyes of an ignorant person, and patience with the opponent's acrimony only lowers him in the eyes of a stupid person who thinks that such humility, weakness, lowness of soul, and shortcoming of tongue in wrangling is merit. He who engages in it becomes accustomed to it, and he who becomes accustomed to it is deprived of correctness and delights in it, and he who is known for that falls like a mote, and he who practices patience and forbearance rises in the eyes of scholars and is noble among the people of debate. His strength over his soul becomes apparent as he prevents it from responding to harshness with its like, and his strength over his opponent becomes apparent as he compels him to resort to wrangling, especially if it appears that he did that out of eagerness to guide to the truth and love of rescuing from falsehood that doubt has stirred from error leading its possessor to ruin and destruction. So by this beautiful intention he has reward from his Lord and praise from every just person who attended or heard of him.' 2 - The shared desire for exchange of opinion and cooperation to manifest the truth: The dialectical process cannot fulfill its task or achieve its goal unless the motive for it is the pursuit of truth and the desire to attain it. This motive should be present in both parties of the debate simultaneously, so that each proceeds from a feeling of cooperation with the other in order to reach the truth in the matter under dispute and debate. Therefore, there must be a desire among the interlocutors to exchange views for the sake of conviction and persuasion. This desire only exists when there is an incentive for it, which must be an absolute incentive that drives the seeking of knowledge for its own sake. Indeed, 'the aim of munazara is originally the cooperation of the two debating parties to know the truth and arrive at it by each making his companion see the dark spots that were hidden from him when he began to look searching for the truth, when neither of them has definitive, irrefutable knowledge of the apparent truth. In this case, the aim of munazara is to make the one who knows the truth enlighten his debating brother about it, and to take him by the hand in the paths of correct inference to convey to him the bright face of truth, using dialogue free from fanaticism, devoid of violence and emotion, proceeding according to the general principles of dialogue in which each of the two dialoguing parties aims to reach the truth as if he were ignorant of it.' Al-Ghazali, may Allah have mercy on him, said: 'Dialectical debate (jadal) is a cooperation in investigation and a fencing with the weapons of thoughts and intellect.'
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage is an exposition on the ethics and purpose of dialectical debate (jadal) from an Islamic perspective. It begins by quoting Imam al-Zarkashi's warning against belittling an opponent, because underestimating them can lead to defeat due to negligence. The relationship between disputants should be governed by complete Islamic etiquette, emphasizing respect, tolerance, and overlooking minor mistakes. The passage then cites al-Tufi, advising that each debater should speak gently, accept the opponent's words with kindness, and respond gracefully, e.g., praising the valid aspect while pointing out what refutes or opposes it. The debater should be forbearing. Then Ibn 'Aqil elaborates: patience with the opponent's acrimony is a virtue, and forbearance raises one's station. Forbearance only lowers one in the eyes of the ignorant; patience only seems like weakness to the foolish. Those who engage in harsh wrangling become accustomed to it, lose correctness, and fall. Those who are patient and forbearing rise in the eyes of scholars and are noble among the people of debate. They demonstrate strength over themselves and over their opponent, especially when their motive is sincere desire to guide to truth and rescue from falsehood. Such a person attains reward and praise. The second section (numbered 2) discusses the shared desire for exchange of opinion and cooperation to manifest truth. The dialectical process cannot achieve its goal unless the motive is the pursuit of truth, and this motive must be shared by both parties. Each must feel they are cooperating to reach the truth. The incentive must be absolute — seeking knowledge for its own sake. The passage then quotes a definition of munazara: it is originally a cooperation to know the truth, with each helping the other see what is hidden. If one party already has definitive knowledge, the goal is still to enlighten the other, using dialogue free from fanaticism and emotion. Both act as if ignorant, seeking truth together. Finally, al-Ghazali is quoted: 'Dialectical debate is a cooperation in investigation and a fencing with the weapons of thoughts and intellect.' The entire passage emphasizes that jadal is not about winning but about mutual discovery of truth, requiring humility, patience, and sincere cooperation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary purpose of munazara (formal debate)?
A) To defeat the opponent and display superiority
B) To cooperate in reaching the truth and making each other see hidden aspects
C) To practice rhetorical skill and win public approval
D) To force one's own opinion on the other

Key Takeaway
Jadal is a cooperative search for truth governed by Islamic etiquette—patience, respect, and mutual upliftment—not a contest to be won.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In a debate about whether the Quran is created or uncreated, write a response to an opponent's argument using al-Tufi's recommended phrasing: acknowledge the valid aspect first, then state the objection.
?
Identify a situation in a historical kalam debate (e.g., between Ash'aris and Mu'tazila) where one party belittled the opponent. Explain how that violated the etiquette of jadal as outlined by Ibn 'Aqil.
